---
title: "Why is Linux less popular than OS X?"
date: 2011-07-06
forum: Recurring Discussions
---

### Post by fontis on 2011-07-06
I was reading the entries on the other thread here in the cafe regarding Linux ads and what not. I came to ponder upon this question and thought I'd start off a topic about it.

So why is it that OS X is so much more popular and usable than Linux distros are?
They both share so many similarities, but in reality when it comes to usage OS X seems light-years ahead of Linux. What gives?

If you take the advertising and the "cult" surrounding OS X away, it feels like Apple has managed to polish their OS in a much better way than the Linux ecosystem has. Stability aside, compatibility seems to no longer really be an issue when using OS X. But then again, I don't own OS X so I can't comment on that really. 

What do you guys think? What is it that we lack, that others seem to have figured out?

---

### Post by Ctrl-Alt-F1 on 2011-07-06
I think there is value in having locked down hardware.  Although  you can run MacOS X on a range of different hardware, not near as much needs to be supported as in Linux.  Linux doesn't have as much support from hardware vendors as either Linux or Windows, so a lot of what we use is hacked together freeware drivers etc.

---

### Post by handy on 2011-07-06
Apple basically bought the GUI to the masses. Apple's GUI was what Bill Gates created Windows to compete with. Apple were who MS created their first application for - a spreadsheet.

Apple have had quite a head start on Linux, which was not built for the desktop but is a modified server platform.

Beyond that, Apple are a hardware manufacturer that is worth billions of dollars, it is well known & marketing has always been something that they have been strong at, as has been creating stylish hardware & systems.

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2011-07-07
Apple has been going since 1978. They have a history of producing innovative attractive computers and couple to that an intuitive OS that is built for their hardware. With their related also innovative hardware, iPod, iPad, iPhone etc they can offer the consumer a full package that integrates well, a one stop shop.

Now looking beyond the hardware OSX is produced for a relatively small range of devices and therefore does not have to be all things to all like Windows needs to cope with, this means a very stable OS.

### Post by kvv_1986 on 2011-07-07
Easy. Linux has traditionally been a hacker's OS, and became really popular on servers since around 2000 only. Whereas Apple has been in the business since 1984 or something. Linux desktop has never been a commercial oriented desktop. Things are changing now, like an earlier poster said, with Canonical and Google investing heavily on the desktop.

### Post by PartisanEntity on 2011-07-07
Centralization plays a big role too. OSX is developed by 1 company, so all developers work towards a unified strategy. Many of the applications in OSX tie in to each other because of this. As mentioned previously, marketing also plays a big role. 

The world of Linux based operating systems is decentralized. This has advantages and disadvantages. One of the disadvantages is that people have different goals and resources cannot be pooled. So it is not possible to compete with Apple on this front. 

For the world of Linux based operating systems to compete you would need 1 group to break off from the rest and work on unified ecosystem where apps tie in to each other. This would be a complete break from the tradition and ideals in the community, but it's the only way can consider competing. 

It's all about unity :-)

### Post by 8_Bit on 2011-07-07
It's pretty easy to answer this question...

Apple has always gone out of their way to create the most **[SIZE=3]user friendly interface[/SIZE]**. Linux developers have instead prioritized functionality over usability. They've largely ignored the design factor. (Something Mark Shuttleworth has pointed out repeatedly, and one thing he wishes to change.)

Take GIMP as an example. Or even Blender pre-2.5x. Both have (or had) ugly as  heck interfaces that scare many people away. The developers of these  have made products that work, sure, but are they user-friendly? Not  really. Apple makes sure that OSX and its applications have both  qualities: functionality, and good looks.

Couple this with the fact that most Linux developers have a high-and-mighty attitude towards non-techies. You guys ever try asking questions on the forums for other distros? Ubuntu Forums is a haven compared to them. You'll sometimes get laughed off and get sarcastic remarks if you ask any noobish question on the forum for the other major distros. I've seen this quite a bit on the Debian forum. Apple, on the other hand, puts noobs as their priority.

The good thing is that Canonical has changed all of this. They're not ignoring the design factor, AND they've created a community where people regularly help each other. And even if you don't like Unity, you are benefitting from this. Their initiative has pushed others in the FOSS community to follow suit. Example: It took Canonical's creation of Unity for the GNOME people to finally stop dilly-dallying around and complete GNOME Shell.

---

> **nec207 said:**
> 
More hardware supports windows than linux and OS X.

Though Linux supplies more drivers than any other system.

> **nec207 said:**
> 
Has for OS X it built for a novice to use thus user friendly. 

I think a better way to say that, would be to say that OSX, has been designed to be easy to use, so that people don't have to spend as much otherwise useful time learning how to use the system. (Which suits me, as I don't have to maintain my wife's computer since she changed from XP to an iMac with OSX. She does just about everything with no input from me. :))

> **nec207 said:**
> 
In windows most of stuff is locked away and lack logic where OS X GUI was built from ground up. 

I think that since Win95, the design of the Windows systems have been made particularly complex (the registry & the splitting up of installed application components into various system directories apart from their home directory) in an effort to thwart software pirates. It is the only reason I can come up with for why they would have made it that way.  

> **nec207 said:**
> 
The problem is Microsoft is for businesses and OS X is for collage and university students the GUI has a artsy look and I hate to say this but a girly splash that businesses frown on. 

After Steve Jobs head hunted Alan Kay (& some others) from Xerox PARC, due to his seeing Kay's Smalltalk GUI system & knowing that this is what the people of the world must have. They created the first Mac's with the monochrome GUI desktop interface.

In their time the first Mac's were wonderful, & they were expensive. The problem that Apple now had was how do we get people to buy them?

He gained the leverage by teaming up with the guys that had just created Adobe, as they had invented postscript. Jobs convinced them to adapt postscript to laser printers, this led to Apple shipping the first postscript printer, the LaserWriter in 1985, this started the desktop publishing revolution & explains why for so many years it would always be Mac's you would see where graphic design & layout, CAD, anything to do with still & moving photography. 

The Mac's started it all with Adobe. 

> **nec207 said:**
> 
Has businesses like every thing plain looking. 
 
It depends on the business. Certainly number crunchers more so than graphics editors.

> **nec207 said:**

Also the itunes and final cut pro is is the golden standard of applications. 
 
The young people are where your future customers/money await. The iPod was one of the things that saved Apple. With it they have just gone on from strength to strength in a business sense.

> **nec207 said:**
> 
The problem with Microsoft is they build OS for older people and businesses .Where apple builds OS and applications for the collage and university students and multimedia buff. 
 
I don't think that MS are as creative as Apple when it comes to their products. They also have a dreadful OS design which places limits of its own on just what they can do. Because MS are primarily a software house, a closed-source software house, they believe that they have to protect their intellectual property. This I think has had a huge bearing on how ugly & inelegant their systems are.

It is really easy to forget that Apple & MS are two completely different types of companies: Apple are a hardware manufacturer that also creates its own OS's (that aren't license verified) where needed & MS are a software company (with rigorous copy protection) that also creates some hardware products in the interest of greater profit.

> **nec207 said:**
> 
And because legally OS X and apple applications can only work on Mac computers the hardware is controlled thus it does not crash and no BSOD .Where windows and windows programs have to run on so many hardware it will crash more and more BSOD .

The command line is just a different kind of experience that is great once you get used to it. Though I agree, it is the last thing that most people want to use at it throws their ignorance in that area straight up in their face.

### Post by Madspyman on 2011-07-07
Linux isn't as popular because there are basically 2 options known to consumers PC (Windows) or Mac (OS X). 

Macs are more expensive. You need a good reason to buy one, just surfing the internet and writing word documents isn't a good reason to spend a fortune. Yet so many people buy Macs just for this reason.   

Others will spend less on a PC with Windows, bloat, viruses, virus scan software rackets, and intrusive Microsoft Live whatever, until they get pissed off enough to drop the dough on a Mac, seemingly the only other option. There friends who are tech savvy enough to make Windows work (but not use Linux) are like why did you get that Mac Windows works fine.

Enter the standard Mac purchase justification "It just works" and that's great argument that on the surface seems very true, however ever been to the Mac support forums just for kicks?

There is no such thing as a Computer that just works, but don't ever expect Apple or a Mac user to tell you that.  

Simple products with high price tags, good word of mouth, and clever marketing are the ones that become the most popular.

Linux is a free, has less marketing, and not as good word of mouth among the those who aren't particularly tech savvy. People unfamiliar with modern Linux still think Linux = Headache. 

It's getting better when you factor in Post-PC devices built for the net (something Linux excels at) that nobody advertises uses Linux, but in the desktop/laptop realm generally the people who passionately use Linux will be the ones less likely to try to sell it to you.

### Post by Gerontion on 2011-07-07
> If you take the advertising and the "cult" surrounding OS X away...You can't. That's what Apple is and that's why its products are popular. Humans are thick - we're attracted to shiny things. Apple is shiny. Linux isn't. If you can make Linux shiny - and rather more usable than it is now, though that's a secondary issue - people will use it but, as it stands, it's about as shiny as used no-brand engine oil. To the extent that the public at large have any conception of what Linux is - and that extent is about two millimetres long - Linux is the domain of socially dysfunctional men with bad hair and worse skin.

### Post by fontis on 2011-07-07
I think some here mentioned how design is an important player here, and I too agree, Linux applications are in most cases hideous, even compared to Windows.

But still, none of you have so far mentioned the overall compatibility and seamless integration of programs in OS X which is simply lacking in Linux. Of course one must understand that the Linux ecosystem differs in the sense that there are multiple different people contributing to various programs that in the end constitute the basics of the OS, but it's still remarkable how none of them are "aligned" with each other.

Take Gnome for instance, there is SOME form of cohesiveness involved with GTK applications, but in the larger sense they are not seamlessly integrated into the DE. An example could be made with Pidgin for us, and Adium for OS X. Adium blends into OS X so perfectly, whereas Pidgin so blatantly isn't.

Why do you think developers don't prioritize the thought of having their software actually integrated into the whole "experience" of the OS? Could it be simply because each developer, selfishly (albeit unknowingly), does this as a form of "branding" to differentiate themselves? Regardless, the results remain the same, interoperability issues arise and the user experience gets ruined.

---

### Post by jhonan on 2011-07-07

Apple doesn't market OSX as a seperate entity (apart from upgrades). Apple markets well-designed appliances, which happen to be running OSX.

If you levelled the playing field; i.e. made Apple a pure OS company, who developed OSX in isolation and attempted to persuade Windows users to download the OSX ISO from a website and burn it to a CD and install it on their PC... How popular would it be then?

Snow Leopard can mount NTFS volumes as read/write, it is not activated by default 

 To get read/write support for NTFS drives in Snow Leopard: 
[LIST=1]
[*]In Terminal, type diskutil info /Volumes/volume_name, where volume_name is the name of the NTFS volume. From the output, copy the Volume UUID value to the clipboard.
[*]Back up /etc/fstab if you have it; it shouldn't be there in a default install.
[*]Type sudo nano /etc/fstab.
[*]In the editor, type UUID=, then paste the UUID number you copied from the clipboard. Type a Space, then type none ntfs rw. The final line should look like this: UUID=123-456-789 none ntfs rw, where 123-456-789 is the UUID you copied in the first step.
[*]Repeat the above steps for any other NTFS drives/partitions you have.
[*]Save the file and quit nano (Control-X, Y, Enter), then restart your system.
[/LIST]
 After rebooting, NTFS partitions should natively have read and write support.

---

[*]Back up /etc/fstab if you have it; it shouldn't be there in a default install.
[*]Type sudo nano /etc/fstab.
[*]In the editor, type UUID=, then paste the UUID number you copied from the clipboard. Type a Space, then type none ntfs rw. The final line should look like this: UUID=123-456-789 none ntfs rw, where 123-456-789 is the UUID you copied in the first step.

[*]Save the file and quit nano (Control-X, Y, Enter), then restart your system.
[/LIST]
 After rebooting, NTFS partitions should natively have read and write support.

Man, that's almost worse than setting up an NFS share in Ubuntu.

The findings were that as the Apple products appeared, the reaction in the brain was similar to that of a religious person to symbols pertaining to their chosen religion.

I was truly frightened at the power they can command over people.

And yet some people on this forum think that Linux is a cult and defend Apple at every bit of criticism. I think those people need an MRI scan on their brains for damage ;)

### Post by rg4w on 2011-07-07
> **cbanakis said:**
> "HA! OSX has the worst compatibility issues of any OS out there. It only works on one brand of Computer!"

Its even worse than that.

Snow Leopard, and whatever the heck name they have coming out next, only work on the newer Intel Macs.

So if you want Snow Leopard, you have to buy a new computer first.
Worse still, the forthcoming Lion only works on Intel Macs using Core 2 Duo and greater; the Core Duo models they sold the year before (2006) are now just part of a planned obsolescence.

That said, I enjoy both Mac and Ubuntu, for different reasons.  My work has me switching between all three (the third being Windows) throughout the day, and while I don't much care for the colossal time sink that is perpetually keeping my Windows systems updated for security, I do very much enjoy the other two.

As others have pointed out, there's really no comparing the usability of Macs with any OS, since the Mac experience is defined by an OS written specifically for an unusually narrow range of hardware components assembled by the same company that writes the OS.  Ubuntu's job is much harder, since it's asked to work on nearly every machine made in the last several years, with varying degrees of success.

If there's an opportunity for comparison of Ubuntu with another OS, it's with Windows.  And even there it's an uphill battle, since most computer manufacturers design their systems specifically for compatibility with Windows.  That Ubuntu performs as gracefully as it does on so many different systems is rather a marvel.

### Post by kaldor on 2011-07-07
Marketing + reputation. OS X itself is a great OS, but it does have problems that bother me. It fits most user's needs (think about non-nerds, please) and it "just works". I convinced a very tech-illiterate family member to get a Mac instead of a Windows PC [SIZE="1"](No, linux wasn't a good fit for their needs)[/SIZE] and it's been a breeze. She turned on her computer, and was browsing the web within 2 minutes; I won't even mention how long it took to set up a brand new Windows 7 PC. Very fast and hassle-free setup and computing for most people. People don't care about software freedom either, so that's another thing. They just want their stuff to work.

Macs are also marketed a hell of a lot more than Linux. ZaReason, System76, and others, do NOT count for the same experience as a Mac; you cannot just walk into a store and buy them like you can with an Apple product.

Ubuntu is just as easy to use and set up as a Mac. What people forget [SIZE="1"](like someone saying it's so hard to use on an earlier post)[/SIZE] is that you install Ubuntu yourself on a PC not built for Ubuntu, whereas you buy a Mac and OS X is already installed and designed to work properly without ANY tweaking. 

Overall, Macs are just very complete and usable computers with everything working fast and straight out of the box.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-07
Frankly this was a great thread until all the angst showed up.  We could discuss differences in strategy and qualitative differences in the finished product and actually get value out of this thread, or we can all bash whatever and whoever doesn't agree with us and pretend we're on Jerry Springer wasting bandwidth for the edification of the masses.

Apple is what it is for a few really good reasons.  First let me say I've been using Linux as my primary workstation for over a decade.  I've also used a Mac off and on since 1984 (and an Apple ][ before that), both ones I've owned and ones others bought for me.  I like them both, for very different reasons.  Microsoft has never produced an operating system I felt was compelling enough to use for my own reasons, although for my work I need to test on several versions.

If Apple's current success and image can be put down to one factor, that factor is unity of focus.  Steve Jobs has had very specific ideas about what makes a computer into a tool that people want to use, and he is overwhelmingly right even if the end result could use some work at times.  The image that Apple presents is trendy, edgy and very definitely not "techie."

Here's something that illustrates the difference between Apple and Microsoft perfectly IMO:  [http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=YvX3laQlg14&playnext=1&list=PL9E6033D96001AB05](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=YvX3laQlg14&playnext=1&list=PL9E6033D96001AB05)

Now to illustrate the difference between that and Linux, you should either know the hardware specs (and the compatibility with Linux) before you even see the box, or you should boot it off the iso image and run a query tool like lspci.

The thing about Linux is that there are so many different philosophies driving development:
[LIST=1]
[*]You have the Apache faction.  These are people who don't see what they want to use out there, get a great idea for a great piece of software, get a group of like-minded people together, and build a truly amazing product to release to the community.  They design software to work with free or proprietary components, they do not really care all that much.  This is how the Open Source community should work IMO.
[*]You have the Richard Stallman faction.  These are people who insist that all software should be free, no matter what the designer thinks, and that anyone who uses any commercial software at all is some sort of traitor to the Open Source community.  They go about blindly copying commercial software feature per feature, approximating the look but not necessarily getting the details right.  They cannot make the intuitive leap to design something better than a commercial product or to design it in a way that is original, so they can never do quite as good a job as the commercial product.  As such, everything they make is always "catching up to" the commercial thing they copied from.
[*]You have the NASA faction.  These guys have a real need to use Open Source software for a higher purpose, and do not hesitate to build a driver or a component and release it as Open Source, but don't really care that much about developing a community.
[*]You have the corporate faction.  This is a commercial entity who uses Open Source and, for whatever reason, releases a product into the Open Source world.  This can be an advertisement for a more fully featured, commercially supported version of the same thing, or "payment" for using Open Source products in the spirit of the original Open Source movement.  Example:  Wine/Crossover Office.
[*]You have the loser faction.  This group uses Linux because they can legally get it without paying for it.  They do not contribute to any project in any way, and think that they should be able to complain to somebody about the lack of a feature or the way a feature works and expect somebody to fix it for them, and they still shouldn't feel the need to contribute to any project for it.
[*]You have the rest.
[/LIST]

While I don't particularly like some of those groups, I must confess that without those groups a lot of applications simply would not exist without them.

One tendency that Linux developers have is the "freedom" to adjust some component far beyond what's reasonable.  I have a particular example in mind:

My sister is a professional photographer.  She bought one of the new iMacs with the huge monitor.  She didn't have enough money to buy Photoshop right away, so I recommended the Gimp.  I don't really know anything about that sort of thing, but she tried it out.  The first thing she noticed was that while most of the features of photoshop existed in gimp, they were neither as easy to find in the menus nor were they as easy to use once you found them.  She bought Photoshop later and showed me an example why.  The feature she used was something I don't remember, but I'll use "brightness" as an example.  She showed me on the gimp how to use the control, then she showed the same thing on Photoshop.  Photoshop, the feature was easily and intuitively available, and once you started messing with the control it had a reasonable limit on the range of values which included only those values which somebody would actually want to choose in real practice.  In Gimp, the range of the control went from pure white to pure black, and the range of reasonable values was maybe 10% of the entire range available, meaning the control was so sensitive that she hardly dared touch it.

A "high vitality" open source project (one where there are a lot of active developers) tends to have a bunch of opinions on how something should go together, and not necessarily much expert advice on what is reasonable.  So for my above example, if I as a non-expert were programming in the "brightness" control I would naturally pick white and black as reasonable limits, not knowing that the truly reasonable limit is a much smaller range.

As a contrast a commercially developed product like Photoshop would hire a bunch of graphic designers to use their product before it gets released, and those people would tell them the control is much too hard to use before it ever got into a user's hands.  They would also say, "We use this thing a lot, so it needs to be easier to get to."  And the Powers that Be would make it so.

Cults:
Never discount the power of a cult.  And never be fooled:  Linux has every bit as strong a cult as Apple does.  It's just a different sort of person who belongs to that cult, although there is definitely a group of people who can belong to both cults.  I'm one of them.

Apple's cult is based on final productivity and simplicity.  They tend to be a bit more fashion conscious than a Linux user, so the look definitely makes a difference.  They tend to think of how the system will look in their office, the impression that will give and the usability of that system in their day.

Linux's cult is much more varied, and they tend to be meter readers and total nerds.  A meter reader looks at a list of features (conspicuously missing from Apple's packaging) and goes down the list.  The price of the thing they buy needs to be compared to the number of items in the feature list, regardless of whether that feature is integrated well or not.

A nerd is much more willing to put up with a more difficult to use product, because they are much more willing to go through whatever it takes to learn to use the thing in question, and more inclined to read the entire manual.

---

FWIW Apple did not release a version of Linux.  It released a version of UNIX.  Linux is UN*X, not having been licensed to create a version of UNIX using proprietary code.  Apple bought Next's license when they acquired the company.  As well, Apple's OS is BSD not System V.

### Post by fontis on 2011-07-07
> **1clue said:**
> Why do "we" need to topple anything?  Apple targets an audience that most Linux users don't even care about, IMO.  We're after the nerds who are on the left edge of Windows.


With topple I didn't simply mean replace OS X, but there is a clear reason as to why their UI is much slicker and apps are more seamlessly integrated into the whole experience than they are in Linux. It is weird because the one big advantage Linux has over OS X is that we use an open source platform, so application integration SHOULD in theory be much better on the Linux platform rather than OS X, yet this is not the case.

I sense that most people arguing here are not willing to be self-critical about their product(s) of choice, simply because it's on the Linux platform. (Kind of makes you think of a mirror-Apple-cult scenario huh?)

Someone mentioned here, that there's been almost two decades of failure in this department, and clearly there is need to be willing to look at the way things have been done and understand that we are lacking cohesiveness in our final product. Now, if the argument is that Linux is a "hobby kernel" to begin with, or Linux is meant to be used as a server station primarily etc, then what's the point in advocating and supporting Linux to begin with? This argument seems to be the safety net many people fall on when this debate starts and we all refuse to accept our shortcomings.

Don't get me wrong, in many ways we are stronger and better, and our resources vastly outnumber those of MS and Apple, the problem is these resources are spent in a bad way. There should be more open steps taken towards centralization of a standard protocol in which applications are put together into the whole, so that the overall experience is improved. You see a lot of people, even here, can complain about some things not being readily available on OS X (which is a problem we also have in our platform) but you rarely read about someone having an issue with some menu or dialog messing up things. Or how applications behave or look completely different than the rest of the OS.

### Post by 8_Bit on 2011-07-07
Yes, it's a good point. OSX has a centralized backing.

The Linux community continually fractures itself every time they create a new distro or derivative. It's all about "me, me, me". *"I *want to create my *own* distro, instead of contributing back to one that exists. It should be on *my* resume, *I* want the glory for myself! Even if the distro has nothing new to offer and only changes the default wallpaper!"

As long as people keep doing this Linux will never be as popular as OSX. It is part of the reason why programs never mesh or blend well into the desktop environment in Linux. Everyone has their own idea about what a UI should look like. That's perfectly fine and dandy but if you want a consistent user experience, you **must **agree on **one** set of guidelines, period, or you just confuse the user and developers.

That is one of the biggest things I miss from OSX. Apps always looked like they belonged there. A consistent UI. Linux has a long way to go on that one...

---

### Post by Paqman on 2011-07-07
Slickness and integration of apps won't help Linux, as it's already significantly better than the mainstream OS, Windows. If all we had to do to get users was present an OS that was more seamlessly put together then we'd have eaten a big chunk of Windows' market share already. Design and integration of apps on Windows is woeful, every app looks completely different. Distros like Ubuntu already have Windows beat in that regard, but it hasn't translated into masses of users.

What desktop Linux needs is strong, consistent backing from a big company like Google, Amazon, or one of the OEMs. It needs to be pre-installed on hardware designed for it. Until it gets that, it won't make much headway IMO.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-07
> **fontis said:**
> With topple I didn't simply mean replace OS X, but there is a clear reason as to why their UI is much slicker and apps are more seamlessly integrated into the whole experience than they are in Linux. It is weird because the one big advantage Linux has over OS X is that we use an open source platform, so application integration SHOULD in theory be much better on the Linux platform rather than OS X, yet this is not the case.


Not really.  I got the developer documentation for Mac OS back in the 80's.  They laid out flat, this is how it works and there will be no accommodation of any other way.  You could do anything you wanted, including building your own window manager, but they all worked off the same model.  There was a spec for the way a video card had to work, rather than whatever the manufacturer wanted and would make a driver for.  For awhile, they even said the dot pitch on your screen was 72 dpi, that's it.  They wanted WYSIWYG to be absolutely true, between the screen and the printed document.

You won't get that with Open Source, there would be too many dissenting voices.

> 
I sense that most people arguing here are not willing to be self-critical about their product(s) of choice, simply because it's on the Linux platform. (Kind of makes you think of a mirror-Apple-cult scenario huh?)


Yes, there is.  I mentioned that before in this thread.  I really belong to both cults, if you can imagine that.  I love both operating systems for their strengths.

> 
... Now, if the argument is that Linux is a "hobby kernel" to begin with, or Linux is meant to be used as a server station primarily etc, then what's the point in advocating and supporting Linux to begin with? This argument seems to be the safety net many people fall on when this debate starts and we all refuse to accept our shortcomings.


Linux is in no way a hobby kernel.  A really big portion of network card development is the United States government.  Linux is used to make cluster computers to study weather, nuclear events, simulate aircraft or spacecraft, and a whole lot of other things.  It's the most cost effective supercomputer, and recently the most maintainable.  PS3 notwithstanding, that is really set for a special case.

Keep in mind the heritage of the three operating systems.
Windows and Mac came about as commercial enterprises, to sell a finished desktop product and make money.  Their focus has always been on the user interface, although they both had incredibly different ideas of what that meant.  The underpinnings of Windows still leave a bit to be desired IMO, and back in OS9 and earlier the underpinnings of Mac were stupidly terrible.

UNIX has been a server operating system since the beginning.  They acquired X not long after the other guys, but it was always server-based.  They focused on a solid kernel, a really stable core operating system and good, relatively consistent compliance with a central standard.

Open Source picking up on the heavy standards-based design that UNIX had is no accident.  If you're going to model an operating system on something, something with standards would be the thing to pick.  And FWIW UNIX was changed by Open Source IMO, in a big way.

Putting UNIX under the Mac UI was the best decision Apple could have made.

> 
Don't get me wrong, in many ways we are stronger and better, and our resources vastly outnumber those of MS and Apple, the problem is these resources are spent in a bad way.

I think this is false.  I can't find the reference but last I heard Open Source had approximately the same number of developer resources as Windows, but that many of them were working for a lot less money.  But I agree that many of the Open Source resources are  spent in a bad way.

But keep in mind that Mac and Windows have hardware specs much much sooner than Open Source.  Intel talks with Microsoft to collaborate on what the next generation of chips should do, while Open Source generally gets hardware tech specs after the public release of the hardware, if at all.

> **8_Bit said:**
> Yes, it's a good point. OSX has a centralized backing.

The Linux community continually fractures itself every time they create a new distro or derivative. It's all about "me, me, me". *"I *want to create my *own* distro, instead of contributing back to one that exists. It should be on *my* resume, *I* want the glory for myself! Even if the distro has nothing new to offer and only changes the default wallpaper!"

As long as people keep doing this Linux will never be as popular as OSX. It is part of the reason why programs never mesh or blend well into the desktop environment in Linux. Everyone has their own idea about what a UI should look like. That's perfectly fine and dandy but if you want a consistent user experience, you **must **agree on **one** set of guidelines, period, or you just confuse the user and developers.

That is one of the biggest things I miss from OSX. Apps always looked like they belonged there. A consistent UI. Linux has a long way to go on that one...

I can't possibly agree with any of that.  Open Source exists because there is a need.  If nobody uses a component or distro then that distro dies, as it should.  I can't say how many times I've seen somebody claim to start a distro on a forum, but it usually dies before there's ever a website.

There are a wildly diverse number of window managers, but they all follow a standard.  There are a diverse number of themes, but within each window manager those themes all are consistent.  In my opinion too many window managers and themes use a Windows/Mac approach and too few use something different.  As long as the standards are met, then let diversity win every time!  It's how things get better.

> **Paqman said:**
> Slickness and integration of apps won't help Linux, as it's already significantly better than the mainstream OS, Windows. If all we had to do to get users was present an OS that was more seamlessly put together then we'd have eaten a big chunk of Windows' market share already. Design and integration of apps on Windows is woeful, every app looks completely different. Distros like Ubuntu already have Windows beat in that regard, but it hasn't translated into masses of users.

What desktop Linux needs is strong, consistent backing from a big company like Google, Amazon, or one of the OEMs. It needs to be pre-installed on hardware designed for it. Until it gets that, it won't make much headway IMO.

I believe you can order any IBM hardware with Linux on it.  That goes from laptops to mainframes.  And it's fully supported even if you change distro.

I don't believe that what Linux has is already better in terms of apps or desktop/consumer drivers.

With drivers, I get decent support for my sound card for example but the external console with the knobs on it could just as well go in the trash.  It has never worked from day one, and I see no comment on any of the manufacturer lists that any support is forthcoming.  The Open Source crowd that handles that sort of thing seems to be after general support of a wide range of devices but don't care about specific features on higher-end devices.  Oh well, I guess that extra panel only cost me $100 so I'm not out that much.

With some apps, the quality is extremely good.  With others you get a bunch of people who are copying features from a commercial app on another OS, and it generally turns out to suck.  The controls are not well thought out, they don't work right or they're hard to get at.  Other apps, some fairly necessary features just aren't there.  Not done yet, or nobody thought of it.

### Post by handy on 2011-07-07
> **fontis said:**
> So, what can WE do to topple that?
What can we start doing differently to increase the aesthetics and seamless functionality of Linux and Ubuntu?
...


People forget that Apple are primarily a hardware company that makes OSs & some software for some of their products & provides other services to enhance their hardware packages & make them more appealing to the marketplace.

As has been said before in this thread, that gives them a great advantage when it comes to reliability of their OS & control of how software is written by themselves & outsiders, that runs on their OSs. All software must abide by certain standards, the same menu items must share the same position & keyboard equivalents etc...

For Linux to compete with this I think may be asking a bit much. We have multiple DEs, WMs & so thousands of independent developers, let alone 500+ distros.

(Thankfully) there is no Steve Jobs laying down the law about precisely what position an icon will be in & what colours it will use for a particular OS release. (Or the famous change of colour of a motherboard story!).

Linux by its very open-source nature allows itself to be all things to all people. Which is why I can use Arch with exactly what I want installed no more & no less, & Openbox with a GTK+ theme & the Xfce-panel, & have the fonts I want at the size I want (24 point) without having someone place limits on any & all of the above.

This is a prime reason why I love to use Linux, I can customise it freely. If I was a programmer I expect that I would love it even more for the same reason.

So, I can't see that we can topple Apple. As far as MS, Apple & Linux are concerned we are far more similar to MS than we are to the multifaceted Apple Co. who have nearly failed more than once in their history, but these days have an extremely cohesive management team that are very serious about making every post a winner.

The question that comes to my mind is why do "we" want to topple anyone? Isn't what "we" have in general moving along very well? I've seen tremendous improvements in the Linux system over the last 5.5 years that I've been involved with it. The average numbers using these forums have increased by roughly 250%. That says a lot to me. I don't have to go looking to try & work out what the percentage of Linux users are, I just look at the forum usage numbers to know that it is surely a great deal more than it was 5 years ago. 5 years is really not a very long time.

### Post by handy on 2011-07-07
> **fontis said:**
> .
...

There should be more open steps taken towards centralization of a standard protocol in which applications are put together into the whole, so that the overall experience is improved. You see a lot of people, even here, can complain about some things not being readily available on OS X (which is a problem we also have in our platform) but you rarely read about someone having an issue with some menu or dialog messing up things. Or how applications behave or look completely different than the rest of the OS.

The superficial problems re. the cohesion of the QT/GTK interfaces when used in each others DE are improving & will disappear altogether before too long.

As far as any centralised administration of Linux is concerned I can't see it happening (thankfully) though there certainly are a few areas that would benefit the whole & are gradually being worked on. 

Such as the creation of a centralised repo that can be used by both .rpm & .deb based distros. Such a thing would free up so many people from package management & allow them to focus on far more productive pursuits which would inevitably benefit Linux as a whole.

### Post by fontis on 2011-07-07
> **handy said:**
> Well I totally disagree with you.

The ability to exercise your freedom of choice & creativity within & through Linux is its greatest strength.

I don't want someone who thinks they have great taste imposing it on me. Causing me to have to live with it & allowing me only the slightest modicum of movement re. the modification of the GUI. (Or anything else for that matter.)

Centralised power by its very nature is disassociated from the masses, (e.g. globalisation), & creates dissatisfaction.

One of the things that I really don't like about OSX, is the way it looks & the numerous limitations placed on what you can actually change. This is not all ascetic it is practically flawed due to this as well. (& I know what I'm speaking of as we have 3 Apple machines in this house.)


I don't think he, or I for that matter, are referring to forcing you to use ONE interface like OS X does. But rather that applications follow a set of guidelines that define the way they behave within the UI. That means that whether you are using GNOME or XFCE, the integration of Pidgin should be seamless because both GNOME and XFCE (and Pidgin for that matter) follow some form of guideline as to how the application will behave and integrate into that set environment. This causes less "glitching" or distortions of your user experience when using the OS or the app(s) that follow.

I don't think anyone is saying that we should all burn down anything which isn't X or Y, but rather embrace that there is X and Y, **however** having X and Y adhere to certain guidelines meaning that they are both viable options that seamlessly integrate into the user experience.


EDIT:
I also don't believe that the Linux ecosystem should continue to remain as it is today, which frankly put just looks like a pissing contest. The immense amount of breakage from upstream is really in most senses counter-productive. Like someone earlier mentioned, you have distro's popping up like banana flies, and just like banana flies die after a short go. There are at least 100 music applications available that all do the same thing, and all lack the same things as well. This is where it stagnates, because fundamentally the FOSS movement is not profit-driven like companies are, therefore there is no incentive in the sense of profit that furthers development of x application(s). Until the mentality of people involved starts to shift away from this fragmented stand and more into a unified stance, we won't reach anything. The quality of applications will also stagnate because every time someone disagrees they will just fork something away just to switch something arbitrary like position of Menu-bar or icons. And as a result of that, Linux on DE will always remain as a sub-par choice for mainstream usage.

As weird as it sounds, the biggest thing holding Linux back is in fact it's own community's selfishness. Not financial backing, not mass-advertising and not even Apple or MS.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2011-07-07
I love Macs, but they do not "just work," as some have claimed. Here is why I would strongly recommend Macs over Linux to anyone not tech-savvy, though: [list=1][*]There are more Apple stores than Linux stores, and in the Apple store, they can walk in and try out the computers in person.[*]The Genius Bar is actually helpful, and much easier for non-tech-savvy users than searching forums.[*]The external Mac hardware is really nice, and the battery life on Mac laptops is amazing.[*]There's lots of third-party support (you often hear "We support both PC and Mac" and by that they mean Windows and Mac--no mention of Linux). Off the top of my head, Netflix streaming, Google Music, Microsoft Office, iTunes, Songbird, Amazon MP3 Uploader, and Adobe Creative Suite are all available for Windows and Mac but not for Linux.[/list] I definitely am not in the "Macs are magic. They just work" camp. But I'm also not in the "Macs are overrated pieces of crap" camp either.

More details:
[Macs are just computers, not magic](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/macs-are-just-computers-not-magic/)
[Macs are computers, not magic (part 2)](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/macs-are-computers-not-magic-part-2/)
[An unbiased view on Macs](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/an-unbiased-view-on-macs/)
[The Macbook Pro Dead Video Card Saga](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/the-macbook-pro-dead-video-card-saga/)

[*]The external Mac hardware is really nice, and the battery life on Mac laptops is amazing.
[*]There's lots of third-party support (you often hear "We support both PC and Mac" and by that they mean Windows and Mac--no mention of Linux). Off the top of my head, Netflix streaming, Google Music, Microsoft Office, iTunes, Songbird, Amazon MP3 Uploader, and Adobe Creative Suite are all available for Windows and Mac but not for Linux.
[/LIST]
I definitely am not in the "Macs are magic. They just work" camp. But I'm also not in the "Macs are overrated pieces of crap" camp either.

More details:
[Macs are just computers, not magic]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/macs-are-just-computers-not-magic/")
[Macs are computers, not magic (part 2)]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/macs-are-computers-not-magic-part-2/")
[An unbiased view on Macs]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/an-unbiased-view-on-macs/")
[The Macbook Pro Dead Video Card Saga]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/the-macbook-pro-dead-video-card-saga/")

Agreed.

But I would add....

Mac's just works for those who use them and like them.
Linux just works for those who use it and like it.
Windows just works for those who use it and like it.

It is horses for courses and that is why I use all three and recommend either or depending on the customer.

I'm thoroughly impressed with what comes down stream & I use Arch which gets upgrades everyday!

> **fontis said:**
> 
Like someone earlier mentioned, you have distro's popping up like banana flies, and just like banana flies die after a short go. There are at least 100 music applications available that all do the same thing, and all lack the same things as well. 

That is creativity at its best. Those that make a distro because they want to prove that they can learn a great deal which then gets applied to something else where they learn more & can express their creativity in different ways.

As far as the coding is concerned, that code is available to all. Because the environment exists where people can create new versions of things, which may be bringing nothing new to the table, it makes the space & environment available for the one that does bring something new to the table.

This is Darwinism. The new code is available for all to read & learn from & use & modify & do things the same until someone else is inspired to make another breakthrough.

This is the weakness & great strength of open-source. You can't change it, it is in the license. :)

> **fontis said:**

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-07
> **handy said:**
> The ability to exercise your freedom of choice & creativity within & through Linux is its greatest strength. (Excluding the server side.)


+1

> 
I don't want someone who thinks they have great taste imposing it on me. Causing me to have to live with it & allowing me only the slightest modicum of movement re. the modification of the GUI. (Or anything else for that matter.)

Centralised power by its very nature is disassociated from the masses, (e.g. globalisation), & creates dissatisfaction.


Then don't go with that thing.  As long as there is enough dissatisfaction with any one approach, there will be somebody to offer up an alternative.

> **KiwiNZ said:**
> The fragmentation is a real obstacle to uptake. Too many choices is just as bad as lack of choice.

Like I said earlier "It is my fervent belief that we need a quantum change, the Linux sector  has been banging away in its current methodology and ideals for two  decades and we have have not even gained one percentage point on the  desktop."

We have good people working on Linux but their efforts are spread of too projects thus diluting the results.

One thing that almost certainly colors your figures is that Open Source is being adopted on non-free platforms.  During that two decades more than one large corporation adopted, in part, Open Source publicly and contributed significant software.  Lots of commercial enterprises have begun using Open Source ... well, openly.

> **fontis said:**
> I don't think he, or I for that matter, are referring to forcing you to use ONE interface like OS X does. But rather that applications follow a set of guidelines that define the way they behave within the UI. That means that whether you are using GNOME or XFCE, the integration of Pidgin should be seamless because both GNOME and XFCE (and Pidgin for that matter) follow some form of guideline as to how the application will behave and integrate into that set environment. This causes less "glitching" or distortions of your user experience when using the OS or the app(s) that follow.


What exactly would you propose?  I haven't looked lately, but last I checked the incompatibilities were considered to be features of whatever window manager.  It's possible that some of the new ideas have solidified around one method or another, but that's normal evolution of software.

> 
...
I also don't believe that the Linux ecosystem should continue to remain as it is today, which frankly put just looks like a pissing contest. The immense amount of breakage from upstream is really in most senses counter-productive. Like someone earlier mentioned, you have distro's popping up like banana flies, and just like banana flies die after a short go. There are at least 100 music applications available that all do the same thing, and all lack the same things as well.


That's democracy in action.  With a commercial enterprise you don't see the pissing contest.  You see what came out on top.  The company no doubt solicited ideas, chose a few to test feasibility on and chose one as the best option.  Maybe more organized but probably less likely to take advantage of a wacky but good idea from left field.

That said I have my own frustrations with media on Linux.  It's frustrating to wade through MythTV when all you wanted to do is stick a Blu-Ray disk in and watch it on your monitor.

> 
This is where it stagnates, because fundamentally the FOSS movement is not profit-driven like companies are, therefore there is no incentive in the sense of profit that furthers development of x application(s). Until the mentality of people involved starts to shift away from this fragmented stand and more into a unified stance, we won't reach anything. The quality of applications will also stagnate because every time someone disagrees they will just fork something away just to switch something arbitrary like position of Menu-bar or icons. And as a result of that, Linux on DE will always remain as a sub-par choice for mainstream usage.


I totally disagree with you here.  You're making a false assumption about FOSS.  Just because you don't have to pay for the software doesn't mean the whole movement is not-for-profit.  They're just using a different model.

It's like back in the 60's when all the hippies were sitting around using drugs.  Free love, communism, a whole lot of other ideas that were either seen as subversive (in ways that they weren't actually subversive) or totally misunderstood.  People thought hippies were lazy do-nothing good-for-nothing wastrels, and now it turns out that an alarming number of hippies turned out to be extremely bright, aggressive businessmen.

Open Source is all about profit.  I submit my project for your use, which gives me the right to use yours.  Combine the parts and come up with a low-cost, highly customizable system that does exactly what I want without bizarre terms of use that are typically imposed by commercial software.

> 
As weird as it sounds, the biggest thing holding Linux back is in fact it's own community's selfishness. Not financial backing, not mass-advertising and not even Apple or MS.

As odd as it may sound with my paragraph above, I think you're wrong again.  I don't see selfishness at all in the community.  There's a lot of conceit from certain individuals and organizations, but you get that everywhere.  If you're selfish you don't put hard work into something, create a great product and then give it away.

### Post by tumbes2000 on 2011-07-08
I really don't think that it realyl has anything to do with OS X itself.  At the heart of it, Apple is a harward manufacturer, similar to Dell, Motorola, Samsung.  They make great looking products and have done a fantastic job making their products techie status symbols.  A very small group of people actually need to use Apple's, mainly graphic artists, indie film, and some educational stuff.  Past those small groups, Apple users are no different than windows users, expect their devices look good and in turn make the user feel cool.  

Apple uses the OS as a way to provide only ONE very controlled experience knowing that the vast majority of their users have a very limited tech back round.  It is a hell of a lot easier to program when you know exactly the hardware installed on the system and easier to avoid bugs.  So when people use the system "it works" because of the limited harward it needs to run on and they are satisfied with the "device" they purchased.  In other words, the consumer does not see a difference between the hardware and software, its just a device, like a dvd player.  

For example look at the issues are people have with their Android phones.  Since many people do not understand that Google produces the software and another company (motorola, samsung, htc, etc) pruduces the hardware in the traditioanl Windows/X86 format,, they will whine continuously to the manufacturer even though the issue is in Google's android OS.  The consumer does NOT see the seperation.  This is where Steve Jobs was abosutely correct - one company has to controll the ENTIRE experience the consumer has with the device.  Not very democractic, but when who cares when your dictactor is so benevolent ;)

Linux, on the other had, is an OS with no hardware and it has to run perfect on a million configruation.  That is a tough job.  However, Ubuntu is doing a d*mn good job getting there.:p

Ubuntu is making progress in this regard (ie, Unity and all of Shuttleworth's indicators, NoticeOSD, etc.) But Apple is a paid for product that invested more into making stuff with intelligent design and ease of use. (Note: I don't think Apple got everything perfect, else they've have more sales). Ubuntu has just started doing this kind of thing. And it prolly does not have as many people doing it or as much money behind its creation, so everything is slower to appear. I often wonder if Ubuntu charged money for the OS whether that additional money would help make Ubuntu implement shuttleworth's vision faster and actually result in more people using ubuntu.

### Post by nrundy on 2011-07-08
> **8_Bit said:**
> Yes, it's a good point. OSX has a centralized backing.

The Linux community continually fractures itself every time they create a new distro or derivative. It's all about "me, me, me". *"I *want to create my *own* distro, instead of contributing back to one that exists. It should be on *my* resume, *I* want the glory for myself! Even if the distro has nothing new to offer and only changes the default wallpaper!"

As long as people keep doing this Linux will never be as popular as OSX. It is part of the reason why programs never mesh or blend well into the desktop environment in Linux. Everyone has their own idea about what a UI should look like. That's perfectly fine and dandy but if you want a consistent user experience, you **must **agree on **one** set of guidelines, period, or you just confuse the user and developers.

That is one of the biggest things I miss from OSX. Apps always looked like they belonged there. A consistent UI. Linux has a long way to go on that one...

I agree. There is too much disagreement and in-fighting. The negative side to democracy. This is where a strong leader is crucial to getting things done. Both Bill Gates and Steve Jobs are strong personalities that drove their product. They were the final word and their vision allowed the singular focus to result in a solid product. Linux hasn't really had this until Shuttleworth came along IMHO. I think Ubuntu would destroy the other OSs if Shuttleworth had the workforce under him that Jobs and Gates had. The Ubuntu developers are enormously talented. I just think there needs to be more of them to get more things done in each release. But to get more developers, I think Ubuntu needs to turn into a paid product, at least I wonder/theorize about this.

Shuttleworth's leadership and decisions even when people strongly disagreed with him are what has taken Ubuntu as far as it is. I can't praise Shuttleworth enough. And just hope that he continues to listen patiently and hear all sides of issues but make a decision in the end and stay true to his vision even if he's still cricitzed.

### Post by GarlicBread on 2011-07-09
> **fontis said:**
> I was reading the entries on the other thread here in the cafe regarding Linux ads and what not. I came to ponder upon this question and thought I'd start off a topic about it.

So why is it that OS X is so much more popular and usable than Linux distros are?
They both share so many similarities, but in reality when it comes to usage OS X seems light-years ahead of Linux. What gives?

If you take the advertising and the "cult" surrounding OS X away, it feels like Apple has managed to polish their OS in a much better way than the Linux ecosystem has. Stability aside, compatibility seems to no longer really be an issue when using OS X. But then again, I don't own OS X so I can't comment on that really.

1) In OS X you virtually NEVER have to use the terminal, in Ubuntu - as user friendly as it is, you still do at times.  People don't like having to use Google to get their computer to work correctly or get what they want done.

2) OS X is shiny and polished, far more than Ubuntu or even Windows 7.  People like shiny things.

3) Computers aren't sold in stores with Linux on them, and the average computer user isn't computer literate enough to bother investigating operating systems and manually switching from Windows/OS X to Linux.

2) OS X is shiny and polished, far more than Ubuntu or even Windows 7.  People like shiny things.

3) Computers aren't sold in stores with Linux on them, and the average computer user isn't computer literate enough to bother investigating operating systems and manually switching from Windows/OS X to Linux.

1) Terminal gives you extra power, it is not necessarily a tool for fix stuff but a productive one. OSX has one but it is really behind *nix in this area.

2) Shiny is subjective and not a primary key in computing.. anyway I believe there are cool things also outside the so famous apple-world.
Something like [this]("http://shop.lenovo.com/us/products/Laptops/ThinkPad/x-series/x1/index.html"), for example (not the OS!).

3) agree with you.

### Post by arunb on 2011-07-09
terminals are not a good idea, if linux needs to get more popular then its time to ditch the terminal once for all. 

Develop better applications and packages that are compatible with linux installations, have self correct and error recovery capability like most Windows installers.

The presence of terminals indicate the product is not matured yet and needs some intricate tweaking.

I don't remember using the terminal in Windows after Windows 98.

Another problem is installing applications from source code, as many goods apps are available as a source code, installing them on Ubuntu is a nightmare, I think there should be an utility that compiles, makes and installs application from source.

Second area that needs improvement is crash recovery and system rescue. Here again Windows scores better, the Restore point system used in Windows XP is very useful. I think Linux needs a similar tool. Also if the crash detection and recovery is made automatically without user intervention it would be very great improvement.

[LIST=1]
[*]Mac OS < X did not have a command line.

[*]OS X supports X11 and gcc and almost anything else you can think of that you might use on Linux
[*]I haven't noticed a need to use the command line for normal user tasks in Ubuntu, but I do it anyway.
[*]The first application I opened in Mac OS X was the terminal.  I use it as much as I use the Linux terminal, which is a lot.

### Post by aysiu on 2011-07-09
> **wolfen69 said:**
> It's an OS to do a job. I hardly think of fun and cool when I think Mac.
Why can't it be both?

I think it does a job well, and it's fun and cool. I like OS X.

I just don't believe people when they say "it just works" or "you don't need to use the terminal." Well, my Time Machine doesn't work, so I do have to use the terminal to back up (I wrote a script using *rsync*). If I want to show hidden files (for example, to see the ~/.rsync directory), I have to paste a command into the terminal: ```
defaults write com.apple.finder AppleShowAllFiles TRUE
``` Not only that, but I have to kill Finder afterwards for the change to take effect: ```
killall Finder
``` which means that any open windows or file operations (copying or moving files) will just die. In Ubuntu, I can hit Control-H and hidden files are shown immediately.

Every OS has pros and cons. No OS is magic.

---

### Post by el_koraco on 2011-07-09

### Post by aysiu on 2011-07-09

> There's no showing hidden folders with an easy way? That's just a weird design decision. I don't get it.
There are some third-party GUI tools you can install to make it point-and-click, but you still have to kill Finder for the change to take effect.

### Post by pedrotuga on 2011-07-09
I guess I'll get a load of angry replies, but I could care less...
If you're an apple fanboy and want to get a nerve poked, by all means, read the following post: :)

I don't think I need to respect or to agree with current time apple's philosophy to have great respect for what they once did.
Heck wozniak himself says that apple has become something that means little or noting to him.

So I don't think it's so fair that you guys pull all the good achievements of apple in the past and throw them in this discussion.
If it matters, I'll remind you that you're bringing up stuff that is twice as old as linux.

On to apple vs linux in present time...

I think apple enjoys a way higher status than it deserves. Micrsoft for example, had to be put through all kind of criticism and were portraited [quite rightly] as the evil being in software industry. Apple resources to pretty much the same policies and gets to be embraced as a gift to the computer technology world. I am sorry, but that's nothing but the result of a society with degraded values ans quite hypocrite too.


On the subject of user friendliness... That is a very personal mater. What0s user friendly to me might not be to other person. I got to use apple computers a couple of times and I didn't find them user friendly at all. If by user friendly people mean flashy, with funny looking visual effects, then I believe my concept of user friendliness is rather different than that of most.

I like linux because it allows me to run a set of simple yet efficient applications that simply makes me more productive.
OSX and windows embrace bloated applications that are supposed to make you look cool for some esoteric reason.

I don't dislike windows XP, but it took microsoft a ridiculously large amount of time to make it a stable secure product. And I don't like that it costs money.

On top of all that it's a mater of principle and ethics. I am 100% against things like itunes or any kind of locks, both on software and hardware. I don't think a software company has the right to charge per copy or decide what the user do whith his/her hardware, that's just unethical in my opinion.

But what greatly annoys me is how popular OSX is among developers. Ok, ignorant people buy macs because they think that will make them cool, but I can't think of a reason why an open source (for example) software developer would prefer to run osx instead of linux.

But as one grows older one learns to be less uthopic and accept things as they are. I've grown into enjoying the lack of popularity of the linux desktop. I actually like that linux is reserved to a smaller and more interesting group of people. This is what allows it to be a more personal thing, more customizable, more open to contributions from many people that do many interesting small things.

So, to answer the question "why is is less popular?": It's less popular because that's a requirement for being an interesting and valuable thing.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
Oh, come on guys!  It's not a contest.  It might be for Mac and Windows, but it's not for Linux.  Linux is for people who want something different, and have very specific ideas about what that might be.

There's way too much hate and angst going around on this thread, and the things some of you are saying are either purely subjective or they don't make any sense.

If it were a "we vs they" scenario -- and it's not -- you need to acknowledge the strengths of the opposing team or you will fail.

I run both Mac and Linux, and have been around both since they were both still green.  I like both architectures.  I do a lot of the same things on both boxes.

Linux has a lot of things going for it, and so does OS X.  Some of those things are similar, since they both have a UN*X heritage.

Linux has an extremely well documented, open architecture.  There was a point where no commercial desktop operating system was as stable as Linux.  The whole concept of live updates to fix bugs through the Internet came from Open Source operating systems as far as I can tell.  The Windows and Mac software centers had to respond to that, so they came up with what they do, which finally spurred Linux to make a bunch of GUI software updaters as well.  A whole lot of Open Source ideas have caused the entire industry to jump.

Linux has a highly configurable choice of user experiences.  They include an extremely robust command line, a plethora of window managers and endless themes within each window manager, along with desktop environments and anything else you see as a feature of a modern computer's user interface.

Mac OS X has, bar none, the best fonts on any platform, out of the box.  I spend hours every day writing software.  The quality of the images on the screen make a huge difference for eye strain.  Ubuntu has the best fonts on any Linux distro I've seen out of the box, but they do not compare to Mac fonts.  Anybody who doubts this, get 100 people who are all over 40 years old and make a study.

Mac OS has the best user experience of any platform.  You only get one choice, but they really nail it.  Apple has literally either invented or have been the first out of the gate for any number of innovations.  Like a window manager.  Like a track pad that people actually can prefer to a mouse.  The first company to release a PostScript printer, which was as far as I can tell the first printer to have rendered fonts instead of built-in fonts.

Mac OS is not magic.  It has bugs and cumbersome features and badness, but if you use a computer the way the typical Mac user uses a computer, you don't see much of that.

Steve Jobs is a user interface genius, but he's not always right.  He can and has made some stupendous intuitive leaps with regards to the usability of technology.  He is the reason why Apple is what Apple is.  That was proved when he left for a few years and the company floundered.  Steve Jobs also has some extremely irritating ideas about openness of architecture and software rights management.

Don't you guys see it?  Apple's strengths are Linux's weaknesses, and Linux's strengths are Apple's weaknesses.  Apple finally got it right by putting their UI on top of UNIX.  Their back-end was always the worst thing they had going, except for awhile the hardware was terrible.

Frankly though, in putting UNIX under Mac OS X, they've done a wonderful thing for Open Source.  Much of Mac OS is built on Open Source.  A few proprietary technologies are left, basically for the really cool stuff that makes apple what it is.  You can download the bare OS and run it the same way we're running Linux.  Or you can start X on top of Mac OS and run any of the Open Source packages you already know.

What they did is give people the idea that UNIX can be glitzy and sharp.  Anybody notice how much change has happened to Ubuntu since then?  Sure there's some copying of ideas, but in general Ubuntu has gone its own separate way but got sharper in the process.

---

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
@pedrotuga,

I won't quote your post because we both made some seriously long posts.

I am that developer you're talking about.  I use both Mac and Linux to develop, and the one at work is the Mac.  I can answer a few things that, while they may not be popular answers here, or answers that I like, they are the perspective of the owner of my company and the reason why we develop on Macs.

[LIST=1]
[*]The Mac is easier to set up, and takes less work to maintain.
[*]The undescribable UI features on Mac OS make getting things done much easier.
[*]While Linux and Windows have many of the features I'll be talking about, those features either take more configuration, or are more cumbersome, or both.
[*]Mac OS has some killer apps:
[LIST=1]
[*]TextMate.  It's just a text editor, but in doing that it can and does do a monumentally better job than any IDE
[*]Versions subversion client.  Better than Eclipse/STS by the same amount that Eclipse is better than the command line.
[*]Quicksilver.  Ubuntu has anything-el, kupfer and gnome-do, none of which measure up.
[*]Path Finder.  Just a dual finder window with some extra features, but those features seem to get things done sooner.
[*]Parallels.  Virtualization tool that integrates the virtualized OS with the host, and even allows the Windows/X11 windows to coexist on the desktop with the mac.
[/LIST]
[*]Mac, as of a year ago when we bought the Macs, handled multiple monitors much better than Linux.
[*]Macs have incredibly better fonts than Linux, and Linux has incredibly better fonts than Windows.
[*]Laptop battery life.  If I'm careful I can go literally 7 hours without plugging in, while doing real work.  That's with a 17" monitor with 1080x1200 resolution.
[/LIST]

On the terminal, Mac OS either already has or can easily get anything that a Linux developer uses.  Vim works on both, any open-source compiler works, the shells are identical.  A few command-line tools have a few different options.  Mysql works as though it were designed for it.

### Post by pedrotuga on 2011-07-09
> **KiwiNZ said:**

A software company is in business to sell its product, fine. If I take copy a software I bought at home, the created copy is NOT their product. They don't have the right to decide that it is just because it's better for their business. If they can't find a sustainable business model why fail to see how that is my problem in any way.

They have the right to set the terms for purchase of their products, but they should not have the right to decide what you do with the product once it gets to the customer hands. It's no longer their property, in fact they should have no right over it, at all. Do I need to remind you that the customer payed for the product? Are we forgetting the basic principle of trading?



> **1clue said:**
> That is extremely false.

> **1clue said:**
> @pedrotuga,

I won't quote your post because we both made some seriously long posts.

I am that developer you're talking about.  I use both Mac and Linux to develop, and the one at work is the Mac.  I can answer a few things that, while they may not be popular answers here, or answers that I like, they are the perspective of the owner of my company and the reason why we develop on Macs.

[LIST=1]
[*]The Mac is easier to set up, and takes less work to maintain.
[*]The undescribable UI features on Mac OS make getting things done much easier.
[*]While Linux and Windows have many of the features I'll be talking about, those features either take more configuration, or are more cumbersome, or both.
[*]Mac OS has some killer apps:
[LIST=1]
[*]TextMate.  It's just a text editor, but in doing that it can and does do a monumentally better job than any IDE
[*]Versions subversion client.  Better than Eclipse/STS by the same amount that Eclipse is better than the command line.
[*]Quicksilver.  Ubuntu has anything-el, kupfer and gnome-do, none of which measure up.
[*]Path Finder.  Just a dual finder window with some extra features, but those features seem to get things done sooner.
[*]Parallels.  Virtualization tool that integrates the virtualized OS with the host, and even allows the Windows/X11 windows to coexist on the desktop with the mac.
[/LIST]
[*]Mac, as of a year ago when we bought the Macs, handled multiple monitors much better than Linux.
[*]Macs have incredibly better fonts than Linux, and Linux has incredibly better fonts than Windows.
[*]Laptop battery life.  If I'm careful I can go literally 7 hours without plugging in, while doing real work.  That's with a 17" monitor with 1080x1200 resolution.
[/LIST]

On the terminal, Mac OS either already has or can easily get anything that a Linux developer uses.  Vim works on both, any open-source compiler works, the shells are identical.  A few command-line tools have a few different options.  Mysql works as though it were designed for it.
Thumbs up for a rational counter-argument.
I admit without any problem the downsides:
 * multi-monitor support is not very polished yet to say the least, true.
 * fonts: Spot on. I totally agree. Peronsally i think windows XP has the best fonts out of the box. On gnome, openbox, etc. I usually install microsoft fonts or, this great font called terminus.
 *VCS, while this is arguable, I _mostly_ agree with you. This depends on the VCS you use, of course.


But I don't really agree with other points:

* gnome do and kupfer might be behind quicksilver, but in my opinion deskbar-applet beats any other easily. It has a state of the art plugin system, it integrates with indexing services on the gnome desktop and with many applications. And it's open which means that you can make it do things that it doesn't yet. Pity it is discontinued :(

* Simply admiting text mate is superior sounds like a very biased opinion. There are MANY text editors out there, I use a handful of them on windows and linux, they impress me in different ways. I seriously doubt text-mate is superior to gedit, scribes, notepad++, pspad, vim, emacs, eclipse, etc. in all aspects.
You can argue that it has the features you like, but that's personal preference. Does it have any characteristic that sets it at a different level than the others?

* battery life. Not sure is OS related. Have you installed windows or linux on your mac? Is the autonomy significantly shorter?

As you can see from my reply, I don't find OS X significantly more suited for a developer. I do think linux has other advantages:
 - choice of desktop, filebrowser, window manager, etc
 - generally more customizable
 - free as in free beer
 - total control of what you want to run on a work session
... etc.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
> **KiwiNZ said:**
