...
They are also very good at bringing all their products together to offer a slick interacting package and thus the success of OSX, IOS and the Apple brand.

HAH!  I played with a Newton way back when they first came out.  They had speech recognition of sorts, you could say something and it would type what it thought you said.

You're right, they were horrible.  Except for entertainment, because you could say "I went to work today" and it would print out "I ate a penguin with hay" or something equally nonsensical.

But I completely agree on timing.  When the iPad came out I thought, "Oh boy, it's a Newton."  I seriously thought it would fall flat.  Now I kinda want one, because some of the apps really kick ... rear.

> **dava4444 said:**
> Hi

okay so I'm OS neutral, but with a blackmarket trade in Ubuntu, Ubuntu vs OSX is a good thing..why?

To quote myself (yuck!) 'Ubuntu is a good OS i love and OSX is a great OS i like' .. 'Canonical has had to do in five years what Apple had to do in ten'.

if we ignore the good points of other OS's we end up ignorant. there is value in neutrality. impartial thought.


Bless you!  You are just what this thread needs.  You brought up a bunch of really interesting points from the perspective of a non-nerd.

> 
I want Ubuntu to 'win' , yes win, like it's a race.. but one that Windows perhaps won or thought they won a long time ago.


Why?  By having only one choice, or one overwhelmingly popular choice, the whole world loses out.  No one system is good at everything.  Let Apple have the people Apple has.  Apple has a significant niche, big enough to maintain a healthy community.  Carve a chunk out of the Windows masses.

> 
...Ask a non tech, a person new to computers, like my GF, I build PC's but she, God love her, gets stressed if something doesn't work with one click. OS X soothes her computer anxiety.  I told her about the security of the object layer (as i understand it) and showed her ' netstat -a ' on XP, instantly she told me to put Ubuntu on our desktop, but she never uses it, she only ever uses her MacBook.    ... so the question... What is Apple doing right? (that WE the FOSS community) and Canonical are doing wrong?


That's the difference between a Mac user and a Linux user.  Mac OS has 'netstat -a' too, but she has no idea that it's there or why she would want to use it, let alone even seeing the Terminal app even once.

Mac users buy computers the same way they buy iPhones and toasters.  It's an appliance.  They buy it and do things with it, and that's it.  They don't want to have to know about the junk underneath it, they just want it to work.

Linux used to be only for people who had tape on their glasses, as a cushion so they didn't wreck your nose.  No tape on your glasses, then go use Windows.  No glasses, well you should either buy a Mac or not even own a computer.

The "stigma" is still there, because a lot of us still have tape on our glasses.  We insist on showing people what's behind the UI because we're proud of it.  We're showing these details to somebody who neither knows nor cares what "UI" means.

The majority of people in the world get by without computers.  At all.  A few years ago, I could have said with certainty that the majority of people in the world had never SEEN a computer.

The majority of skilled jobs in the world do not directly deal with computers.

The vastly overwhelming number of jobs in the world which require computer skills do not require intimate "under the UI" knowledge of how a computer works.  They do not require the understanding of what a UI is.

Go to Ubuntu.com and you'll see "cloud" computing on the front page.  I use the cloud, most of the people reading this probably know what it is to some extent, but why should your girlfriend even care?  The intro-to-the-intro page on Ubuntu.com is already talking over her head.

What's the cloud?  What's encryption?  Why do I need to know this stuff in order to check my email or go to facebook?

> 
Windows is social often stable...


That's another big thing for most people.  The only way they want to stand out is by being more pampered than somebody else.  Even then they want to associate with people who are equally pampered.  Linux does not pamper.  Even Microsoft hides the details that Linux users take pride in, and Apple just ignores the existence of the nerd tools that are already there, but not interesting unless you're a nerd.

Personally, I would not buy a Mac unless it had a full set of nerd necessities.  It does.  It also has a full set of the stuff that makes people buy Macs.

A few years ago, my 70-something dad called me up because he wanted to buy a computer.  They were $300 at Wal-mart.  I live 1000 miles away.  They don't know anybody who even has a computer, let alone somebody who can fix it.  They're on a fixed income.

So what do you do when that happens?

I've been using Linux for well over a decade.  I'm not supporting that over a 1000 mile telephone call, when they don't even know what a mouse is.  I'm not installing Windows, that's an even worse support nightmare.  I bought them an iMac.  Mailed it to them.  They loved it.

> 
peace

Dava

That's the key, right?

### Post by SeijiSensei on 2011-07-09
> **1clue said:**
> WI have a tendency to lay blame to unethical practices by Bill Gates in a lot of my discussions -- whether he deserves it or not -- and in this particular case I would claim that the whole PC-DOS/MS-DOS debacle had something to do with an initial boost.

And you'd be wrong.

As some who worked with the original two-floppy IBM PC in an academic setting, I'd have to say the reason the PC, and by association DOS, became the dominant player in the "microcomputer" marketplace was the name IBM.  Suddenly lots of large organizations felt comfortable using a new piece of technology knowing that a large, familiar corporation was there to provide support.  

Microsoft has engaged in a variety of shady business practices to maintain its monopoly on the desktop, but that all came later.  What made the PC a success was the name IBM.

Remember that an IBM PC cost about $5,000 when it first appeared.  Businesses and universities bought PCs, not ordinary people.  My first PC was an IBM XT clone made by Tandy with a 10 MB hard drive.  It cost us about $3,000 and was considered a bargain at the price.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
> **pedrotuga said:**
> Well, I agree with everything you said. What you probably don't get it's that it doesn't go against anything I said.
A software company is in business to sell its product, fine. If I take copy a software I bought at home, the created copy is NOT their product. They don't have the right to decide that it is just because it's better for their business. If they can't find a sustainable business model why fail to see how that is my problem in any way.


This is the keystone of your entire argument, and it is based on a false statement.

You do not own the software.  Depending on the place you live and the laws there, you may not even own the CD/floppy/tape/whatever media that the software was distributed on, or the printed documentation that came with it either.  You surely do not own the knowledge contained in the printed documentation since that has been protected for longer than software has.

I'll give you a 20+ year window.  Find me ANY piece of off-the-shelf commercial software made on or after January 1, 1990 which did not come with a license agreement stating that what you purchased was not ownership of the software but the right to use it under some fairly specific circumstances, including how many working copies you could have, how many systems it could be installed on and/or how many people could use it.

You did not buy software.  You bought a license to use it.  Probably for personal use only, probably only on one computer, and probably for only one user total.

When you received your software in physical form, it came with a CD containing the executable.  That CD had a sticker on it so that you couldn't open it without looking at that sticker.  The sticker said the equivalent of, "by opening this package you implicitly agree to the terms of the license agreement."

When you received the software in digital form, before you could get to the executable link, you had to agree to the terms of use on that web form.

Notice the phrases "terms of use" and "license agreement."  It does not say "purchase agreement" anywhere.

Surely you have read at least the first paragraph of at least one license agreement in your life?  If not, then I can assure you that ignorance of the law does not excuse you from the requirement of following it.

Now here's another exercise for you:  Find ANY Open Source license where it says you own the software, or that you are allowed to use it outside of its terms.  Find ANY Open Source license where it does not specify what sort of use you are allowed to use that software for.

> 
...
No, that is not extremely false. I can't see how is false in any way. If you think it's false I would suggest you explain why instead of freely accusing me of lying. Otherwise, the above post has zero value to this discussion.


See above.

> 
...
But I don't really agree with other points:

* gnome do and kupfer might be behind quicksilver, but in my opinion deskbar-applet beats any other easily. It has a state of the art plugin system, it integrates with indexing services on the gnome desktop and with many applications. And it's open which means that you can make it do things that it doesn't yet. Pity it is discontinued :(


Maybe if the only argument were quicksilver, this might warrant further investigation.  But it doesn't.  Productivity of a workstation depends on seamless integration of all aspects.

> 
* Simply admiting text mate is superior sounds like a very biased opinion. There are MANY text editors out there, I use a handful of them on windows and linux, they impress me in different ways. I seriously doubt text-mate is superior to gedit, scribes, notepad++, pspad, vim, emacs, eclipse, etc. in all aspects.
You can argue that it has the features you like, but that's personal preference. Does it have any characteristic that sets it at a different level than the others?


I have used vim as a programming editor for as long as I have used Linux, which is since before Bill Clinton became President for the first time.  I've used gedit, vim, eclipse and a lot of other developer editors, most of them tied to some IDE.  

The first editor to win me over from Vim as a primary editor is Eclipse.  The IntelliSense for navigating through code made all the rest of the inconveniences worthwhile.  I still shelled back to vim though for global replacements, regular expression searches and a few other things, and once I was there I generally stayed there until something in Eclipse was more convenient.

TextMate has more convenient regex searches than Vim, and better searching tools, and more convenient file management.  It gets rid of the IDE tools like compiling and debugging, which is both an advantage and a disadvantage.  Since I now work with languages which have dependency injection and runtime-modified code, the debuggers I've seen are essentially useless and the IntelliSense is irrelevant since it will never work anyway.

TextMate focuses on simply being a really good editor, and as such (like vim) the developers could do a really outstanding job.  TextMate does almost everything with key combinations, but allows the mouse as well, which vim doesn't really do well even with external aid.  The key combinations are extremely convenient for a programmer, and they are organized rationally so that even if you don't know the combination you might be able to figure it out with a good guess.

> 
* battery life. Not sure is OS related. Have you installed windows or linux on your mac? Is the autonomy significantly shorter?


Not exactly OS related, but since the hardware comes with Mac OS on it it sort of does.  As well, the Linux forum posts I've seen regarding Linux on my hardware suggest that Linux does not do nearly as well with power management, getting less than 3 hours from the same batteries.

> 
As you can see from my reply, I don't find OS X significantly more suited for a developer. I do think linux has other advantages:
 - choice of desktop, filebrowser, window manager, etc
 - generally more customizable
 - free as in free beer
 - total control of what you want to run on a work session
... etc.

Mostly true, except the "free as in free beer."  If you were to start reading license agreements, and then start reading about the history of Open Source, and the outlooks of the initial players** EDIT:  And what they do for a living -- mostly coding software for money**, the whole idea of Open Source is that eventually everyone who uses it contributes something significant toward Open Source.  In other words, you don't get off scott free.

Nobody makes a big deal out of it, because sometimes it takes years for somebody to decide what contribution to make.  Sometimes the community is much better off for waiting, because some outstanding products have come from all sorts of places and took their time to do it right.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
> **forrestcupp said:**
> I have an M-Audio FastTrack USB sound card used for recording. I tried plugging it in and setting up Jack Audio to use it to record into Ardour. After a lot of screwing around with Jack and everything else, I finally found the FastTrack, but I never actually got it to work in real time, or hardly even at all.

In Windows and Mac, I can just plug it in, install its ASIO drivers, and it just works beautifully in real time without having to labor through any settings manually. I know that people have had success with getting this sound card to work in Linux, but this is just one example of why Windows and Mac are more popular, especially for specialized uses.

+10.

My current Linux hardware was bleeding edge when I got it.  I have lots of hardware from companies that typically support Linux, but the driver support hadn't happened yet, and in some cases still hasn't.

[LIST=1]
[*]nVidia Corporation GT200 [GeForce GTX 260].  I can always get it working, but not necessarily with the nvidia driver and generally there's something broken about it.  Any distro change and many upgrades and I have to monkey with it.  Why?
[*]Conexant Systems, Inc. CX23887/8 PCIe Broadcast Audio and Video Decoder with 3D Comb.  Never worked.  Why?
[*]Creative Labs SB X-Fi.  $300 sound card, has an external control panel and while I get an audio out port the control panel has never worked and getting the audio itself to work at all was a massive PITA.  Why?
[*]Blu-ray writer.  Works fine as a DVD burner, doesn't play or burn blu-ray format unless it's a raw disk copy.  Why?  (OK this one is DRM but a consumer doesn't care, they just want to be able to stick their disk in and hit play)
[*]Linksys WUSB600N Dual-Band Wireless-N USB Network Adapter.  Did not work for over a year.  Magically worked when somebody got the driver for it into Ubuntu.
[*]Logitech, Inc. QuickCam Orbit/Sphere AF.  USB WebCam with servos to point it where you want.  After much PITA, I got the camera to work but the pointing part never worked.  Why?
[/LIST]

Every one of these things is a brand that is known to work well with Linux, and most have one driver that "works with everything" but didn't work for me.

Canon MF8050 all-in-one color laser printer.  Why should I as an end user need to download source from a site on another continent, compile it and make it go on my system?  That's what I had to resort to.  Finally they got the thing supported in Canonical.  Half the features don't work but at least I can print.

As a Linux user I can take some of these things philosophically.  And since I have a Mac right here, I can say that both USB devices I listed just plugged in and worked completely, and the printer had a simple driver install (the mac knew where to look) and it was going no problem.

You want to know why OS X is more popular?  Start there.

### Post by fontis on 2011-07-09
> **Madspyman said:**
> 

Linux IMO as an OS is every bit as good as OS X, what it lacks is mainstream software. If Linux had full developer support from companies like Adobe, and the likes it'd be offered on more PC's over Windows. The security plus the price point coupled with the software compatibility would finally give consumers a stronger third option, that would hurt Apple. Two stones with one bird. 

It doesn't look like it'll ever happen though. So until then OS X will remain more popular.

For Linux to get support from mainstream heavyweights, Linux needs to be more centralized around some form of guidelines. Which will never happen because the general atmosphere in the world and in this thread are against such a thing. So what Linux distro should developers choose from? Will THAT distro give them the reach they want or is it unpopular in 2months time? What packages do they code for? Are they the "mainstream" so we don't risk hefty random changes?

And the list goes on. 
For the record, more software "heavyweights" support Windows, and did so even more in the past. They turned more into Mac software after Mac persistently kept their system the same (post OS X). And even still, the support is not nearly the same.

If I was a developer, honestly, if I wasn't developing some target-specific super nerd application then I wouldn't bother. And even in that case, I'd consider it heavily. The time needed to invest and produce some heavyweight app is better spent coding for Win, or OS X. They have far more users and easier to distinguish what packaging system to use.

Want that to change? Increase the amount of users using Linux, and centralize stuff.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
Sorry about the rant.

People go on and on about what's intolerable about Mac OS.  I think half of them think those things are intolerable because they can't afford a Mac, or maybe they can't justify one in their mind.  FWIW my Linux box cost more than the 17" MacBook Pro did.  Not trying to brag, just saying that to me I could care less if I have to spend a few hundred bucks on an operating system and some software.

I bought the Linux box specifically to put Linux on it, I googled the devices for Linux support right there in the store (and got "supported using xxx driver") and bought them thinking it would be a snap.  I stuck to brands I knew would work, and generally chose models based on what I wanted by reading the box after somebody on a Linux site said there were no red flags.

FWIW if a Windows or Mac user had even one part of his printer not print it would be back in the box and returned to the store.  If their sound card's control panel had one dial act funny, same thing.  We all know that's true.

I'm a true believer in Open Source.  I don't belong to any OS projects, but I have submitted code to several.  I also believe in commercial software, and I believe that they can and should work in collaboration.

I've gone to software symposia, where you spend a few thousand dollars for a few days of education.  I've talked to a whole lot of people who lead Open Source projects.  Guess what?  They were giving the talks, and they generally talk about experiences with people who hired them to write software.  Those guys don't have problems mixing commercial software with Open Source either.

These problems some of you have with Mac OS are considered features by a good share of the Apple customers who bought them.  There is no way anyone would call what I posted a couple posts back to be a feature.

I think some of you heard "free software" and then stopped listening.  Most businesses which use Open Source accept that the system in question will actually cost more over the life of the system, because finding somebody who understands the maintenance, and sitting through problems, costs much more than a Windows Enterprise Server license.

Free software is not about the amount of money you spend on it.

### Post by steptotheedge on 2011-07-09
The reason is because Linux is confusing and lacks basic software that majority of people are looking for.

Example: Microsoft Windows has MSPaint. A great little program that one can quickly use to make a quick edit to a graphic file.

Linux lacks basic programs like this. I am not aware of any such program that runs on Linux, which can draw straight lines, draw squares, and has an undo feature.
Before anyone says GNU Paint. GNU paint is junk and does not have an undo feature!
So, as you can see, Linux is not really mature at all. (do not get technical with kernel definition!)

Linux also lacks quality video editing software. Cinderella looks ok, but when I installed it (back when I was noob) using Ubuntu Software Centre, it installed, but failed to launch without giving any error message, as a package I had that it depends on was broken. I only found out when trying to launch it through terminal.
What chance does a normal person have figuring that out, or even know what a dependency is? See, people just want **** to work, Linux always has problems.


Another reason is that, what is Linux? When someone with "Linux" switches on their computer, they don't see "Linux"... they see, "Ubuntu", or "Debian" or something like that. How confusing.

Installing software is also really time consuming and annoying. Can you imagine the normal person downloading source code and then compiling it?
... and Linux developers still ride the 'user knows everything' mentality.
How many times I have ./configure to only get an error message saying something that makes no sense to me.


Another reason: Linux is not in retail stores.


Linux is a computer enthusiast operating system, like an excotic car is to a car enthusiasts. As much we love it, and love digging into the operating system. People just want to use their computers.

[http://linux-faq.org/eng/index.html](http://linux-faq.org/eng/index.html)
This was very inspiring. I suspect written by a Mac user. lol


Just a sub-note. I've only signed upto this forum, because I am about to write a post asking if anyone knows good MTA software for Linux? I currently run a server and the mail server runs in a virtualized Windows environment... and I would love to get rid of it, but there just doesn't seem to be any comparable MTA software for Linux. So there you go, even a power user using Windows users can't use Linux for his needs.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-09
> **wolfen69 said:**
> I might as well use windows or mac then. I like linux the way it is, and don't care how many people use it.

+1.

While I don't exactly agree with this, I agree with the central point.  I could run a Windows Enterprise Server if I wanted to.  I put Linux on it instead.  Not because Linux doesn't cost anything.  Because I like Linux.

On the other hand, I DO care how many people use it.  I want the community to thrive, and I want to be part of it.

My biggest problem with this discussion is that everybody seems to think Linux needs to displace Mac OS, or Windows, or something.  That's wrong.

Linux is for the people who don't like Windows or Mac.  If they're happy with what Mac OS X is, or Windows, then they need to use Mac or Windows.  We don't want those guys here.  If I wanted something that looked like or worked like some other OS, I would be using that other OS.

I like the difference between Mac and Linux.  Why should my desktop look like theirs?  Why shouldn't I be able to use FVWM and tailor my computer to be something that no other system on the planet has?

It would be really neat to have a few things though:
[LIST=1]
[*]I would like to be able to buy cutting edge hardware and reasonably expect it to work.
[*]I would like to be able to stick a DVD or Blu-Ray in the drive and watch a movie without spending a couple weekends trying to configure the player.
[*]I would like more open document standards than we have now, and decent apps to use them.
[*]I would like every user on this forum to actually read an Open Source license from end to end.  I think a bunch of you would be really really surprised.  Trust me on this though, an Open Source license is generally easy to read and easy to understand, and not nearly as long as something from Microsoft or Oracle.
[/LIST]

All this thread comes out from a wrong assumption, it is not true that Linux is less popular than OSX.
I would say it is just the **opposite**.

OS market share analysys made by Gartner in April 2011:
[http://www.gartner.com/it/page.jsp?id=1654914](http://www.gartner.com/it/page.jsp?id=1654914)

[IMG]http://oi52.tinypic.com/a1tt29.jpg[/IMG]

....this thread is the heart of Ubuntu's coming of age.

home truths.

1. We are all so grateful that Ubuntu is free we never criticise ourselves. 

2. as 1clue said expecting compilation of code from an end user is absurd. (paraphrase)

3. This is an OS, not a beef hotpot, meaning you can't just throw anything in the mix and hope it will work. 

Programs who do not meet a set of standards that should be set by Canonical should not be allowed the 'privilege'  of being included in Ubuntu. yes if YOU the end user choose it, but Canonical need stability in brand protection by having stability in the included software. think Mac, would they risk their brand by unstable software?

4. we are still recovering from being 'the outcast'. Google is our popular friend who told people we were cool, and slowly there listening, but we still need time to recover.

5. we need organisation on the level of our competitors. we are new to this, we need advertising ON TV, partnerships with coding teams that last and grow deeper over the years, OEM reps to let OEM's know Ubuntu is not a Linux flavour of the month but a reliable long lasting competitor.

and finally, 6. being friendlier to non-techs, we all are i suppose but.. not really, how can they understand us? sudo root..is that a soft drink? who cares about the engine, i just wanna drive the car.

### Post by Syndicalist on 2011-07-10
I was reading that Linux and OSX might be under represented due to infected Windows computers showing up as multiple users. I have no idea how accurate that is, but the article about it is being passed around. 

From what I understand, Linux has a much greater following than OSX internationally and may be very significantly cutting into Microsoft's profits. 

OSX has very limited popularity in the most populous non-western countries due to it being hardware specific, more difficult to pirate, and cost-prohibitive.....a recycled machine with pirated XP is easy to come by. Pirated Windows 7 is equally easy, even to disable Windows genuine with a few simple clicks. OSX is a non-viable option for the majority of the earths population who cant afford it, and I think Windows genuine has given Linux a major popularity boost.

So, Linux has a greater desktop following than OSX, but not in the USA. I think having a Mac is a status symbol. 

Personally, I dislike the feel of Mac. The doc is nice enough looking but I think its clunkier than docky and less user friendly, and not nearly as nice as Cairo. Mac in general just feels less intuitive than Windows. It uses hardware more efficiently, but for the money its not that great of a deal. I think its one of those things you buy to be seen with, which is why you get a giant logo on the back of the laptops to let everyone know you are part of the privileged class who can afford one.

I think Linux is nicer looking, at least potentially, than Windows or Mac....The biggest downfalls is limited proprietary software, lack of popular well polished free alternatives for certain things like payroll, a bit of a gaming deficiency, and the fact that Ubuntu is buggy.

Personally I think Unity was a bad idea. I think some people feel like the community is being used to test a product intended for tablets, not desktops. It wouldnt be well tested as a niche product though, so they make it the default.....maybe that is just an incorrect conspiracy theory, but some people are talking like that.

There was a great performance boost with the latest kernel, but lots of overheating and freezing.

I have noticed that a lot of downstream derivatives of Ubuntu, like Pinguy, are having stability issues in their latest releases. Moves like this might hurt Linux's popularity if the bugs are not ironed out. I also think its unlikely that people will go back to Ubuntu with Unity if their Ubuntu derivatives become unstable. They would probably distro hop away from the perceived source of the problem.



However, I am still optimistic. We know Linux is going to kill Windows in the Tablet market. If we can keep current performance or better and iron out the bugs without overheating, I think Linux can become popular on the desktop.


I think that Meego, a true GNU-Linux distro, might end up introducing proprietary software and gaming to the Linux desktop. I dont think people should give up on the desktop, and claims that the desktop is going anywhere are highly overrated. People like powerful workstations with lots of bells and whistles. A hand held device will have a hard time competing with something capable of sucking 100x as much power from your wall when it comes to resource hungry activities like gaming.

---

When I started using Linux, I Spent about a year distro hopping, before I found a distro that i felt comfortable in.

currently my 2 favourite distros are Arch and Ubuntu, I haven't used Arch in a while, the rolling release nature of it would often break my system, and a lot of arch users would get snooty if i asked for help in the forum saying "i shouldn't use linux if i dont understand it", i just didnt expect a routine update to cause X to freeze up every time it started. I did use Arch for about 18months, as I liked the minimalistic approach, that you get pretty much nothing and only install the bits you want. When I started uni I needed my pc to be reliable rather than fast, I just stuck full ubuntu on it as it pretty much comes OTB with almost everything I needed.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-10
> **pedrotuga said:**
> For one ting, I don't. I use windows and a proprietary anti-virus at work but that's about it. Otherwise I don't use software that charges me to simply run it on my own computer. I find that whole concept retarded and extremely unethical. 


But you will claim ownership of software that does not belong to you.  Including GNU, which even though is on your computer does not belong to you.  You cannot do with it what you will, and the GPL is every bit as draconian as anything Microsoft ever came up with, only in a different way.

> 
I am aware that that is the keystone of my argument. In fact I do try to stress that when I get into such discussions as this one.

If it's a false statement... based the law of almost every country it is. But I do believe that such laws are unacceptable and have been for many years. I don't think I should be deprived from owning the bytes that make up a copy of a software if I bought it. That should be a basic right of a citizen.


If you had actually purchased the software, you would be right.  You didn't.  If you didn't write it yourself while not being paid to do so by somebody else, or if you did not pay somebody else to write software which by mutual agreement would belong to you afterward, then you do not own ANY software.  Period.

> 
Just to put it in comparison with other kind of products: Would you happily buy a shirt that would come with a license that would only allow you to use it on sundays? Would you think that that would be reasonable? I wouldn't.


If you're sitting at a restaurant and somebody who is eating alone gets up to go to the bathroom, leaving their jacket and maybe car keys on the table, what do you do?  Do you "find" a jacket and car keys and assume that they are yours, or do you leave them there for the rightful owner?

What if it's something that can't be traced?  They left $20 in cash on the table too.  Do you take the $20 simply because you can?

You KNOW the intent of the owner.  You knew it before you paid for it.  You intended to ignore what you know to be the agreement before you even went shopping.  FWIW Microsoft and a whole lot of other companies generally have more than one license arrangement for any piece of software.  Did you read through a few of them and see if there is a model that works for you?

> 
Jokes aside, I surely do own the knowledge, once it's in my head I own it and nothing can change that. They may say that I don't but that changes nothing really. When the software industry realizes this, it will undoubtedly find ways of being profit without imposing on everyone non-sense beasts such as copyright.


You cannot claim that research as your own.  You cannot claim ownership of the knowledge.  You can claim to understand it, but it was not your idea to begin with.  You may understand Newton's laws of motion but they are still Newton's laws, not yours.

If what you're saying is true, then I can take the deed for my house and copy it as many times as I want, and then any recipient of that document owns all of or part of my house.  It doesn't work that way.

If you're going down a highway, your tax dollars paid for that highway but you do not own the highway.  You can't tear it up and put a house there.  If it's a toll road, you may put hundreds of dollars into that toll road over the span of years, but you have only purchased the right to use it for a limited time, not right of ownership.

If you rented a DVD you have purchased the right to watch it for a limited period of time, you did NOT purchase the right to make copies.

> 
Getting a bit more on topic... Ok, looks like using apple products did add value and a production increment at your company. Fair enough.
I still think that is not allways the case.


Of course it's not.  Nobody would say something like that.  Nobody could correctly claim that any single piece of software would improve productivity for everyone.

> 
As for text editors...
I'm happy with eclipse/netbeans + gedit (or notepad++), occasionally I use vim (I run gvim on windows too) If i need to open really large files. I owned a mac for a couple of weeks and I did install ubuntu on it for this very reason.
But of course, that depends, for a java programmer like myself, it's not really fair to compare eclipse or netbeans to textmate.

Why not?  Java uses aspect-oriented programming in some cases, whether you deal with it or not.  Do you use Spring components?  I develop in Java and groovy/grails.  My arguments are directly related to that.  So yes, I took TextMate head to head with Eclipse and STS and come out on top with TextMate.  And Versions, which is the only SVN client I've found that's better than what comes with Eclipse.

**Edit:  And when we switched, I insisted that STS would beat out TextMate.  I insisted that a Linux box made an ideal developer's platform, and I was wrong.  For my case, that is.**

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-10
OK I really have enough for another post.

@pedrotuga,

The thing you need to understand is that Mac users can ALSO use STS and Eclipse and vim and gimp and Inkscape.  It's no harder to use on a Mac than it is on Linux.  When you buy a Mac, you simply pay extra for the ability to put certain commercial software on a platform that already runs everything you need to develop Java.

You lose out on a whole lot of freedom to choose what goes on under the covers, but Ubuntu doesn't really give you a lot of choice there anyway, they install a complete system for you by default.  You lose out on cool customization features, and you have to put up with Apple's draconian software model on the commercial side of it, but some people would call that a fair trade.  They don't stop you from putting Open Source on there, and if you sign the non-disclosure agreement you can even get source code for the whole thing.  Including all the proprietary stuff.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-10
How about this one?

The software is not on your computer at all.  It's hosted somewhere, you sign up and pay a fee and then you can use that software.

Realistically, that's a much better analogy of what really happens when you "buy" software.

ANY software license can be revoked due to noncompliance.  That goes for Microsoft, Apple, and GNU.

If your GNU license gets revoked, you basically lose the right to use anything licensed under GNU.

If you don't strictly comply with the GNU software license agreement, then all sorts of badness can happen.  For example, commercial software can get sucked into a GNU license if you statically link to it from the commercial software.  Richard Stallman and GNU makes Bill Gates and Microsoft look like a priest in a monastery.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2011-07-10

What I get with a Macbook Pro is extremely long battery life, a power button flush with the case but not difficult to press, a magnetic cord (very important for clutzes like me), speakers that aren't tinny, a solidly built case, easy-to-press keys that don't make loud clacking noises, fully featured multi-touch touchpad, and great customer service at the Genius Bar (yes, they did replace my failed graphics card politely, quickly, and for free even though my Apple Care had expired).

There are definitely some non-Macbook Pro laptops that have good speakers. And there are others that have long battery life. And some others that have nice keyboards. And others that support multi-touch. And others that have solid construction. I think what's nice about Macbook Pros is that they take the best of everything and put it into one consistently good package. And I cannot stress enough how important it is for me to have the MagSafe cord. I have tripped over and broken many power cords in my day. Maybe ya'll are coordinated, but I am not!

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-10
> **1clue said:**
> How about this one?

The software is not on your computer at all.  It's hosted somewhere, you sign up and pay a fee and then you can use that software.

Realistically, that's a much better analogy of what really happens when you "buy" software.

ANY software license can be revoked due to noncompliance.  That goes for Microsoft, Apple, and GNU.

If your GNU license gets revoked, you basically lose the right to use anything licensed under GNU.

If you don't strictly comply with the GNU software license agreement, then all sorts of badness can happen.  For example, commercial software can get sucked into a GNU license if you statically link to it from the commercial software.  Richard Stallman and GNU makes Bill Gates and Microsoft look like a priest in a monastery.

This is in fact a very serious risk with both Microsoft and Apple software but not a risk at all with GNU. In the Microsoft case we have product activation and validation which in fact does allow Microsoft to remotely disable or cripple Windows XP and later versions of Windows for **allegedly** violating the license. Furthermore patching Windows to disable the ability of Microsoft to remotely disable or cripple Windows is a violation of the terms of the EULA. In the Apple case the situation is even worse. Apple reserves the right to remotely remove or disable software on IOS devices without even an allegation of an EULA violation on the part of the end user. Again a patch to IOS to disable Apple's ability to disable or remove software is a violation of the terms of the EULA. 

Contrast this with the GNU case. Sure one can distribute software under the GPL and add this kind of remote disabling capability, but the end user can remove or cause to be removed the "malicious features", as RMS likes to call DRM, and remain in perfect compliance with the GPL. Furthermore the changes in GPL v3 are designed to provide additional protections to end users against this very threat. 

Then there comes the question of GPL violations. Can someone please explain how an end user can violate the GPL without distributing or conveying the software?

---

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-10
> **KiwiNZ said:**
> If you do not like the terms and conditions set down by a seller/creator of a piece of software do not buy and or use it, that is the rights and freedom you have. You cannot unilaterally change the terms and conditions the seller/creator has set in their license.

This assumes of course that the EULA, or parts thereof are actually legally enforceable and do not contradict the legislation in your jurisdiction. More often than not EULAs have run afoul of local laws or there are provisions in Copyright Legislation that give rights to an end user that are not present in the EULA.

@1clue
You missed my point once again.
Read carefully, the software authorship is one thing, a copy's ownership is a totally different thing. All the licenses created by the the Free Software Foundation are very clear stating that you OWN the copy got and have four basic freedoms:
> 
The freedom to run the program, for any purpose (freedom 0).
The freedom to study how the program works, and change it to make it do what you wish (freedom 1). Access to the source code is a precondition for this.
The freedom to redistribute copies so you can help your neighbor (freedom 2).
The freedom to distribute copies of your modified versions to others (freedom 3). By doing this you can give the whole community a chance to benefit from your changes. Access to the source code is a precondition for this.


Nowhere in the GPL is stated that you can take credit for something that you didn't do.

You fall into a similar fallacy when you touch the subject of knowledge. Sorry, what I have in my head is MINE, it will ever be. I don't need to violate anyone's authorship nor steal credit from anyone to own knowledge. That's a whole different thing and I am not defending it, at all.
In my opinion you guys are mixing up ownership and authorship. They do not apply to the same subject. That's the core idea of this discussion.

The example of hosting an application in a server is a good example of a valid profitable possibility. Charging for a service.
Distributing a copy and limit its usage to ridiculous levels is only possible due to a whole set up laws that exist for that specific purpose.

> have you ever tried some ibm / lenovo thinkpads?
have all the characteristics you described from a lot of years.
Big elephant in the room!
Do you still think the cool factor is not the main reason?
Thinkpads are quite common among large consultant companies and older developers. I won't draw any conclusion on that myself.

### Post by fireflower on 2011-07-10
I love Ubuntu. I wish it would take over the world. But I don't think it should right now because it is far from perfect, and I think its less popular than OSX for the same reason.

First, I just up/downgraded from 11.04 to 10.04 because 11.04 is slower (objective fact) and Unity is for netbooks (debatable but a reasoned opinion). I hear 11.10 won't even have gnome included, and that makes me wonder just what direction Ubuntu is going in. Is it still supposed to be an easy-to-use light-on-resources OS when Unity was a big slowdown and indecipherable (at least to me)?

Second, both times I've installed Ubuntu (10.10 almost a year ago, then 10.04 today) I've had major problems. If I didn't have a second computer to turn to, I would never have figured it out. Ubuntu doesn't come with wireless internet support, a huge problem if you have no wired internet available and trying to figure out how to make it install. Speaking of, it doesn't just install, there is always some bizarre problem or other requiring internet access to find a solution to.

A year ago when installing from a CD, I had to go into terminal (which was terrifying, as I had never used linux before) to manually make grub do a basic install. This afternoon, I had to have my boyfriend search the internet to problem-solve the "lol not installing from a USB stick either nub" errors I was getting. There is no excuse for a second-patch version of a release installer giving a user a blank screen with a command line. Or, after doing an internet search to get past that, having the same installer never get past the "loading" screen. That solution irked me even more: I had to disable a floppy disc drive I didn't have in the BIOS.

Again, this is nothing fancy. This is a clean, single partition install, to a pre-patched, years-old version. The most basic of the basic. And even so, there were problems no non-techie could solve. What about actually using the operating system? An Ubuntu neophyte shouldn't be expected to download an extra program from the internet using terminal to use the internet. A "human being" shouldn't have to have a second computer running to get a copy of a program that will allow Windows wireless drivers to work on her Ubuntu computer. To a non-techie, these kind of shenanigans approach unusability.

Really this isn't about the Cult of Mac. You don't have to disparage Mac users to see why Ubuntu isn't as popular. You have to be a techie to install or use Ubuntu. If you're not, you'll install something that you can install. This is sort of a question of "why don't fish breathe air?" The real question is do techies use OSX, and why? After all there are fish that can breathe air.

### Post by ScionicSpectre on 2011-07-10
It's Adobe. Actually, if you stick to GNOME or KDE applications, they tend to be very coherent and friendly to use, even in comparison to OS X. Having a menubar can sometimes drastically change the look of an application, though.

There are a few technical hurdles in GTK and GNOME that haven't been jumped yet, though, that make the handling and look of the desktops based on GNOME feel a bit old-school (lack of antialiased menus and window borders, for example).

Things are getting better all the time- a lot of development versions of big applications are a lot better than what's available on Ubuntu now. I think if you look at what Linux was like in 2008 compared to now, you'll see a ton of improvement.

But honestly, the developers of KDE and GNOME have different ideas about usability than Apple does. There are a lot of areas where we do better, whereas they might just do glam.

I think it's obvious that the commercial presence of Apple is the main reason OS X is more popular than Linux. Even if we have a lot of designers and advertisers who are trying to help promote Linux, the majority of people promoting open source aren't really well educated in that sort of thing. So it seems geeky and nerdy since it's perceived a niche thing.

I can guarantee that the Linux kernel is in more peoples' lives than OS X, though, despite the lack of brand popularity.

> 
@1clue
I don't work dependency injection nor with AOP. Therefore I can't say much about text-mate's features to support such paradigms. But I'm confused... does text-mate have any code completion features like eclipse?

On the SVN topic, allow me to fully disagree, even tortoiseSVN is easily superior to eclipse's client in my opinion. What other clients are you refering to?

Re:  TextMate.  It's an editor, not an IDE.  AOP and similar have essentially negated the benefit of Eclipse's IntelliSense.  I understand that STS does better with it, but it's still a long way off.

The Eclipse "code completion" is a macro completion, and TextMate has some of that.  It does not complete the method name you're typing in, but it will surround your highlighted code with a try/catch or for loop.  Some of that I miss occasionally, STS and Eclipse have a better feature with respects to that, as long as the thing you're trying to complete isn't injected later.

Part of the problem there is that if a method is injected at runtime, completion can't work because at compile time it doesn't exist.  So you can't rely on the feature to work correctly when you're coding, so it just as well not be there.

TextMate's superiority to Eclipse comes mostly with respect to it NOT being an IDE.  Every now and then Eclipse/STS (same basic IDE for those of you who don't know, but STS has better Spring integration.) loses its brain and you spend half a day or more getting all your projects working together again.  If it's a pure Java project (or C or whatever) then it's OK because Eclipse can be your entire build tool.  If it's groovy/grails, then you're probably working on the command line for much of your development process, and Eclipse losing its brain and showing errors all over can only be a distraction.

Since much of groovy is injected, almost no file is without some sort of red mark.  STS/Eclipse is worthless in that case, or worse than worthless.  It's a hindrance because it insists that you need to fix something which is not broken before you can continue.

TextMate is what I consider to be the next Vim.  Vim has plugins which aid developers, main features designed around source code development, but it does not try to compile your code for you.  It does not try to manage your source code repository.  It just tries to be a really good editor.

Re:  SVN.
Personally I can't see any benefit to Tortoise SVN at all, so we'll just have to agree to disagree on that.

---

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-10
> **1clue said:**
> What are you talking about?  If you signed up on a web site to be able to use a service, then you have leased a service.  You don't own anything, you have the right to use the service by their terms but that's it.  That's my point. 

If one uses Microsoft Windows or IOS it is like subscribing to a Website, and one can easily loose control of one's data. If one uses FLOSS this risk is not present.  



> **1clue said:**
> Sure.  By suing an Open Source firm to get control of the code, the way SCO did.  By compiling proprietary code which statically links to GNU code. 

This is incorrect on both counts. Suing someone for copyright or patent infringement does not invalidate your rights under the GPL. Compiling proprietary code which statically links to GNU code does not violate the GPL. What does violate the GPL is to convey or distribute that resulting derived work under a license other that the GPL. This is in fact one of the most common misconceptions about the GPL 

> **1clue said:**
> Maybe you should start by actually reading the GPL instead of taking somebody's word for it.  There is a substantial list of ways you can violate the GPL, and some of them can be done by an "end user." 

I actually have read the GPL. I have also read the EULAs for many propriety software products including Windows 7, Vista, XP etc. even Windows 3.1. I am also familiar with the relevant sections of Canada's Copyright Law. I must also add that if many of the supporters and proponents of propriety software actually read the EULAs the number of GNU / Linux uses would actually skyrocket. How many people actually know that lending a computer with Windows 7 Home Premium is actually a violation of the Windows 7 Home Premium EULA. Please read the EULA before allowing those relatives that are visiting to use your Windows 7 Home Premium computer to check their email. 

> **1clue said:**
> As a general rule, they are enforceable completely, or they would no longer exist.  I'm dying to hear of any court case that actually supports what you're talking about here.

Not true. There are many examples. For example when the European Union forced Microsoft to change certain of its patent licensing terms to be compatible with GPL v3. The SAMBA project actually makes use of these provisions.

### Post by aysiu on 2011-07-10
> **Dr. C said:**
> Section 8 from [http://download.microsoft.com/Documents/UseTerms/Windows%207_Home%20Premium_English_a0cdb148-2381-47cd-bfc9-9b9102e0cb28.pdf]("http://download.microsoft.com/Documents/UseTerms/Windows%207_Home%20Premium_English_a0cdb148-2381-47cd-bfc9-9b9102e0cb28.pdf")
> **SCOPE OF LICENSE**. The software is licensed, not sold. This agreement only gives you some rights 
to use the features included in the software edition you licensed. Microsoft reserves all other rights. 
Unless applicable law gives you more rights despite this limitation, you may use the software only as 
expressly permitted in this agreement. In doing so, you must comply with any technical limitations in 
the software that only allow you to use it in certain ways. You may not 
· work around any technical limitations in the software; 
· reverse engineer, decompile or disassemble the software, except and only to the extent that 
applicable law expressly permits, despite this limitation; 
· use components of the software to run applications not running on the software; 
· make more copies of the software than specified in this agreement or allowed by applicable law, 
despite this limitation; 
· publish the software for others to copy; 
· rent, lease or lend the software; or 
· use the software for commercial software hosting services. 
 You can lend your computer, just not the software. The license is per computer. You can't say "Here, just borrow my Windows 7 and install it on your computer." But once Windows 7 is installed on your computer, you can lend the computer itself.

Or perhaps you missed section 2? > **One Copy per Computer.** Except as allowed in Section 2 (b) below, you may install one copy of the software on one computer. That computer is the &#8220;licensed computer.&#8221;

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-10
No. When one lends the computer with the software installed one also also lends the software that is on it. Section 2 is about installing the software on another computer. 

Microsoft is actually offering a rental rights upgrade to allow lending but only on certain enterprise and business versions not on home versions of Windows. [https://partner.microsoft.com/40104043]("https://partner.microsoft.com/40104043")

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2011-07-10

Microsoft is actually offering a rental rights upgrade to allow lending but only on certain enterprise and business versions not home versions of Windows. [https://partner.microsoft.com/40104043](https://partner.microsoft.com/40104043)

If you are lending your PC  the software is going with it, you are not installing it on a subsequent device, it's the same device and install.

You of course cannot install a copy on second or third device unless you have a multi licence copy.

---

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-10
> **KiwiNZ said:**
> If you are lending your PC  the software is going with it, you are not installing it on a subsequent device, it's the same device and install.

You of course cannot install a copy on second or third device unless you have a multi licence copy.

That is section 2 of the EULA. Section 8 deals with lending. It is not "like a book" licensing as was the case with Windows 3.1 where lending was allowed. There is a reason why Microsoft is offering rental rights. It is so those who need to lend, Internet Cafes, Hotels etc. can be in compliance.

---

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2011-07-10
> **Dr. C said:**
> That is section 2 of the EULA. Section 8 deals with lending. It is not "like a book" licensing as was the case with Windows 3.1 where lending was allowed. There is a reason why Microsoft is offering rental rights. It is so those who need to lend, Internet Cafes, Hotels etc. can be in compliance.

You are misinterpreting that section. You stated this " lending a computer with Windows 7 Home Premium"

Section 8 refers to lending the software only, meaning you cannot install it a PC then lend your copy of the install disc so that the OS is also installed on the borrowers PC.

If you lend a PC with the OS installed you are meeting the One install allowance under teh EULA.

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-10
> **KiwiNZ said:**
> You are misinterpreting that section. You stated this " lending a computer with Windows 7 Home Premium"

Section 8 refers to lending the software only, meaning you cannot install it a PC then lend your copy of the install disc so that the OS is also installed on the borrowers PC.

If you lend a PC with the OS installed you are meeting the One install allowance under teh EULA.

Yes and that is in compliance with section 2 of the EULA but not with section 8 of the EULA. If one were to un-install Windows 7, then lend the computer and when one gets the computer back reinstall Windows 7 back on the same computer, then one would be in compliance with both section 2 and section 8 of the EULA; however if the borrower gets to use the Windows 7 software, they are also borrowing the installed software. Section 8 refers to the software, it does not refer only to the un-installed software.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-11
> **Dr. C said:**
> 
This is incorrect on both counts. Suing someone for copyright or patent infringement does not invalidate your rights under the GPL. Compiling proprietary code which statically links to GNU code does not violate the GPL. What does violate the GPL is to convey or distribute that resulting derived work under a license other that the GPL. This is in fact one of the most common misconceptions about the GPL 


Thanks.  For some reason I didn't know there was a v3 license.  I won't comment on that one until I've had ample time to study it.  And yes, I'm talking about a commercial work for a third-party sale.

However:
[http://www.gnu.org/licenses/old-licenses/gpl-2.0.html](http://www.gnu.org/licenses/old-licenses/gpl-2.0.html)

Preamble, third paragraph:  To protect your rights, we need to make restrictions that forbid anyone to deny you these rights or to ask you to surrender the rights. These restrictions translate to certain responsibilities for you if you distribute copies of the software, or if you modify it.

Me:  By trying to deny rights to GPL software you have broken that clause and several others.  You at least seem to have read one or more license agreements before.

Terms and conditions, part 2b:  You must cause any work that you distribute or publish, that in whole or in part contains or is derived from the Program or any part thereof, to be licensed as a whole at no charge to all third parties under the terms of this License.

Me:  That is the toxic clause.  The entire part 2 is also interesting with respect to this topic but I won't quote the whole thing here.  You CAN dynamically link to an interface which could be implemented by non-free software, and then not distribute the GPL library with the commercial software.  This sort of thing happens with JDBC/ODBC drivers as an example.

While the intent of the V2 license may or may not be to infect corporate software, a whole lot of corporate lawyers insist it can be easily interpreted that way and as such is dangerous to flaunt.

> 
I actually have read the GPL. I have also read the EULAs for many propriety software products including Windows 7, Vista, XP etc. even Windows 3.1. I am also familiar with the relevant sections of Canada's Copyright Law. I must also add that if many of the supporters and proponents of propriety software actually read the EULAs the number of GNU / Linux uses would actually skyrocket. How many people actually know that lending a computer with Windows 7 Home Premium is actually a violation of the Windows 7 Home Premium EULA. Please read the EULA before allowing those relatives that are visiting to use your Windows 7 Home Premium computer to check their email. 


I think that if people read the Apache license or similar the Open Source licenses would skyrocket, but anything written under the v2 GPL is just plain scary.  I've read that one several times, and I stand by what I said.

> 
Not true. There are many examples. For example when the European Union forced Microsoft to change certain of its patent licensing terms to be compatible with GPL v3. The SAMBA project actually makes use of these provisions.

That just proves my point.  What the EU did was refuse to allow Microsoft to sell software in the EU unless they changed the terms.  If the terms were unenforcible or invalid then they wouldn't have forced Microsoft to change them.

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-11
If one is in the propriety software distribution business, then I agree the GPL can actually be scary, if one does not pay very close attention to the legal ramifications. If one is into DRM then GPL v3 is really very scary. The latter is the main reason I really like GPL v3, since I loathe DRM with a passion.  

On the other hand an end user be they an individual or a corporation has nothing to fear from the GPL. It is the common propriety licenses that are really scary in this case.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-11
@Dr. C,

For someone who actually reads agreements, you seem to have a fairly partisan way of interpreting them.  Maybe my interpretations go a bit in the opposite direction, who knows?

My company uses Windows for testing, because nearly all of our customers use Windows.  Microsoft seems to have a license for just about any application.  I know about the ones for developer testing, but surely there is one for rentals or just about any other legitimate business.

Just because one of their licenses forbids use as a rental doesn't mean all of them do.

[Edit:]
I've found the best way to deal with Windows licenses is contact Microsoft.  Tell them what you want to use it for, and they will point out the license that works best for that.  For testing it's actually very reasonable.

### Post by CraigPaleo on 2011-07-11
> **fireflower said:**
> 

Second, both times I've installed Ubuntu (10.10 almost a year ago, then 10.04 today) I've had major problems. If I didn't have a second computer to turn to, I would never have figured it out. Ubuntu doesn't come with wireless internet support, a huge problem if you have no wired internet available and trying to figure out how to make it install. Speaking of, it doesn't just install, there is always some bizarre problem or other requiring internet access to find a solution to.


I couldn't remember where I saw this but I've finally found it. [Pinguy OS]("http://sourceforge.net/projects/pinguy-os/files/Pinguy_OS_11.04/") has extra Wifi support. It's the only Ubuntu-based distro I could install on my mother's laptop that supported her wifi. Even the 10.04.2 version supports it. I don't know why it's not included in Ubuntu itself unless it has to do with being proprietary. You might want to give it a try.

### Post by santaslittlehelper on 2011-07-11

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-11
> **santaslittlehelper said:**
> Ubuntu got [Bug #1]("https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+bug/1") and I don't actually think it's meant as a joke?

What in your opinion would make Linux users happy? Why should all the moms and pops not get a fair chance to use Linux are you afraid the GPL will claim their souls before they discover the evil truth of it? :p

I don't think the GPL is inherently better or worse than any commercial license, but for some reason everyone thinks that Richard Stallman is the best thing since sliced bread.  He has an agenda just like anybody else, though, and while it benefits some of you it certainly does not benefit all of you.  Just saying that based on how social change works.

Personally I like the Apache license best, but that's just me.  My whole point for the licensing argument is that we should be aware of what it is that we're supporting.  There is a huge amount of misinformation about licenses, assumptions that are just plain wrong.  I had them too, before I figured out how incredibly easy it is to read an Open Source license.  They're not meant for lawyers, they're meant for regular people.  Go ahead and read a couple of them, GNU and Apache are good first and second licenses, and quite a bit different from each other so they give a good example of how broad the licenses get.

I don't think bug #1 was a joke, I just don't subscribe to it.  I don't want to "steal market share" in Linux.  I would love for Linux to grow and develop, and for some of the rough spots to be smoothed out, but I don't think we should try to entice people who are happy with something else that we will do what Mac or Windows do only better.  What we call Linux today is frankly better in every way than what we called Windows or Mac OS when I started using Linux.  Computing technology moves forward, for everyone.  That's a good thing.  Different communities move in different directions, based on what their populations find helpful.  That's good too.  Linux uses a different measure of success, a different weight to social pressure.  That's incredibly good.

Most importantly, Linux moves forward based on the needs of its users, instead of at the whim of some guy whose sole focus in the quarterly profit for his company.  I think a whole lot of newer Linux users just don't understand the incredible significance of that.  A central management of development would negate that advantage completely.

Linux is for an educated "community."  I don't want to use "market" because it's not as much driven by profit for a single entity but rather answering the needs of its community, which could be money or could be something else.  Ubuntu is the first distro I have seen which could conceivably be installed and run by a novice, without a huge learning curve.  Wonderful!  Go to it, it will no doubt help everyone.

One thing I would like to see is a hardware driver support list that actually reflects the true state of the drivers.  For example, for my sound card they should have said that the basic sound output is perfect, but that none of the extra features are supported at all.  Would have saved me over $100, I could have bought the version without the extra gadgets.

Another thing I would like to see for a distro like Ubuntu is that the LTS branch be the "main" branch, and have another suitable name for what we call the main branch.  People continually gripe about Open Source and using the regular users as a testing strategy.  It has always been that way for Linux.  Non-experts who install Ubuntu seem to expect something more tested than our main branch.  Or it's possible that the page letting you choose which branch to load could be altered such that those people pick the LTS branch a bit more often.

A lot of amazing things happen with Linux.  A lot of technical knowledge doesn't get released until a product hits the market, and still we get drivers or software support for new protocols in weeks.  That's nearly impossible in a commercial setting, just because of the bureaucracy in a large company.  Open Source has really shaken up software design in corporate settings, for the better.  Better yet, Open Source has contributors from the corporate world both from individual developers and from companies that put some commercial-worthy product into the Open Source realm.  The mixture is a great advantage for both worlds.

---

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-11
Maybe I could put my ideas about what Linux should do to improve more concisely:

I think we should do what we're doing now, only better.

That will attract more people to Linux, rather than stealing them away from someplace they would probably be happier.

In other words, we should be after those people who like what Linux is, rather than being after the people who like something else.

There are a lot of people who are hovering on the edge of giving Linux a try.  We don't need to change to what they're used to in order to win them over, because they're not happy with what they're used to.  All we need to do is what we are already doing, but find ways to smooth out the rough spots.

We should take a good look at the forums, both this one and all the other distros, and fix problems that lots of people have, especially frustrating first-timer problems that cause newbies to give up and go back to what they had.

Linux doesn't need to be better than Mac OS X.  It doesn't need to be better than Windows 7.  Those things are subjective.

Ubuntu needs to be a better Ubuntu.  Gentoo needs to be a better Gentoo.  Suse needs to be a better Suse.  Linux users who are giving help on the forum need to be more aware of what they're saying, whether it's personal opinion or real truth.  (That applies to me as much as anyone else)

---

### Post by NightwishFan on 2011-07-11
It does not 'just work' for everything, sure. It also does not 'just not work' either. Various GNU/Linux live cds are the closest thing to 'just work' I have ever seen.

As for being ready for the mass market. People have some sort of jaded view of what that means. The operating system and it's software only has to do what the users want it to do. It is not 'ready' when it becomes a clone of Windows or Mac.

It has to find its niche and work well there.

### Post by GSF1200S on 2011-07-11
Apple has a very comprehensive design for the entire spectrum of their offerings; while MacOSX might be focused on by guys like us who pay much attention to the Operating System as a concept, in reality OSX is only a piece of software designed to augment their hardware. Apple manages to sell their products at inflated prices (considering hardware specifications) chiefly because people are heavily focused on symmetry and aesthetics instead of capacity-specification, ostensibly giving its entire product a more refined appearance. That said, they do introduce great products and concepts.. Apple has, in terms of its customer service and marketing, many similarities to a cult; I say this not to attack them, but merely suggest that much of their popularity is their approach, not their offerings. Hitler was popular in his day, as was Ghandi (each with their own enemies; Ghandi good, Hitler evil).

I agree that OSX is more polished than many variants of Linux, but then it all depends on what you want. OSX fails completely on dual screens (in terms of providing XFCE-like control over each screen), and I for one could never stand the dock (or even the windows 7 superbar in icon mode). However, it works for others, so Im not looking down on the product... I would say that if polish is what you want, it is fairly hard to beat KDE 4.6/4.7 (though I dont use it because it too fails on dual screens), with XFCE 4.8 probably winning on stability (XFCE+ Compiz makes for some aesthetic pleasure though). Linux cannot create a product that works out of the box on everything (Apple can since they create both the hardware and software) since the range of hardware it must run on is insane. Just my .02..

### Post by dava4444 on 2011-07-11
> **1clue said:**
> HAH!  I played with a Newton way back when they first came out.  They had speech recognition of sorts, you could say something and it would type what it thought you said.

You're right, they were horrible.  Except for entertainment, because you could say "I went to work today" and it would print out "I ate a penguin with hay" or something equally nonsensical.

But I completely agree on timing.  When the iPad came out I thought, "Oh boy, it's a Newton."  I seriously thought it would fall flat.  Now I kinda want one, because some of the apps really kick ... rear.



Bless you!  You are just what this thread needs.  You brought up a bunch of really interesting points from the perspective of a non-nerd.



Why?  By having only one choice, or one overwhelmingly popular choice, the whole world loses out.  No one system is good at everything.  Let Apple have the people Apple has.  Apple has a significant niche, big enough to maintain a healthy community.  Carve a chunk out of the Windows masses.



That's the difference between a Mac user and a Linux user.  Mac OS has 'netstat -a' too, but she has no idea that it's there or why she would want to use it, let alone even seeing the Terminal app even once.

Mac users buy computers the same way they buy iPhones and toasters.  It's an appliance.  They buy it and do things with it, and that's it.  They don't want to have to know about the junk underneath it, they just want it to work.

Linux used to be only for people who had tape on their glasses, as a cushion so they didn't wreck your nose.  No tape on your glasses, then go use Windows.  No glasses, well you should either buy a Mac or not even own a computer.

The "stigma" is still there, because a lot of us still have tape on our glasses.  We insist on showing people what's behind the UI because we're proud of it.  We're showing these details to somebody who neither knows nor cares what "UI" means.

The majority of people in the world get by without computers.  At all.  A few years ago, I could have said with certainty that the majority of people in the world had never SEEN a computer.

The majority of skilled jobs in the world do not directly deal with computers.

The vastly overwhelming number of jobs in the world which require computer skills do not require intimate "under the UI" knowledge of how a computer works.  They do not require the understanding of what a UI is.

Go to Ubuntu.com and you'll see "cloud" computing on the front page.  I use the cloud, most of the people reading this probably know what it is to some extent, but why should your girlfriend even care?  The intro-to-the-intro page on Ubuntu.com is already talking over her head.

What's the cloud?  What's encryption?  Why do I need to know this stuff in order to check my email or go to facebook?



That's another big thing for most people.  The only way they want to stand out is by being more pampered than somebody else.  Even then they want to associate with people who are equally pampered.  Linux does not pamper.  Even Microsoft hides the details that Linux users take pride in, and Apple just ignores the existence of the nerd tools that are already there, but not interesting unless you're a nerd.

Personally, I would not buy a Mac unless it had a full set of nerd necessities.  It does.  It also has a full set of the stuff that makes people buy Macs.

A few years ago, my 70-something dad called me up because he wanted to buy a computer.  They were $300 at Wal-mart.  I live 1000 miles away.  They don't know anybody who even has a computer, let alone somebody who can fix it.  They're on a fixed income.

So what do you do when that happens?

I've been using Linux for well over a decade.  I'm not supporting that over a 1000 mile telephone call, when they don't even know what a mouse is.  I'm not installing Windows, that's an even worse support nightmare.  I bought them an iMac.  Mailed it to them.  They loved it.



That's the key, right?




not to tread older posts, but my GF laughed and agreed all the way through your post. (it was her I was on Skype to, sorry. #-o )

thanks!

:)

Dava

That the source is still available means that Apple has done their part.  It takes more than one to dance, and if Apple is the only one that shows up it's probably going to get boring.

### Post by fontis on 2011-07-12
> **pimentel28 said:**
> 
Personally, when I installed Ubuntu (aiming to make it my only OS), I wiped Windows completely off my HDD, including the recovery partition, so I wouldn't be tempted :P

And how well did that work for ya? :D

Anyway, 30 pages topic, who would've thought this was such a good topic to discuss.

OT: There seems to be quite a vast distribution of opinion regarding how we should view the current state of Ubuntu. But regardless, the goal that Ubuntu has set (to be a mainstream OS) requires us to deal with our shortcomings TODAY instead of labeling them "for the future". The reason why it's so relevant to rather look at OSX than Windows when it comes to usability is because OSX inherently IS easier to use for a first timer than Windows is. 

Most of us here have used different OS's for a very long time, and therefore we are familiar with how things ought to work and what not. So it's hard for us to determine ease of use from "our point of view" since whenever we use an OS we're technically already familiar with it at some level. Needless to say that the "first timers" still DO find the learning curve for Linux to be far higher than Windows, with OS X being the easiest to learn and use. 

Just the Unity step in itself is making things ridiculously harder for "first timers". The OS X approach e.g. of having an "application folder" with all your stuff is much easier than having a dash where you have to "search" for your program. What if you don't know what you're looking for?
