### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-12
To avoid confusion on what exactly we're talking about, I went back to the post you said I didn't understand.

> **1clue said:**
> I don't think bug #1 was a joke, I just don't subscribe to it.  I don't want to "steal market share" in Linux.  I would love for Linux to grow and develop, and for some of the rough spots to be smoothed out, but I don't think we should try to entice people who are happy with something else that we will do what Mac or Windows do only better.  What we call Linux today is frankly better in every way than what we called Windows or Mac OS when I started using Linux.  Computing technology moves forward, for everyone.  That's a good thing.  Different communities move in different directions, based on what their populations find helpful.  That's good too.  Linux uses a different measure of success, a different weight to social pressure.  That's incredibly good.




> **santaslittlehelper said:**
> 
I don't think it is just about "stealing market share" or "enticing people" I think it is about giving people a fair chance to choose Linux. The following is not something I have just made up but picked up reading over time and it makes sense to me. Most people don't care about operating systems they wont ever go download a iso of the Internet and install any OS themselves so the most reachable way to introduce people to Linux would be if major hardware vendors offered it pre-installed as a option beside Windows. The people who would have a hard time installing apps or maintaining Ubuntu are most likely the same people who have a hard time managing the very same thing on there windows systems. I don't think Ubuntu is really worse in terms of usability than Windows that is of course just an opinion.

I am not really disagreeing with you on anything else other then I don't think that Linux should be for a "educated community" only there are plenty of distributions with different goals and philosophies as you said yourself. 
My guess would be that they would only ship pre-installed systems with LTS but software will unfortunately still crash at times. We all wish for better hardware support a lot could be said about that I suppose however if Ubuntu came pre-installed it should be much less of an issue. 
If I should choose one thing I really like about open source and free software then I think it would be the diversity.

I completely agree that most people will not ever download an ISO and wipe their machine to install Linux.

I feel that those who do so have a compelling enough reason to make it work, and enough curiosity to solve problems that come up.

Simply making Linux-based computers available next to Mac and Windows might get some initial sales, but then what?  I see that as an instant failure unless you have some amazing support behind the product.  IBM did it 10 years or so ago, in conjunction with RedHat support.  You can still get any IBM system pre-installed with Linux on it.  It's still supported.  They get used, or else they wouldn't still be selling them that way.

So you have your random Walmart customer who comes in, buys a PC with Ubuntu on it.  Then they go get the latest bleeding edge video card so they can play whatever new 3d game is out there.  First, the card is probably going to only have half the features working, and second is going to not run the game anyway because it's a Windows game.

You can tweak the hardware all you want and come up with a system where everything works perfectly with Linux, but what happens when somebody plugs some random unsupported device into it?  Where they gonna get drivers?

There's a whole lot that needs to happen IMO before some generic Linux community can handle that sort of thing.  It takes a real company with lots of money and a whole lot of support people.  E.G. Redhat or Suse, who are already doing it.  (is there an echo?)

Off Topic:  For the record, I've installed Windows and Mac OS from CD as well, and it's not a picnic there either.  The easiest install I ever made was Ubuntu, literally 5 minutes from power on to rebooting into the real OS, and amazingly simple install.  That's when it goes right.  And also for the record, that was an IBM ThinkPad with XP on it, and XP would no longer recognize half the hardware.  Ubuntu recognized everything and set it up almost as if it were Mac OS...  :)

The reason I switched was that I found Vista to be very unstable on my system, with long boot times and Windows Aero constantly crashing and overheating my Nvidia Geforce 9100 graphics card (I have a Slimline case, thus it overheats very easily)

Depending on how things go, I may actually consider using Elementary as my primary OS (I have it installed on another partition).



What I see, is that people find the UI "different". People fear the unknown. No matter how simple it really is. 

Of course, I'd hate to see Ubuntu as a clone of Windows, I believe Ubuntu is in the right direction (just a bit more customization would be appreciated with Unity).

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-13
FWIW the only Microsoft operating system license I have ever personally purchased was Windows 7.  It was a $250 USD laptop, 64-bit, with a web cam.  I had intended to put Linux on it, but after having booted it up I realized the hardware was $#!+ and so I gave it to a friend whose laptop had recently been fried by an electrical storm.

I have always been very careful about not ever buying a Microsoft license, both because I don't like Microsoft (heavy bias on my part) and because I can't see buying a license I would never actually use.  The above Windows 7 laptop, I just heard "64-bit Windows 7 laptop with webcam" and went nuts, figuring you can't go wrong for $250.  Well, turns out you can.

Of the testing at work, I have never had a BSOD on any virtualization server, and the last time I recall a BSOD on something that I was even remotely responsible for it was NT4, an IBM build, which was failing on a Token Ring driver because it hadn't been able to get an address.

XP, Win2k, Win2003, anything above NT4 that I've actually used has been free of BSODs.

To give Microsoft credit that they're probably due, I believe all BSODs I've witnessed since Windows 97 were probably due to third-party software being installed badly.  Every truly bare-bones Windows install from a Microsoft CD seemed to work fine.  The BSODs came from third party junkware.

Note to all, that doesn't mean I in any way endorse or enjoy using Microsoft operating systems.

Lots of Open Source developers earn their living by writing software for pay.  Lots of Open Source contributors are commercial companies who decide that one or more of their products would make a good offering.  Lots of Open Source developers release a relatively sparse OS project and then write commercial plugins for it.

There is nothing in any Open Source license I've ever heard of that prevents you from making boatloads of money from Open Source.  Some of them simply limit the WAY you can do so.

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-14
> **santaslittlehelper said:**
> So what you're saying is that you don't feel Ubuntu is ready to get shipped pre-installed because some parts are to broken so people will get upset with it? Couldn't you just have said that to start with and if you did then I have to admit I missed it.


I thought I had been saying it all along.  Enough people have been "assertively debating" with me that now I wonder if I somehow didn't say it after all.

IMO, anyone who tries to ship boxes pre-installed with Linux you need to go further from an old-school distro and get into something like RedHat or Suse.  Not saying to use a non-debian distro, just saying that you have a huge professional staff building, testing and supporting the product.  Which is IMO about all RedHat and Suse did, with an eye toward enterprise environments.

Which brings another thing.  I think it would be much much easier to support an enterprise environment, because there you have professional IT staff who can comprehend that you can't just stick some random board in and expect it to work.

IMO you have three groups of Linux distributions:
[LIST=1]
[*]Old-school distros like Slackware, Debian, Gentoo, etc for people who have tape on their glasses.
[*]Commercially oriented distros like RHEL and Suse
[*]Less expert-oriented home oriented distros like Ubuntu.
[/LIST]

> 
That doesn’t make much sense really. If you and your company put time and money into software then you can define the terms of use however you see fit. Why do you insist to complain about something that really only offers possibilities and something that you are completely free not to use at all?     
One of your claims was "and the GPL is every bit as draconian as anything Microsoft ever came up with, only in a different way." if that's how you figure it I’ll tell you that you're wrong and that you should read the darn thing again. That's all I got to say really. :P


I'm not complaining about the licenses.  I'm complaining about misinformation about the licenses.  I'm completely fine with any sort of license anybody wants to put on their software, whether it means I can use it for my purposes or not.  There are really very few places where that sort of thing has caused me grief.

My only point is that rather than assuming you can use software absolutely any way you can dream up without any sort of consequences, you should read the license to make sure you understand what the terms of use are.  That's it.

Regarding the "draconian" quote, the way I read the GPLv2 license that statement is accurate.  If you're trying to sell software that you wrote for money, and you hard-linked a GPLv2 library on it, then the GPLv2 license is worded in such a way that somebody could claim that the commercial license is superseded by the GPLv2 license.

GPLv2 is not "draconian" at all if you're just using Linux the way Linux comes out of the box.  Microsoft is not "draconian" if you bought a Windows box and use it the way most people use a Windows box.  However, step outside of the bounds of the license and the GPLv2 might actually have a stiffer penalty, depending on your situation.

> 
So we agree you are free to make as much money as you can imagine if you can figure out how to do it.

Yes, with any OS and any software, as long as you don't violate the terms of use doing it.  With the exception of some not-for-profit, development, testing and educational licenses I've seen.

---

> **1clue said:**
> I'm not complaining about the licenses.  I'm complaining about misinformation about the licenses.  I'm completely fine with any sort of license anybody wants to put on their software, whether it means I can use it for my purposes or not.  There are really very few places where that sort of thing has caused me grief.

My only point is that rather than assuming you can use software absolutely any way you can dream up without any sort of consequences, you should read the license to make sure you understand what the terms of use are.  That's it.

Regarding the "draconian" quote, the way I read the GPLv2 license that statement is accurate.  If you're trying to sell software that you wrote for money, and you hard-linked a GPLv2 library on it, then the GPLv2 license is worded in such a way that somebody could claim that the commercial license is superseded by the GPLv2 license.

GPLv2 is not "draconian" at all if you're just using Linux the way Linux comes out of the box.  Microsoft is not "draconian" if you bought a Windows box and use it the way most people use a Windows box.  However, step outside of the bounds of the license and the GPLv2 might actually have a stiffer penalty, depending on your situation.
This is probably where we disagree then. In my opinion the GPLv2 as well as GPLv3 was made with good intention and is actually beneficial to people in general where Microsoft in my opinion like to troll when they get the opportunity.

### Post by lucazade on 2011-07-14
In a speech to investors in February 2009, Steve Ballmer of Microsoft presented a slide based on Microsoft's research about OS market share.

> As you can see, Microsoft sees piracy as its biggest competitor. "Number two market share goes to Windows pirated, or unlicensed," Balmer said, "That's a competitor that's tough to beat, they've got a good price and a heck of a product, but we're working on it." This isn't exactly new information, but it's interesting to see it spelled out so clearly.

Much more interesting is Microsoft's idea of Linux and Apple, According to Microsoft, Linux is a bigger threat to the company than Apple, placing Linux above Apple in the marketshare figure pie chart thing.

[http://www.osnews.com/story/21035/Ballmer_Linux_Bigger_Competitor_than_Apple](http://www.osnews.com/story/21035/Ballmer_Linux_Bigger_Competitor_than_Apple)

:)

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-14
> **santaslittlehelper said:**
> This is probably where we disagree then. In my opinion the GPLv2 as well as GPLv3 was made with good intention and is actually beneficial to people in general where Microsoft in my opinion like to troll when they get the opportunity.


ps glad to see we have someone fixing spelling errors lol.

It's possible we disagree here.  If we do, then that's OK.

I believe that the GPL was made with good intention, but I also think that Microsoft licenses were made with good intention.  In all cases though, the person/group writing the license is a human rather than a deity, so they can only see reasonably clearly to protect the interests of one group, while making possibly unfair conditions to some other group.

In my experience, home-based Linux users tend to think of Microsoft as evil, and corporate lawyers and C*O's tend to think of Open Source as evil.  I think in most cases both sides are biased by ignorance or by an extreme viewpoint.

I work for a commercial software company, so we MUST stay legal with both commercial interests and with Open Source of all flavors.  It is my strong belief that the very best computing environment contains both Open Source and commercial software.  I know some of you guys strongly feel that I'm wrong, and I know other people in my profession who feel strongly that you guys are wrong.

It takes all kinds to make the world go around.

Peace out!  O:)

### Post by 1clue on 2011-07-15
> **murderslastcrow said:**
> Macs are definitely selling a lot better than they were even two years ago. I can't say that Linux's popularity has grown at such an accelerated rate in the same time frame. There was a time that even the biased web ratings put Linux above OS X, but that was because the Linux base had been growing for far longer than Mac's (it really picked up when OS X came out, obviously).


I can attest to that.  Not so much from working at my company, what any company does is hardly anything to base statistics on.  But showing up at a software symposium where you spend a couple thousand dollars for a few days of concentrated education by some really big names in the industry, you take a look at what everyone is running.  When I first started showing up, I saw a whole lot of Windows laptops, a handful of Linux systems and one Mac.  (The Mac was me).

The last one I showed up to, there were maybe twice as many Linux systems and maybe an equal (to the Linux number) amount of Macs.

Thinking back on it it's hard to give reliable numbers.  It seemed like about 1 in 5 systems was not Windows.  Maybe I just noticed the non-Windows systems more.  Not valid statistics.

> 
I'd say Linux is definitely used in business a lot more often than OS X, at least from what I've read and researched. There might be a hugely overwhelming based of Mac users in the enterprise that totally eludes my searching.


On the server side, absolutely.  As well there must be some big businesses who use Linux on the desktop, or these RHEL and Suse workstations would not still be available, nor would those companies still be pushing out what they push out.

In my area near Chicago, I have noticed a whole lot more people carrying Macs as opposed to some sort of PC hardware.

> 
Considering Apple's advertising, if Macs were cheaper than other computers (on average) I'm sure a lot more people would be into OS X. If any distribution of Linux had as much money and talent to spend on good advertising and product identity as Apple does, I'm sure we'd be a lot more visible to consumers.


FWIW I think a part of Apple's success is due to having real graphic artists work on UI design.  I would buy a commercial product that put a Really Good UI over the top of Linux.  I'm reasonably sure that others would do so as well.

> 
I wonder if we could get another Super Bowl commercial showing off what Linux actually is to people today. That would be interesting- with more designers in the community these days, I'm sure we'll get some better websites and ads pretty soon.

I think you're referencing the 1984 Apple Superbowl commercial?  [http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8UZV7PDt8Lw](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8UZV7PDt8Lw)

I think you can only do that once.  When Apple did it, it was brilliant.  Probably more so because they only aired the commercial once ever.  Since then EVERYONE has been trying to run a replay of that marketing genius, and nobody entirely succeeds.

Some things work not so much because of inherent genius, but because of the time and place where somebody had a new idea.  The original War of the Worlds radio play is an example, it was fiction and advertised as such but people actually thought it was an alien invasion because nobody had ever used radio in that way before.  I don't think the world will ever be convinced of something like that anymore.

That Apple ad is another one.  It was a bold move with perfect timing, and made by a relatively obscure company at the time.

### Post by Syndicalist on 2011-07-16
Witch Lady, that was for an Ipad, not really relevant to my point.

If we are throwing all Apple products together, then yes in 2011 there has been a 76% jump in Apples popularity in these mobile devices.

Android has also seen huge growth. Android is now more popular than IOS. Therefor Linux is more popular if you dont separate these devices. 

So we are faced with a problem of hypocrisy.....we have people generously pooling apples combined products together but not Linux's. Why the double standard?



I cant even find an article talking about Apples desktop and laptop sales, only generic "sales" which presumably are mostly for their mobile devices which are the only devices that get any internetional press outside of a handful of articles from 2009 and 2010 that site "poor international sales".



But its really common sense. American made products cost more. We see the difference in price in the store, but when you are already in the poorer economy the domestic products are going to see an even larger divide in price against an American import compared to what we see. The only place you wont see that is healthier economy, like parts of the UK or Europe.

Just studying peoples average incomes, it becomes painfully obvious that only a very privileged few in these countries are going to be paying what Americans pay for their devices.

I didnt research this groups methodology or standards, so Im just throwing it out there for information purposes. If the source is weak, thats fine because my opinion isnt really based on it. Just saying that the numbers dont "always" come out the same.

And while there are more people in China there are probably less computers owned by only one person, and less leisure time in the working classes.

Apple Iphone until recently had poor sales in China, but saw a pretty rapid jump in their MOBILE markets.

[http://www.goodscool.com/wholesale-news/article-680.html](http://www.goodscool.com/wholesale-news/article-680.html)



Android more popular than IOS....but that says nothing about desktop and laptops. 

[http://thenextweb.com/mobile/2011/02/07/android-now-more-popular-than-ios-in-the-us-according-to-comscore/](http://thenextweb.com/mobile/2011/02/07/android-now-more-popular-than-ios-in-the-us-according-to-comscore/)


Linux has been killing the server market in China for a long time, which means much of the web runs on Linux....the software that makes our online experiences possible, at least what its hosted on.....but still not specific to personal desktops.

[http://www.internetnews.com/dev-news/article.php/3599931/SCO-Loses-in-China-as-Linux-Gains.htm](http://www.internetnews.com/dev-news/article.php/3599931/SCO-Loses-in-China-as-Linux-Gains.htm)





The future is really the blurring of lines between mobile devices and laptops....a tablet can do more of what a laptop can do and you can plug them into keyboards now.....currently a niche market, but I think the future will be a combination of cloud and tablet devices with optional keyboards and mice....Apple may be able to stay relevant, but I wouldnt expect them to maintain any supreme dominance over Linux once that happens.


And more popular does not mean "better" anyway. It doesnt even mean that its a more polished and user friendly OS. It just means they spend more and marketing and image. It proves absolutely nothing about quality, or why would Windows be beating Mac?

Personally, I think the 4% and 5% statistics are exaggerated, outside of maybe some Communist countries or former Soviet Republics....Linux seems to be the most popular in the former soviet block, and southeast asia SOUTH of China....based on some statistics I found online. I would say my best guess, by no means a credible source on its own, would be closer to the 2% statistics I have seen, maybe 3%....A guess, but I have seen the range from 0.17% to 6% excluding the mobile market.


Apple isnt growing as fast as the combined Linux based alternatives in the mobile market, overall in the last stretch of a year or longer. Maybe in a specific quarter Apple grew slightly more in specific international markets, but Im not even sure of that.




I cant find a single article talking about macbook or apple desktops in China....they seem to get no press whatsoever there!
[/B]

### Post by aysiu on 2011-07-16
> **Stephaans said:**
> because people don't trust free things. They would rather trust a company that they pay therefore they are 'guaranteed' service and have reason to complain
While I understand what you're saying, this isn't 100% true. When I introduced someone recently to TeamViewer, he was more than happy it was free. There was no distrust. Same with Firefox, Chrome, Skype, Dropbox, GIMP, OpenOffice...

I think there is more of a distrust of any computer (as opposed to mobile) operating system that isn't Mac OS X or Windows, because so often we hear the phrase "We support both PC and Mac" or "This is a PC-only program," and by *PC* they really mean *Windows*, so when people see something that isn't *PC* (meaning Windows) or Mac that is free and that "no one" else seems to be using, they're naturally suspicious.

### Post by Brad55 on 2011-07-23
One of the reasons I think that the OS X is so popular is because of the way they did things. They took one OS and a specific hardware set and made the OS work and a stable system. When they first started out it was a Motorola cpu  just like the Amiga. Because of the stable platform that they built that built the following for the system. At the time this was happening Commodore was in the running with the Amiga that also ran with the Motorola cpu. In the late 80's early 90's Amiga had the 2000 that could run Windows, Mac, Commodore64, and Amiga OS all in the same box, the only problem was it coast about $12,000 USD for such a box. 

But it really comes down to one thing and thats marketing, MS marketed theirs for Office usage and Mac marketed theirs for supporting business, such as servers because it was stable. Commodore failed because they did not have a good marketing plan, but is still used in the Music industry today because of its superior sound quality. Oh it is also used in a lot of Graphics production companies to. Commodore if I remember right was the first one to break the 640k barrier, and separate main RAM from video RAM. Oh and the 640k is still not broken today in the computers we have, that first 640k. [[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]Conventional Memory[/COLOR]]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conventional_memory")

### Post by aquarius18 on 2011-07-23
Aren't we talking about popularity here? 

Like many others, I was cheesed off with the constant Windoze stuff-ups (many and varied). Windoze (still) is the most popular OS, no question. The next alternative - by popularity was a Mac. No worries with that, apart from the cost factor to buy one of their machines. Apple is gaining ground on MS - no doubt. 

At some stage I discovered a couple of redundant former Windoze machines in my garage, revamped them with some new hardware, wacked Ubuntu on one and took it for a test ride. Ubuntu easily delivered what I needed (web development, home server, media center, some office stuff etc). Sure, it took me a bit of getting used to but I haven't turned back. Should I need another new machine I'd take another old box and do it up with some new hardware and Linux as OS, all at a fraction of the cost of a MAC and without the various hassles surrounding Windoze.

My webservers are running on UNIX/Linux and I refuse to take on any web development where the client wants me to use some Windoze .asp virus-vulnerable slomo system.

Linux sure is gaining popularity but fast and I am proudly waving the flag. My guess is that in a few years time we may see a 1/3 market share each between the 3 systems (of which 2 are second or third cousins).

Aside from the obvious reasons like being installed on the Mac which is a very popular and recognizable name in the computing world, OSX does it better than Linux. I will never buy a Mac because I tinker far too much and, though I have no problem spending money on my computer habits, I still find the Mac wildly overpriced.  Having said that, there is an appeal to having something that, for the most part "just works" and works well.

I also have to be honest, and this is an area of particular personal interest, Linux gui's are second rate.  People buy the Mac and by extension OSX because it is simple and beautiful.  Yes, you can make Linux look nice, but it doesn't have the coherent beauty of OSX.  It doesn't take a lot of looking to find the rough edges of Linux.  Eventually you'll run into ugly icons and windows that load with the wrong size and such.  

I also thing the Windows gui is garbage too but they have the advantage of the world as a user base.

I think Ubuntu is right to target OSX in the looks department.  I don't want them to clone it necessarily but I would love to see them make something as meticulously beautiful and, in tern, use that beauty and eye candy for function.

If Ubuntu can become easier to use and more consistently stable I think you'd see it gain some popularity.

### Post by Dr. C on 2011-07-24
> **KiwiNZ said:**
> Don't hold your breath when Corps have funds like Apple and MSFT have they can change direction, reinvest or purchase in order to meet market changes easily.

They can but more often than not choose not to adapt. The issue here is not financial resources but the internal corporate culture that prevents fundamental change. The larger the corporation the harder it is to change and adapt.  Where a corporation has successfully reinvented it self in the face of technological change it is because at a very fundamental level the nature of the business has not changed. IBM is a prefect example in migrating from tabulating machines to mainframe computers over the last 100 years.

As I said before only time will tell.

### Post by fancy_ninja on 2011-07-25
> **fontis said:**
> If you take the advertising and the "cult" surrounding OS X away, it feels like Apple has managed to polish their OS in a much better way than the Linux ecosystem has. Stability aside, compatibility seems to no longer really be an issue when using OS X. But then again, I don't own OS X so I can't comment on that really. 

What do you guys think? What is it that we lack, that others seem to have figured out?

First of all, we don't have to sell our souls to use Linux ;) Kidding, kidding (sort of..). Gotta realize that we live in a capitalist society-the "advertising" and "the 'cult'" you identify as "surrounding OSX" is precisely WHY Apple is succeeding in our society. Linux is like functional socialism as it is FOSS; free and open source for all, and people believe in that (or just wanna use it because it works and yes, because it is free). Apple is successful/popular because they have MONEY--lots of it, and money=success in our society. Apple also has popular, usable products (despite being hellishly overpriced). Such money is earned from users purchasing their products (obviously), the success/usability of the products themselves, the inflated cost (loaded word) of the products, appeal/advertising to the masses/"successful" marketing, etc. etc.

Yes, Linux is "less popular" than OSX; but then again, one has to ask oneself "isn't that necessary in a society such as ours when open source runs counter to the capitalist model of commerce?" Linux is made by people for people..OSS is the antithesis of capitalistically driven companies such as Apple (and yes, i acknowledge this is necessary in our society in order to compete with other companies and make money to SURVIVE).

What does Linux **lack** in a capitalist society? Capitalist aims rather than socialist ones. To personify the OS, Linux possesses a soul and purpose unfettered by the confines of a capitalist ecosystem..btw, OP--GSF1200s has many excellent (and better worded) thoughts regarding this topic..

### Post by blueridgedog on 2011-07-25
People like OSX for similar reasons to those for which they should like Linux.  It has a powerful Unix based kernel and a stable xwindow system.

Where OSX deviates from Linux is consistency (one window manger) and integration (specifically tailored hardware).  I think the unified control over hardware/OS/Window DE make an impressive platform.

Don't let the candied exterior fool you, I know several technical savvy individuals that can do great things with their Mac's.  I run several GNU applications as well as many standard Xwindow applications on my Mac and do a good bit of my shell work from a Mac.  The Unix kernel, specially tuned hardware and platform specific drivers make a nice package.

---

### Post by cbanakis on 2011-07-27
For anyone to even consider the possibility of apple overtaking Microsoft is a pipe dream at best.

This is 100% Fact.

OS X is incapable of replacing Windows.

You can do anything on a windows computer that you can do on a mac, NOT the other way around.

Apple still has a very limited supply of software providers due to its lack of popularity.

I work at a graphic design and printing company.

The owner of the company is pro mac, but unfortunately for him, there is no way the company can possibly operate without windows.

Everyone at the entire company is on macs, but now here it the kicker...

No one can charge a credit card without using remote desktop to a windows server.

Accounting also needs to remote to the server because the version of quickbooks for mac is completely worthless. 

Shipping needs to remote in, because the fedex software only works in windows.

All 3 of our presses are ran by windows.

The color server that processes color setting to send to the press is windows.

Our plate setter (Burns plates for the press) runs off windows.

Our color proofer...  Windows

Our digital press... Windows

Our Cutters... Windows

Our Booklet maker... WIndows

Need I go on.

There are no industrial applications where OS X can replace windows.

Probably a great big secret that will make all the Apple fanboys cry themselves to sleep, but here it is.

I'm pretty sure all the machining equipment used to build all those pretty cases for apple products are all ran by windows.

The bottom line is that Apple cannot possibly exist without windows.

Sure apple will always be around, but they will always be the little guy.

### Post by DoubleClicker on 2011-07-28
[Apple's design process]("http://www.businessweek.com/the_thread/techbeat/archives/2008/03/apples_design_p.html")

Interesting presentation at SXSW from Michael Lopp, senior engineering manager at Apple, who tried to assess how Apple can ‘get’ design when so many other companies try and fail. After describing Apple’s process of delivering consumers with a succession of presents (“really good ideas wrapped up in other really good ideas” — in other words, great software in fabulous hardware in beautiful packaging), he asked the question many have asked in their time: “How the f*ck do you do that?” (South by Southwest is at ease with its panelists speaking earthily.) Then he went into a few details:

Pixel Perfect Mockups
This, Lopp admitted, causes a huge amount of work and takes an enormous amount of time. But, he added, “it removes all ambiguity.” That might add time up front, but it removes the need to correct mistakes later on.
10 to 3 to 1
Apple designers come up with 10 entirely different mock ups of any new feature. Not, Lopp said, "seven in order to make three look good", which seems to be a fairly standard practice elsewhere. They'll take ten, and give themselves room to design without restriction. Later they whittle that number to three, spend more months on those three and then finally end up with one strong decision.

Paired Design Meetings
This was really interesting. Every week, the teams have two meetings. One in which to brainstorm, to forget about constraints and think freely. As Lopp put it: to "go crazy". Then they also hold a production meeting, an entirely separate but equally regular meeting which is the other's antithesis. Here, the designers and engineers are required to nail everything down, to work out how this crazy idea might actually work. This process and organization continues throughout the development of any app, though of course the balance shifts as the app progresses. But keeping an option for creative thought even at a late stage is really smart.

Pony Meeting
This refers to a story Lopp told earlier in the session, in which he described the process of a senior manager outlining what they wanted from any new application: "I want WYSIWYG... I want it to support major browsers... I want it to reflect the spirit of the company." Or, as Lopp put it: "I want a pony!" He added: "Who doesn't? A pony is gorgeous!" The problem, he said, is that these people are describing what they think they want. And even if they're misguided, they, as the ones signing the checks, really cannot be ignored.

The solution, he described, is to take the best ideas from the paired design meetings and present those to leadership, who might just decide that some of those ideas are, in fact, their longed-for ponies. In this way, the ponies morph into deliverables. And the C-suite, who are quite reasonable in wanting to know what designers are up to, and absolutely entitled to want to have a say in what's going on, are involved and included. And that helps to ensure that there are no nasty mistakes down the line.

---

### Post by Mikeb85 on 2012-01-14
Apple appeals to visual people (the hardware is very nice looking), and those who believe a higher price tag equals quality.  

They're really just taking people for a ride though.  Buy an Apple computer, an Apple phone, or an Apple iPad, and you're getting a subpar product (compared to the competition), yet paying much more.  

If you want a real picture of the situation, look at their investor reports - they have huge margins (3-4 times that of the competition), and are hoarding cash without paying anything out to their investors.  

As for why Linux is less popular, it's just not as well known, and Linux has done a terrible job of creating a consumer 'product', and marketing themselves.  The OS itself works great, but everything else sucks (must be the communist tendencies the open source world seems to harbor).

### Post by Greenborn on 2012-01-15
I have a Mac and laptop PC.  Both computers have a partition of Ubuntu on them.
  
As stated in previous posts, Apple&#8217;s control over the hardware configuration of their computers along with a well-designed OS makes for a very smooth and trouble free environment.  Liking an Apple computer is a matter of preference. They are priced higher than comparable PC&#8217;s but that seems to be of little concern to consumers since Apple&#8217;s market share has increased dramatically in the past 10 years. Humans like things that look good and function well- two things an increasing number of people, in the US at least, see in an Apple computer.  As Apple&#8217;s market share grows so will support from software vendors, which has been the case in recent years, making Apple computers more relevant to the home user.  
Linux has no control over hardware and little support from vendors.  As we all know this can make for a troublesome computing environment at times. Linux is still too much trouble for the average home user to install and use on a daily basis.
   
Linux isn&#8217;t packaged for the consumer like an Apple computer is or even a PC for that matter. There have been some attempts- Dell used to offer an Ubuntu loaded PC line but that obviously didn&#8217;t turn out so well. For Linux to replace Windows or OS X as a primary OS it will have to be marketed for and accepted by the average consumer. Something I&#8217;m not so sure I want to happen to Linux.  Linux isn&#8217;t Windows and it isn&#8217;t OS X.  It is what it is. And I like what it is- free, open source and fun but sometimes a real pain in the ***.

---

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2012-01-15
> **Mikeb85 said:**
> So Apple doesn't have visual appeal and Linux has great marketing and consumer awareness?  

Just walk past an Apple store today (and then a Sony store, and maybe a regular PC store), and you can see why Apple products sell.  If people knew a Mac was really just an Asus computer with a modded out BSD operating system, it wouldn't really have the same appeal, would it?  

Linux has no marketing close to what Apple has, requires people to install their own OS, and has a fragmented, seemingly complicated world to the average user.  If Ubuntu can get into stores, pre-installed on good hardware, it has a great chance of overtaking Apple IMO.  But until then, Linux will be perceived as being for 'geeks', whereas Apple enjoys a great 'image'.

Walk into an Apple store look around ask some questions, learn then return.

---

### Post by Ctrl-Alt-F1 on 2012-01-15
I think Apple has given itself a reputation and track record for quality.  My sister-in-law broke the screen on her MacBook Pro.  She took it in to the Apple store and they replaced the screen for free despite the fact that it was out of warranty.  She then noticed that the colors of the new screen were off, so she took it back in and they gave her a brand new model.

That's customer service, and it is what changes disgruntled consumers into loyal fanboys.  That's just one example.  I don't know much about MacOS because I haven't used it much, but the OS has the same reputation.

> **Ctrl-Alt-F1 said:**

That's customer service, and it is what changes disgruntled consumers into loyal fanboys.  That's just one example.  I don't know much about MacOS because I haven't used it much, but the OS has the same reputation.
I had a similar experience a year and a half ago with a failed graphics card:
[http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/the-macbook-pro-dead-video-card-saga/](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/the-macbook-pro-dead-video-card-saga/)

Love the Apple Store. Great customer service. Not too oversolicitous, either.

I've been using Ubuntu for almost seven years now, and there are many things I love about it, but I still can't use it as my main OS because of wireless problems (I've filed bug reports and seen many releases go by with no real fixes) on numerous machines. Instead of trying to support absolutely everything, Ubuntu should focus on several preinstalled models and make sure they all work seamlessly.

With my Macbook Pro, I get instant wake from sleep (including wireless). I have a sophisticated and seemingly real-time responsive multi-touch trackpad (not the jerky and laggy two-finger scrolling I get with Windows and Ubuntu), and I get a decent amount of third-party support (for example, Netflix streaming on Mac OS X--still none on Ubuntu after all these years).

Linux is great for a lot of things. When I had to set up kiosk computers for our school library, I popped Lucid Lynx on there. Works excellently. When we have to clone literally hundreds of Windows 7 laptops, we use CloneZilla (a modified Debian using Xfce).

But if people ask me for a laptop recommendation, I always say "Macbook Pro." I am not an Apple "fanboy." I'm about as objective as you can get. I use Windows 7 at work. At home I have a Ubuntu netbook, a Macbook Pro, and an Android phone.

One point I fully agree with Mikeb85 on is > Linux has no marketing close to what Apple has, requires people to install their own OS, and has a fragmented, seemingly complicated world to the average user. If Ubuntu can get into stores, pre-installed on good hardware, it has a great chance of overtaking Apple IMO. But until then, Linux will be perceived as being for 'geeks', whereas Apple enjoys a great 'image'. I wrote [Ubuntu: The Open Source Apple Challenger?](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/ubuntu-the-open-source-apple-challenger/) three and a half years ago, saying pretty much the exact same thing, but Ubuntu (along with the other "user-friendly" distros) is still going the "if we make it "user-friendly" and pretty, people will download an .iso and repartition their hard drives, etc.

Make a seamless product. Price it competitively. And then market it.

Ubuntu isn't a product right now. It's an .iso.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-16
> **Mikeb85 said:**
> For me personally, and my needs, I just don't see what any Apple product will do for me to justify the inflated cost.

Because Apple doesn't sell products. They sell complete, vertically-integrated computing solutions in which every piece is specifically engineered to work with every other piece. It is an entirely different approach to either Windows or Linux. When you buy a Mac, the OS works seamlessly with the hardware. You get all the ancillary apps you need to immediately hook up your phone, or camera, or whatever, and do things. You don't need to install drivers or remove bloatware or decide which window manager you want to use.

Essentially, Apple's purpose is to abstract the computer away so that people can just use the things. It is much more akin to what IBM does for its mainframes. When you buy big iron from IBM they don't just sell you hardware. They sell you the hardware, the OS designed specifically for that hardware and service contracts specifically designed for your needs. They come to your location and install everything for you, and, if something breaks, they will stick the new hardware on a plane, with a tech, to install it.

This isn't everyone's cup of tea and, clearly, it isn't the only approach. But it is Apple's, and it makes them successful at what they do.

---

### Post by Mikeb85 on 2012-01-16
> **aysiu said:**
> 
 I don't understand why you keep saying "Apple product" when you talk about "inflated cost." The iPhones, iPads, and iPods are priced about the same as or cheaper than competitor products. For example, an iPhone 4S with 32 GB of storage is US$299. A Galaxy Nexus with 32 GB of storage is US$299. Right now the iPhone 3GS is selling for US$0 on contract. An entry-level iPad is US$499. Similarly-sized Android tablets are sometimes up to US$799. A 2 GB iPod Shuffle is US$49, about as much as I paid for my 2 GB Sansa Clip. 

When you look at specs, and what you're actually paying (especially full price), Apple is a rip off.  Here (Canada) an iPhone 4S 32GB is $749 full price, or $269 on a **3** year contract.  I paid 99 bucks for an HTC Amaze on a contract, which has better specs (faster processor, faster wireless, bigger screen, better cameras, etc...). Also, Apple's Siri program can't do any location searches in Canada - I was pretty disappointed when playing around with one my friend bought.

When you compare apples to apples, and match Apple products with competitors with the same specs, Apple is more expensive.  Sure there are 800 dollar Android tablets and phones, but generally you get more for the same dollar (except with Motorola).  

Even more telling is when you look at a company's quarterly earnings, Apple's margins are 3-4 times better than the competition, which should tell you something.  While good margins are great for investors, they're not always indicative of good value to consumers...  

So yes, I do believe that Apple products have an 'inflated' cost.

[]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criticism_of_Apple_Inc")[]("http://www.businessweek.com/finance/occupy-wall-street/archives/2011/10/whats_the_moral_price_of_the_iphone.html")

### Post by Mikeb85 on 2012-01-17
To get back on track, I think Ubuntu could be more popular if Canonical decided to outsource hardware to say, Asus, and actually sell Ubuntu computers.  They could seriously undercut Apple, and deliver an experience adequate to the majority of the population.  

Of course, the other alternative is that ARM mobile devices become the most popular computers, eventually fully on par with desktops, and the most likely OS to dominate would be Android and forks of it.  I know since getting an Android phone my desktop gathers dust now.  

Anyhow, Linux as an OS will never rival OSX or Windows on the desktop, but as a product it could.

And I'll add, Mark Shuttleworth's vision will never come to be if he tries to be an OS option like Windows, Ubuntu will have to enter the consumer space themselves.

---

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-17
> **Mikeb85 said:**
> To get back on track, I think Ubuntu could be more popular if Canonical decided to outsource hardware to say, Asus, and actually sell Ubuntu computers.  They could seriously undercut Apple, and deliver an experience adequate to the majority of the population.  

First, you're vastly underestimating what is required to ship a good product. To do this, Canonical would have to pick one hardware vendor, one set of locked down hardware specs and put a lot of work into making sure Ubuntu worked with that hardware, seamlessly. Canonical would need to set up tech support, return policies, 800 numbers, and the like. Canonical would also have to decide the point at which they no longer offer support. Is it when someone adds a non-official ppa? Is it when someone installs Openbox instead of Unity?

Essentially, Canonical would have to stop being an OSS shop.

Second, you seem to think Apple is overpriced. You can make that argument for the Mac Pro, but none of the Wintel makers are able to match the price of the MacBook Airs, or most of their laptops. Apple has leveraged their huge cash hoard to ensure they can't be undercut on component prices.

And, beyond this, why worry? Making Ubuntu into something to directly compete with OS X would make it something other than OSS. For the foreseeable future, Apple isn't going anywhere, MS isn't going anywhere and Linux isn't going anywhere. Why not just enjoy the diversity?

### Post by llanitedave on 2012-01-17
> **KiwiNZ said:**

There is no law requiring use of Apple products they are not compulsory purchases. To assert that there is a moral issue is weird.

Just the opposite.  If purchasing an Apple product were mandatory, then it would not be a  moral issue because no choice would be involved.  That it is a choice is what introduces the moral componant.

You can choose to use a product the manufacturer of which [abuses its employees]("http://www.smh.com.au/technology/technology-news/apple-admits-supplier-abuse-of-workers-20120116-1q1wt.html") to the point of mass suicide (Foxconn), that [limits the freedom of its users,]("http://mashable.com/2011/11/18/linus-torvalds-apple-lockdown/") and that [is an abusive patent bully,]("http://www.techdirt.com/articles/20111213/02292717063/apple-abuses-patent-system-again-to-obstruct-w3c-open-standard.shtml") among other things, or you can make the moral choice to avoid that product and choose one that is not involved in such systematic and broad malfeasance.

There's no law requiring you to incorporate a moral dimension into your purchases, but for those who do, I applaud them.

Any time corporations are allowed to seek the lowest possible cost in an area of few limitations someone will be exploited.

### Post by llanitedave on 2012-01-17
> **dpny said:**
> These problems won't go away: they're endemic to capitalism. The history of the US from the end of the Civil War to the start of WWI is very similar to China now: a large population willing to do hard work for low pay and a government committed to laissez faire capitalism.  Any time corporations are allowed to seek the lowest possible cost in an area of few limitations someone will be exploited.

I don't disagree, and I partially apologize for the partial derail.  But it isn't a complete derail, because these factors do influence our choices in the products we buy, including our computer os.  We can't create a worker's paradise via the retail market, and it would be counterproductive to try, but consumer choices multiplied by the number of informed and concerned consumers can have a positive, if limited, effect.  So using moral considerations as a factor in one's os choice is not "weird", nor is it irrelevant.  It's a perfectly valid reason to prefer Linux over OS X.  And the argument that Free software is more moral than proprietary, whether you agree with the argument or not, is hardly unheard of in this day and age.  So the reaction of disbelief or dismissal by our respected Kiwi to the idea that someone would utilize that factor in their decision is what strikes me as a bit odd.  To disagree, fine -- to be derisive, not so fine.

There are a lot of reasons why OS X is more popular Linux, and most of them have been discussed at length in this thread.  Some of those reasons are good ones, others seem more questionable.  I would tend to the opinion that at least one of those many reasons is due to the fact that most consumers are not aware that one of the possible considerations is a moral one.  But if that idea were to gain traction, it might result in maybe an ever so slight change in the preference equation.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-17
> **llanitedave said:**
> There are a lot of reasons why OS X is more popular Linux, and most of them have been discussed at length in this thread.  Some of those reasons are good ones, others seem more questionable.  I would tend to the opinion that at least one of those many reasons is due to the fact that most consumers are not aware that one of the possible considerations is a moral one.  But if that idea were to gain traction, it might result in maybe an ever so slight change in the preference equation.

While I admire your idea, I don't think it's practical at the moment. Take a look at that list again: every major computer and smart phone vendor is on it. Almost all of our electronic devices are made at Foxconn, or a similar place, so there is almost no room to move here. If you want to use a smart phone or a PC, you will have to use something which was made by someone working 16-hour days for low wages in dangerous conditions. You may choose Ubuntu because you feel it's a more moral choice, but the hardware you're running it on came from the same place as all other hardware. Even if you build your own machine, the various components are made somewhere in the same complex as are Android and the iPhone.

I think this will shift as China changes, and we are already seeing pushes for higher wages, shorter hours and better working conditions in China. However, unfortunately, I think the exploitation will just move on to the next pool of cheap labor.

And all of this has nothing to do with why OS X is more popular. The simple answer is that Apple makes a better product, and Ubuntu is not competing against other ideas, but other OS products.

### Post by rg4w on 2012-01-17
> **dpny said:**
> The simple answer is that Apple makes a better product...
The main difference is that Apple makes a complete product.  To use Ubuntu you also need to buy a computer, which probably already has an OS.

It will never matter whether Ubuntu becomes "better" than Apple.

Most people don't choose OSes, they choose computers.

This may be hard for Linux fans to appreciate, but everything we do in the Linux world is completely unfathomable to the average computer user.  It's not even that installing an alternative OS is particularly difficult, just that the very idea of doing so is completely foreign to how consumers think of computing.

Consumers buy whole-product solutions, and Ubuntu is only half of a solution.  

System 76 and ZaReason make nice machines, but they have very modest marketing resources.

Asus, Dell, and the other major OEMs have the resources, but seem afraid to differentiate their products with an OS other than Windows, choosing instead to deliver a pixel-by-pixel replication of the user experience every one of their competitors offers.

Ironically, this bizarre refusal to differentiate reduces the sum of their respective businesses to commoditization, allowing them the freedom to move in only one dimension, price, and in only one direction, down.

Their margins get ever slimmer, and their shareholders complacently accept the notion that consumers won't accept an alternative to Windows.

Meanwhile, Apple is one of the largest companies in the world by market cap, selling nothing but alternatives to Windows.

I don't know when or even if the major vendors will wake up, or why so many of their stockholders demand so little from them in the way of innovation which could raise margins.

Until at least one of them wakes up, they will continue to make ever smaller profits, and Linux will remain relatively marginalized.

---

> **KiwiNZ said:**
> [QUOTE=C.S.Cameron;11616976] I believe the use of Apple products is a moral issue.
How on earth is it a moral issue?

There is no law requiring use of Apple products they are not compulsory purchases. To assert that there is a moral issue is weird.[/QUOTE]

I'm using specific examples in other fields in order to illustrate a general point.  You seem to be trying to avoid the general point by reverting to specifics when pressed, but you're perfectly willing to reference boats cars, toothpaste and flaws when it seems safe.

But your objection, that it's "weird" to assert a moral issue in a computer purchase, is essentially a universal one.  So other examples that counter your assertion are perfectly relevant.

So, it appears that you're conceding the general point through avoidance, but trying to stick solely to Apple/Linux doesn't help either.  Linux is released under the PL, which was invented SPECIFICALLY to add a moral dimension to software distribution.  So for one with 6 years and 25,000 posts in a Linux-focused forum to appear unaware that the choice of Linux is legitimately a moral one is what appears weird to me.

C.S. Cameron was correct.  You can credibly argue that your purchasing priorities trump any moral consideration, and I couldn't dispute it, since the priorities you have to judge by are solely your own.  But you can't argue that the moral considerations don't exist or aren't valid.

Linux is released under the PL, which was invented SPECIFICALLY to add a moral dimension to software distribution.  So for one with 6 years and 25,000 posts in a Linux-focused forum to appear unaware that the choice of Linux is legitimately a moral one is what appears weird to me.

C.S. Cameron was correct.  You can credibly argue that your purchasing priorities trump any moral consideration, and I couldn't dispute it, since the priorities you have to judge by are solely your own.  But you can't argue that the moral considerations don't exist or aren't valid.

Linux was created specifically for morals........ nonsense.

---

### Post by llanitedave on 2012-01-17
> **dpny said:**
> While I admire your idea, I don't think it's practical at the moment. Take a look at that list again: every major computer and smart phone vendor is on it. Almost all of our electronic devices are made at Foxconn, or a similar place, so there is almost no room to move here. If you want to use a smart phone or a PC, you will have to use something which was made by someone working 16-hour days for low wages in dangerous conditions. You may choose Ubuntu because you feel it's a more moral choice, but the hardware you're running it on came from the same place as all other hardware. Even if you build your own machine, the various components are made somewhere in the same complex as are Android and the iPhone.

I think this will shift as China changes, and we are already seeing pushes for higher wages, shorter hours and better working conditions in China. However, unfortunately, I think the exploitation will just move on to the next pool of cheap labor.

And all of this has nothing to do with why OS X is more popular. The simple answer is that Apple makes a better product, and Ubuntu is not competing against other ideas, but other OS products.

Again, I don't disagree, and I'm not tilting at windmills.  I agree that Apple products are for the most part terrific (with some exceptions).  For those who want to buy them for that reason, I won't criticize.  I think Richard Stallman's argument that one should not use products at all if they aren't moral enough is unrealistic and overblown.  All I'm arguing is that it's a legitimate consideration, and if the choice is there to make, without having it undermine your own self-interest, then it's a good thing to use that criteria.

### Post by Mikeb85 on 2012-01-17
> **rg4w said:**
> The main difference is that Apple makes a complete product.  To use Ubuntu you also need to buy a computer, which probably already has an OS.

It will never matter whether Ubuntu becomes "better" than Apple.

Most people don't choose OSes, they choose computers.

This may be hard for Linux fans to appreciate, but everything we do in the Linux world is completely unfathomable to the average computer user.  It's not even that installing an alternative OS is particularly difficult, just that the very idea of doing so is completely foreign to how consumers think of computing.

Consumers buy whole-product solutions, and Ubuntu is only half of a solution.  

System 76 and ZaReason make nice machines, but they have very modest marketing resources.

Asus, Dell, and the other major OEMs have the resources, but seem afraid to differentiate their products with an OS other than Windows, choosing instead to deliver a pixel-by-pixel replication of the user experience every one of their competitors offers.

Ironically, this bizarre refusal to differentiate reduces the sum of their respective businesses to commoditization, allowing them the freedom to move in only one dimension, price, and in only one direction, down.

Their margins get ever slimmer, and their shareholders complacently accept the notion that consumers won't accept an alternative to Windows.

Meanwhile, Apple is one of the largest companies in the world by market cap, selling nothing but alternatives to Windows.

I don't know when or even if the major vendors will wake up, or why so many of their stockholders demand so little from them in the way of innovation which could raise margins.

Until at least one of them wakes up, they will continue to make ever smaller profits, and Linux will remain relatively marginalized.

+1 to the entire post, either Suse or Ubuntu wake up and start selling branded hardware, or an OEM actually puts some effort into selling a Windows alternative.

---

### Post by Mikeb85 on 2012-01-17
> **dpny said:**
> First, you're vastly underestimating what is required to ship a good product. To do this, Canonical would have to pick one hardware vendor, one set of locked down hardware specs and put a lot of work into making sure Ubuntu worked with that hardware, seamlessly. Canonical would need to set up tech support, return policies, 800 numbers, and the like. Canonical would also have to decide the point at which they no longer offer support. Is it when someone adds a non-official ppa? Is it when someone installs Openbox instead of Unity?

Essentially, Canonical would have to stop being an OSS shop.

Second, you seem to think Apple is overpriced. You can make that argument for the Mac Pro, but none of the Wintel makers are able to match the price of the MacBook Airs, or most of their laptops. Apple has leveraged their huge cash hoard to ensure they can't be undercut on component prices.

And, beyond this, why worry? Making Ubuntu into something to directly compete with OS X would make it something other than OSS. For the foreseeable future, Apple isn't going anywhere, MS isn't going anywhere and Linux isn't going anywhere. Why not just enjoy the diversity?

First, lots of Asian OEMs will build on contract, Canonical need only worry about the software.  

Second, an Asus Zenbook starts at the same price as a MacBook air, and there's lots of laptops cheaper than the cheapest MacBook pro.  

Third, this ideological attachment to OSS is what prevents Linux from becoming mainstream.  Yet at the same time, OSS, with a few ideological concessions could do a lot of good, for the developing world, and for those who can't afford prohibitively expensive software.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-17
> **Mikeb85 said:**
> I never said they were bad for it, but that they don't pass on savings to the consumer or investors.  They hoard cash and don't pay out.

A basic misunderstanding of what Apple's cash is for: they use it to, essentially, give their suppliers short term, zero-interest loans to ensure that Apple's production isn't constrained by lack of supply. They've made loans in the billions to Samsung and their display providers to do things like build new production lines or entire new factories.

Shareholder value is the most useless metric of business performance yet invented.

Do not assme the mechanisms which have meant success in one platform exist in another platform.

### Post by rg4w on 2012-01-18
> **dpny said:**
> Linux will never have any serious desktop penetration. And, as the desktop market slowly dwindles, it will matter less and less.
"Dwindles" is hard to quantify.  During the tablet frenzy these last couple years, the rate of PC sales growth has indeed slowed - but it's still growth.  That is, more PCs were sold last year than the year before.  Think about it.

Mobile devices are definitely going to be dominant for the foreseeable future, but not to the degree that they completely displace PCs.

The phone is arguably the most essential gadget, but there's only so much it can do.  And to fit in your pocket the screen is small.  Very small.  And the keyboard on a phone will never be the sort of thing on which anyone writes the great American novel.

Tablets are an interesting niche, but they're not nearly as portable as a phone (you can't put it in your pocket, so like an Ultrabook you need a carrying case), and as useful and fun as they are they're also somewhat limited in utility; most reviews citing the iPad as the future of computing weren't written on one, they were written on a PC.

And there's the rub: as fun as tablets are, with 0.5 GM RAM, a modest processor, and a virtual keyboard that consumes half of the screen, they're great at the cafe or on the sofa or on the factory floor or on hospital rounds, but they're not the optimal choice for doing much serious work at a desk.

Steve Jobs compared PCs to trucks, with a misunderstanding about the evolution of the automobile in which he suggested that trucks were once the dominant form of automobile but eventually gave way to the car, and they're still useful but just not dominant.  Likewise, he suggested that PCs will be like trucks, in that people will still use them, just not as often.

He may be right to some degree, but the reality is that the current definition of a tablet simply doesn't provide the usability or raw horsepower of even a cheap PC for real work.

Tablets are great for grazing content, but for creating the content that others graze we still need PCs.

I'll go out on a limb to predict that by the fourth quarter of this year we'll see an uptick in PC sales, now that people have had enough time with tablets to see what they can -- and cannot -- do.

Taking this further, I believe the surprise story of 2013 will be the resurgence of the PC, not nearly as dominant as phones but strong just the same, as more and more people come to appreciate the value of having a tablet when they're on the road, and the value of a PC for everything else.

While it remains to be seen whether Ubuntu can get sufficient OEM interest to fully capitalize on these opportunities, its ability to cover the spectrum of both x86 and ARM devices bodes well.

Fingers crossed....

---

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-18
> **rg4w said:**
> Taking this further, I believe the surprise story of 2013 will be the resurgence of the PC, not nearly as dominant as phones but strong just the same, as more and more people come to appreciate the value of having a tablet when they're on the road, and the value of a PC for everything else.

I disagree.

You're assuming that tablet technology will not improve. However, as in all things, processors will get faster, RAM will increase, etc. We will continue to see huge growth in tablet sales (which really means huge growth in iPad sales) and flat or declining desktop sales. I think we will also continue to see growth in laptop sales.

> While it remains to be seen whether Ubuntu can get sufficient OEM interest to fully capitalize on these opportunities, its ability to cover the spectrum of both x86 and ARM devices bodes well.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-19
> **C.S.Cameron said:**
> If giving away free software worked for Google I suggest it will work for Canonical. The desktop/laptop makers can compete better if their software is free and it surpasses the competition, (MS and Apple), which it does.

You don't understand the difference in markets: Google can only give away Android for free because there exists an entrenched infrastructure of carriers who have set up a captive delivery system in which people trade lower phone prices for contracts. Google doesn't have to worry about marketing, sales, returns, tech support, production costs, localization or anything else. The carriers do all the hard work of actually selling the product.

No such conditions exist in the desktop market. And, as I have already said, to enter the desktop market Linux would have to include closed-source, proprietary software in the distribution, so it would no longer be an OSS product.

I know you want it to happen, but it won't.

> . . .and thus Microsoft saved Apple.

No, it didn't. Jobs saved Apple.

> If MS had made Office open source . . .

Easily the funniest thing I've ever read.

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2012-01-19
I would sincerely love to see Linux on the Desktop for Enterprises and consumers succeed. The reality is Linux is not new, it has been around in the current paradigm for at least 10 years and its penetration on the desktop has been largely static at +/- 1%.

To compare the success of Android to Linux in general is a false equivalency they are not the same product or a close derivative. Android is a new product.

For Linux to succeed on the Enterprise and consumer desktops there has to be a substantial change from the current paradigm.

---

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-19
> **C.S.Cameron said:**
> Why would Ubuntu need to include proprietary software anymore than MS and Apple? There are Linux distributions with absolutely no proprietary, closed-source, software.

If you want to compete against OS X and Windows, you will need to do the things they do.

The first thing people will want to do is plug in their iPhones and iPads and have them just work. Given the current sketchy state of iOS device support in Linux, that will mean going to Apple and getting their help in writing drivers and, probably, porting iTunes. That means incorporating Apple's code--closed-source, proprietary, not free--into Ubuntu. This would, obviously, effect Ubuntu's OSS position.

The second thing people will want to do is open MS Office files--all MS Office files, natively--without any problems. This means either putting a herculean amount of effort into WINE, or, more likely, going to MS and essentially begging for help and for a Linux port of Office. MS code will be, like Apple's, closed-source, proprietary and not free. Knowing how MS works, it will likely mean Ubuntu will have to pay MS a license fee for every copy sold.

This process keeps going. Anything on Linux which doesn't work out of the box will require Canonical going to another company and asking for help, or drivers, or something like that, and none of it will come free of charge.

Desktop Linux doesn't compete in the same sphere as OS X and Windows.

> I find this insulting.

I apologize. I did not mean to insult you. I meant that I can't imagine MS open sourcing anything, ever.

> Miicrosoft has a lot more to fear from Linix than from Apple.

MS has never had anything to fear from Linux on the desktop. In the server and mobile worlds, MS has much to fear from Linux.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2012-01-19
I got the second Android phone released in the US (the MyTouch 3G), and it was horrible--slow, with usability issues. Even then, though, I knew Android would succeed, and that's why I picked it over WebOS, which was getting rave reviews for its interface.

The real problem with "desktop Linux" is that the focus has always been about improving the interface or making it more "user-friendly." I love user-friendly. I think interfaces should be more user-friendly. I like usablity and all that. But usability is **not** what pushes you into the mainstream, as Android has demonstrated.

I'll tell you what the American launch was like. I'd love to hear non-US perspectives on this, too. In the States, T-Mobile announced the G1, which a bunch of people got because it was a "Google phone." They could buy it, sign in with their Gmail accounts, and be up and running. G1 was not a big success, but it had visibility. The MyTouch 3G was a horrible phone. Then a few months later, Verizon, Motorola, and Google partnered up together to market a product. Droid commercials were everywhere. And the Droid was a big success--not quite as big as the iPhone. Still, you could see Android was going places, because it was 1) priced competitively 2) marketed appropriately and 3) available to try out and buy. Android phones were also Android phones. They were not hacks to get some alternative operating system onto an iPhone (though some hackers did try to do that with no real usable results).

For the past seven years that I've been privy to (and maybe longer) "desktop Linux" distributions have been "marketed" as "Have Windows problems? Try this magic free thing. You need to download an .iso (first figure out what an .iso is), burn it, use it to boot on your computer, repartition your hard drive and then install it. Then follow these guides to troubleshoot any hardware issues. Then follow these guides to get your codecs installed. Take a look at this website to see alterantive software for the software you're used to."

See the difference between that and the Droid launch? 1) not a product to market 2) not priced competitively (if you complain about hardware issues, Linux zealots tell you to buy Linux preinstalled, and Linux preinstalled is always more expensive than Windows preinstalled on similar systems) 3) not available to try out and buy as a product.

That's what most consumers want. They want a product with name recognition that's mass-marketed, that they can try out in stores, and then just buy and use. Some power users are fine with hacking some thing to work.

I heard for years that I shouldn't use a Broadcom wireless card because it wasn't open source. I have had a hell of a time the last couple of years getting decent performance from Ubuntu on an Intel card. I'm tired of kernel regressions and bug fixing politics. My Android phone is built to be an Android phone, and it runs Android perfectly.

Linux could succeed on the desktop if a company ran Linux as a business and not an ideology.

---

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-19
> **KiwiNZ said:**
> It needs a quantum shift is belief,process,mindset and goals.

That's part of what I mean.

Take, for example, software compliance. For Canonical to market Ubuntu as an OS X/Windows competitor, it will have to offer telephone tech support. In order to offer that, Canonical will have to make sure they can support all of the software which comes with a standard Ubuntu install, either through Canonical themselves, or through the company which makes the software. This would mean a serious audit to ensure all the software meets this goal. This would have several consequences.

Severely limited selection of software in the Software Center/Store. If every piece of software needs to offer tech support, that cool project hosted on Sourceforge, with no central organization, is out;
All software included must be audited by Canonical to meet Ubuntu's UI rules;
Non-Canonical software which is included need to be run like a business: tech support, returns, billing, etc.;
Rules about voiding your warranty if you install some third party software;
Restrictions on adding ppas to your Ubuntu install. Any adding of a non-Canonical ppa would need to bring up a warning message telling the user that Canonical will not support the software they're installing and will not help with any support problems caused to the install by the software.
And the list goes on.

OSS is a wonderful thing, but is not a business. And, to compete with OS X/Windows, a business it will need to be.

---

### Post by JayKay3OOO on 2012-01-20
If Ubuntu was branded Google then it's a safe bet that more people would be using it and vendors more willing to deploy off the shelf systems to customers.

Computers are emotional devices to some people. Apple is a brand that people want to be associated with because they think it makes them part of an elite social sector and is a small display of wealth compared to the MS system that is targeted at average joe.

Although OSX is more novice friendly than even Windows 7 as W7 requires some knowledge to use it whereas OSX is pretty idiot proof and this is what a lot of people want. MS wins customers because it can be deployed on really cheap entry level systems and often these users are first time computer users who go to a store and see a computer on the shelf and have no interest in what OS it runs. The fact that it runs MS windows is 'fine' because there is no obvious alternative most of the time. It's on there so it must work.

Linux is not even on the radar and is reserved for users that have an interest in computers.

I recently stopped a client throwing away a printer by using Libre office as MSoffice would not display .doc documents properly. Installed libreoffice and they display fine and the printer is saved from the trash. +1 to OSS. A fact that remains that even on my Windows laptop all the software is open source.

I have Linux to thank a lot for showing me the power of OSS. There is still a place for Linux, but it's never going to take over the world.

Never is a long time though.

### Post by Browncoyote on 2012-01-21
As a new user, I agree with many of the comments above and will attempt to answer the question without digressing into arguments or attacks against Apple's business model. I will say that having hardware and software both developed in house resolves all compatibility issues which makes average users very happy. Most of the linux user's time is spent making the system do as they want, while Mac users spend most of their time doing what the machine was designed to do, function with other mac hardware. I have observed that many professionals in the field of art go with Mac for reliability and cutting edge performance. Myself and other linux users tend to migrate away from mainstream operating systems due to budget constraint and/or ideological reasons. OS X is essentially a *nix custom build. #justsayin

### Post by aysiu on 2012-01-22
Are we really arguing semantics over the word *Linux*?

Many people use the word *Linux* as a shorthand for desktop/laptop/netbook-oriented Linux distributions, not necessarily referencing every single operating system that uses a Linux kernel. Is that okay?

Yes, Android uses a Linux kernel. So does a Roku box. So does PS3. Are we now going to start determining Linux market share based on embedded and mobile?

### Post by philinux on 2012-01-22

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-22
> **C.S.Cameron said:**
> I don't see your argument as being the least bit valid.

The argument is simple. What's the first thing someone will want to do after buying their Linux machine? Plug in their iPhone/iPad/iPod and have it Just Work. The only way this is possible is to go to Apple and have Apple write the drivers. As iOS is closed source, this means Ubuntu will have to include proprietary, closed-source software in its distribution. What's the second thing people will want to do? Open MS Office documents natively. This means going to MS and, essentially, begging them to either port Office to Linux. We all know how MS operates: in exchange for this they will want a hefty price, and their version of Office will not be OSS, and will not be cheap.

> Asus, HP, Dell, Epson and many others offer computers with Linux pre-loaded.

None of which have sold well.

> As soon as there is less ignorance and more demand, most of the other vendors will offer Linux as well.

As Kiwi said, consumers practicing choice about computers is not ignorance. Describing it as ignorance is merely a way to avoid looking at the issue. Computers are products, and selling a successful product is a multi-faceted problem.

> Office Libre is just fine for most users, I use it at work myself.

When your job requires you to work heavily in Office, 'just fine' isn't good enough. People will require a Linux version of Office to work exactly as well as the Windows and OS X versions.

### Post by xyzzyman on 2012-01-23
