The revolution is happening and the desktop is being pushed to the side. Tablets and smartphones are dominating more and more of the users time, and they are increasing in speed, functionality and capabilities at an extremely fast rate due to competition in the marketplace. The desktop will serve it's place but for the majority of users, it is becoming the last resort option. (The users of ubuntu are not representative of the general public.) Even in the workplace outside of resource intesive applications, things are moving back towards thin-clients accessing the cloud and at that point the OS doesn't even matter.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-23
> **C.S.Cameron said:**
> This is not refering to the average user who just uses the computer to surf, chat, send email, download movies, write a novel, etc. 

You'd be surprised how many average users use Office. It is ubiquitous. It's preinstalled on every Windows machine, and comes installed on a lot of Macs.

> Come the revolution it will be MS Office that will be required to match OL formatting etc.

What revolution? How will this revolution happen? Will people suddenly throw up their hands? If Ubuntu is to be successful on the desktop, it will need to give people a compelling reason to switch. Windows is compelling because it comes preinstalled and most people are familiar with it from work. OS X is compelling because it is a vertically integrated solution which seamlessly covers both desktop and mobile systems and focuses on user experience.

Ubuntu is compelling because. . . ? It's free? You can tinker? You can choose your WM? There is no compelling reason for someone not emotionally invested in their computer--which covers the vast majority of computer users and which describes no one here-- to use Linux. Computers and operating systems are not ideas. They are products, and products must be sufficiently differentiated to be noticed.

> And looking through these forums it looks like there is not much problem plugging in an iPhone/iPad/iPod and having it Just Work.


If you read more closely you will see that people have varying levels of success with older iOS devices and various problems with iOS 5 devices. Additionally, there is no app store. If Ubuntu is to be successful, iOS 5 devices would have to be supported at the same level as OS X and Windows.

Beyond all this, why does it matter than Linux has a tiny desktop share? It controls the server market, runs the internet and has a large share of the mobile market. Linux isn't going anywhere.

---

### Post by xyzzyman on 2012-01-23
> **dpny said:**
> Ubuntu is compelling because. . . ? It's free? You can tinker? You can choose your WM? There is no compelling reason for someone not emotionally invested in their computer--which covers the vast majority of computer users and which describes no one here-- to use Linux. Computers and operating systems are not ideas. They are products, and products must be sufficiently differentiated to be noticed.

That's one of the best arguments made so far. Most people aren't emotionally invested in their operating system. They are invested in their facebook, itunes, twitter, email. My 5-year old niece wanted to play on NickJr.com, and I opened up chrome on ubuntu and she got on just fine and was surfing away. As far as most people, customizing their OS involves being able to change the wallpaper (It's anecdotal, but that's the only complaint I've heard from normal users about Windows 7 Starter Edition.) They can set their own wallpaper and they are happy. As soon as they can't play music, watching a youtube clip, play a game on facebook then sh*t has hit the fan. 

As much as some people hear hate windows and mac, any person buying a system nowadays knows that they can go buy virtually any system off the shelf running one of those two OS's and they can do anything they want. It's not even like 5, 10 or 15 years ago when you had to worry about specs, as even cheap systems are coming with 4GB's of RAM and 160-320GB hard drives (Unless they are pirating media the average user does not use 160GB of hard drive space.) Microsoft is practically giving away Win7 Starter to OEM's so Linux is not offering anything different to sway them.

$200 tablets seems to be the sweet spot. Kindle Fire is borderline performance wise from what I've heard, but by next holiday season with improved hardware & developers having more experience with ICS tablets at that price point will be able to handle what regular people want to do 90% of the time. Hence why I said for the foreseeable future there will be a use for the desktop/laptop, but it's become marginalized, and possibly rightly so. How long have we been using desktops in the modern form, 15-25 years? and they still are not as easy to use as an iPad or android tablet. 

I'd be interested in a survey or study of how many long time linux users actually are annoyed now and don't tinker. I can do all my customizations to an 11.10 install in less than 10-15 minutes and I'm set. I switched back to using Ubuntu full time this past August after using Windows for a few years because I tried it again and it just worked and stayed out of my way more than Windows 7 was. It's still not at a point where I'd go change all my family members as they all have Windows 7 other than an rogue Vista, that all just work so why change for no reason other than they wouldn't be using Microsoft?

I've been using Ubuntu since 2006, and ever since the laptop on which I had it installed died I don't even have it installed on bare metal anymore, but in a VM. My main machine runs OS X because I don't have to tinker with it, and, while I jailbroke my iPod Touch, I have no desire to do it to my iPhone.

### Post by xyzzyman on 2012-01-23
> **dpny said:**
> I wouldn't say I'm annoyed, but that the older I get the less I want to tinker with things and the more I want them to just work, as there are other things I want to spend my time on. I've been using Ubuntu since 2006, and ever since the laptop on which I had it installed died I don't even have it installed on bare metal anymore, but in a VM. My main machine runs OS X because I don't have to tinker with it, and, while I jailbroke my iPod Touch, I have no desire to do it to my iPhone.

I'd love to have Ubuntu installed on a machine, but there are other things right now more important than a new laptop, like a sick cat.

I almost went back and changed it from annoyed, as I knew that wasn't the best word for my point. I first used Linux back in 1999 in college as my TV tuner from ATI would lock up in Windows with my nVidia TNT2 card and neither company was releasing a driver fix. It worked under Mandrake so that was my first dual-booting. I then moved in Gentoo as back then I had no qualms spending 3 days compiling everything and sitting in terminals. I even would edit my BIOS to enable options I would never need.

I'm only 30 now so still young, but I rooted my android phone just so I could uninstall stuff and have no desire to play with custom ROM's. I am glad I don't have to sit and play with config.sys and autoexec files to squeeze out an extra 30k of base memory to play a game. The biggest gripe I had 3-4 years ago was getting fonts in Firefox on Ubuntu to look as good as Windows. The first thing I noticed when I installed 11.04 and then the 11.10 alpha/beta was that Chrome looked identical when I had the mscorefonts installed. Ubuntu has font hinting and anti-aliasing working out of the box now. Even though I used to be able to go and edit conf files to set them, I prefer to not have to. Fedora, OpenSuse and Arch all failed at this when I was shopping around. (Arch I can't say anything bad about as it's designed all around editing the conf files. Once it's setup it does just seem to work though.)

If only there was a way to graph the progression of a Linux user. There's a zealot phase to some level (I was really into it from 2003-2005 but because I dealt with alot of regular end users for a living I knew it wasn't ready for primetime and I never actually hated MS like some people), but at some point we all get infected by the "average user" and just want to use our computer as a tool to get something else done with minimal fuss. Like dealing with a sick cat, we all (at least I hope) "get a life". 

(I tried Mac OSX Lion for a week around same time I switched back to Ubuntu but personally found it more hindering of my usage... But I use docky on the bottom of my screen and I love global menus suddenly, go figure.)

### Post by C.S.Cameron on 2012-01-23

Just a few:

Governments
Africa

    The Council for Scientific and Industrial Research (CSIR) of South Africa has adopted OpenOffice.org on over 2,500 workstations in its offices in Pretoria and throughout South Africa, specifically to make use of ODF.
    The Ministry of Finances of Morocco began the adoption of OpenOffice.org in its departments in 2006, continuing into 2007. Microsoft Excel macros have emerged as a challenge, slowing the migration process. 

Asia

    Aizuwakamatsu City Gov't Introduces OOo into all its offices. They are planning to switch their office software from Microsoft Office to OpenOffice.org on 850 PCs in their government offices.
    Malaysia migrate's to OpenOffice.org. The Malaysian Administrative Modernisation and Management Planning Unit (MAMPU) is moving to OpenOffice.org on 19th March 08 onwards. MAMPU is not the only government agency to have an agency wide roll out of OpenOffice.org, previously state governments and the Ministry of Human Resource have already rolled out well planned efforts to implement OpenOffice.org.
    Vietnam's Party Central Office will switch 20,000 computers from Microsoft Office to OpenOffice.org, starting early in 2008. (alternate source)
    Vietnam's Department of Education has also announced it will switch 20,000 computers to OpenOffice.org in 2008.
    Election Commission of India (EC) to switch to OpenOffice.org
    Election Commission of India
    The Postal Service of South Korea moved to Linux on 4,748 desktop systems in 2,800 branch offices at the end of 2005, and expects to save about &#8364;680,000 per year.
    Ministry of Defense in Singapore
        "MINDEF has already installed the OpenOffice productivity suite on 5,000 desktop computers... We hope to deploy OpenOffice on 20,000 desktop computers by the end of March 2006." 
    Software Industry Promotion Agency, Thailand
    The government of India is distributing millions of CDs with OOo in local languages. 3,500,000 in Tamil, 3,500,000 in Hindi distributed in 2005. Other languages are coming: "The next stage of the project is to distribute CDs containing applications in Punjabi, a popular Indian language. This launch is due to happen in August and as Punjabi is also spoken in Pakistan, the project hopes to hold launch events in both countries."
    Macau Productivity and Technology Transfer (CPTTM), Macau. Started in 2003 and completed in 2005. Upgrading in summer 2007 from OOo 1.1.5 to OOo 2.2.
    Federal Data Center Project, Pakistan: 1,000 initially, moving to 4,000
    Alexandra Hospital, Singapore
    Government offices in Tamil Nadu, India: "More than 3,000 Suse Linux desktops have been dispatched to government offices in Tamil Nadu, and Windows is not offered unless there is an absolute necessity."
    Elcot (Electronics Corporation of Tamil Nadu) India's largest Linux rollout forges ahead despite tremendous odds 

Europe

    City of Rovereto (Trento -Italy): 420 MS Office desktops and laptops replaced June 2010 by OpenOffice.org
    Danish resolution B103 would require the government to define and use open file formats (see this unofficial translation of the actual parliament decision).
        "Parliament directs the government to ensure that the use of information technology, including software, within public authorities is based upon open standards... No later than January 1st, 2008, the government should introduce and maintain a set of open standards that can serve as inspiration for other public authorities. Hereafter, open standards should be a part of the basis for public authorities' development and purchase of IT software, with the aim to further competition."
        A report describes the implementation of the resolution. Here is a summary in english (PDF). 
    County of Gribskov, Denmark is using OpenOffice.org on all PC workstation in the administration [1] (in Danish)
    City of Rødovre is using OpenOffice.org in conjunction with case- and document handling
    The University of Southern Denmark [www.dsu.dk:](www.dsu.dk:) Administration and 22,000 students will be using OpenOffice.org.
    City Council, Ogre, Latvia
    Metropolitan Court, Budapest, Hungary: 1,500 workstations
    MultiRacio Ltd.[2] deployed more than 60,000 copies of its OOo commercial derivatives called MagyarOffice and EuroOffice in Hungary since 2002 [3]. One-third has been deployed in the public sector and two-thirds in small and medium businesses. For example:
        2,300 copies of MagyarOffice were deployed by the Ministry of Informatics in the so called e-Magyarország points in 2004,
        more than 60% of 10,500 PCs were shipped with MagyarOffice in a public procurement for institutions in 2003, in which MagyarOffice and MS Office were treated on equal footing. 

The article of IT-Business in Hungary is a good account of the present state of the Hungarian Office suite market. See: [4]. An English translation is available here (PDF).

    Region Brussels Capital, Belgium: both government and schools
    Belgian Government Chooses ODF: "From September 2008 on, all document exchanges within the services of the Belgian government will have to be in an open, standard format, according to the proposal. ODF is the only accepted standard in the proposal."
    City of Birmingham, UK
    City of Berlin, Germany
    City of Prague: 60 MS Office desktops replaced by OpenOffice.org
    City of Bristol, UK: 5,000 desktops migrated toStarOffice.
        More in-depth coverage by Jono Bacon at LinuxDevCenter.com

    Gda&#324;sk, Poland
    Sonneburgh foundation, Rotterdam, The Netherlands
    The City of Munich, Germany plans to migrate 14,000 desktops to OpenOffice and Linux by 2008.
    City of Mannheim, Germany
    City of Schwäbisch Hall, Germany
    Paris administrations
    Conseil General of Pyrénées Orientales has deployed 1,500 workstations with OOo since February 2006.
    French Gendarmerie: 70,000 desktop PCs were migrated to OpenOffice.org. It started in 2005 and is now finished. Link in French.
    French National Assembly
    The French tax agency (Direction Générale des Impôts): 80,000 desktop PCs were migrated to OOo in 2006.
        "The agency has calculated that switching its 80,000 desktops to Office XP (from MS Office 97) would cost around &#8364;29.5m, but switching to OpenOffice.org only &#8364;200,000. Although this cost is a one-off saving, Lapeyre points out that due to the need for regular upgrades it will effectively save this amount every five years."
        Groklaw interview

    Ministry of Finance, Republic of Macedonia has installed more than 150 desktops with OpenOffice.org 2.0
    The Finnish Department of Justice migrated 8,500 Windows desktops to OpenOffice.org beginning in December 2006.
        Migration Case Study (PDF): "The migration decision was based on office software evaluation in 2005 and an extensive OpenOffice.org pilot project in 2006. The main results of the office software study are presented in the report including the various migration options considered during the evaluation. In the pilot project, the functionality and compatibility of OpenOffice.org was tested in daily office use. In addition, the evaluation of training and support requirements, workstation installations and configurations were among the tasks of the pilot project." 
    Other case studies of migration to FOSS in Europe
    The Municipality of Groningen, the Netherlands is gradually installing OpenOffice.org on all systems, thus saving 330,000 Euros in license renewal fees.
    The Municipality of Vaals, the Netherlands has replaced 90 systems with Linux and Openoffice.org.
    The Municipality of Heerenveen, the Netherlands is migrating all of its 400 desktops from Microsoft Office to OpenOffice 2.0 [2006/2007]
    The Municipality of Alba-Iulia, Romania uses OpenOffice.org since version 1.04, on a network of about 200 computers.
    Junta de Andalucía, the Regional Government of Andalusia, Spain has around one million users distributed among the 185,000 computers in its schools, all exclusively running Guadalinex and OpenOffice.org. Also the libraries of the region have deployed a special version of Guadalinex and OOo. Finally, the IT tele-centers for the towns of the region with fewer than 10,000 inhabitants total, in 2007, about 235,000 registered users of Guadalinex and OOo. Additionally, since 2003, the Junta de Andalucía, has distributed to citizens more than 500,000 CDs of its Linux distro Guadalinex, containing OpenOffice.org. It maintains a version of Guadalinex updated about every year and personalized for each of the above described environments.
    City of Bologna uses OpenOffice.org on 3.600 computers.
    NRK, the Norvegian national broadcaster uses OpenOffice.org and ODF files on about 850 computers.
    The Municipality of Kamenica, Macedonia 

North America

    Argentinian state choose ODF: "Any electronic document created or generated by the state government of Misiones/Argentina aimed to store and distribute information to state offices and employees must use the OpenDocument format ( ISO/IEC 26300 specification )." The resolution cited explicitly rejects the following formats: MS-Office (doc, xls, ppt), RTF and other propietary binary formats like Wordperfect's wp.
    Banco do Brasil, a successful case on the OpenOffice.org migration The "Banco do Brasil", Brazil´s state-owned bank, has installed 71,000 copies of OpenOffice.org (also known in Brazil as BrOffice.org) covering almost every computer of the Enterprise, which reduced the MS-Office 2003 licences in 70%.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-23
> **C.S.Cameron said:**
> Where did you hear that it is pre-installed on every Windows machine?

Every Windows machine I've ever touched has Office on it. Every big company I've worked for does all their inter- and intra-office stuff in Office.

> Yeah, just like when they abandoned Iphone for Android.

The iPhone is the [#1 selling phone](http://www.bgr.com/2012/01/06/iphone-4s-remains-best-seller-at-top-three-carriers-in-december) in all US carriers. And preliminary data suggests that availability of the iPhone on more than one US carrier may have [halted](http://techcrunch.com/2011/06/22/verizon-iphone-android/) Android's growth.

> Free can be pretty compelling compaired to spending $469.00 (MSRP), for an inferior product.

People don't see the cost of Office: it usually comes preinstalled. Just like people don't see the cost of an OS. They see the cost of a computer, on which the OS is already installed. Giving people a free operating system doesn't mean anything.

Once again, the question needs to be answered: what could Ubuntu do for people that Windows or OS X doesn't do? And the answer can't be it's free, either like speech or beer. The answer has to be something practical. Windows answer is that it's everywhere, so consumers already know how to use it and everything works with it. Apple's answer is OS X is a seamless desktop/mobile OS which focuses on user experience. Linux doesn't have an answer.

And let me ask again: why does Linux need to have a big desktop share?

The argument has been that people are leaving iPhones for android, even though all the sales # show that while overall in the entire 'smartphone' market iphones % has gone down, it's tops in the high end, and the market itself has been growing exponentially and it's the feature phones from nokia and rim that are tanking.

Maybe Android is like say Ford, who makes everything from tiny econo-boxes all the way up to Lincolns. iPhone is the cadillac but they don't have their own chevy or pontiac, etc... 

Either way free isn't that compelling when people do see a value proposition difference.

Now the 'hot' phones are the Galaxy Nexus, the Samsung Galaxy S2, the Note (which is coming soon), and any high end HTC.  I've seen many of my coworkers and friends switch from iPhone to a big screened, high end Android, as I've done myself.  And why not, when you can get a 1.5 ghz, 4.5 inch screened LTE Android for 99 bucks on contract, or an iPhone 4S with weaker specs (and a neutered Siri) for 159-399...

Now I have a high-end Android phone on the same carrier (Telus), with absolutely no text message issues or dropped calls, plus the performance is stellar.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-23
> **Mikeb85 said:**
> BTW, to everyone who complains about fragmentation, allowing choice of hardware is how Windows gained their dominance in the PC market.

Wrong. MS gained their competitive advantage by leveraging OEMs to install Windows and then using anticompetitive tactics to ensure Windows remained the choice of OEMs. It had nothing to do with choice and everything to do with abusing a monopoly position. In effect the OEMs had no choice: install Windows or be cut off.

> And what I've seen all around here (Canada), while a year ago the iPhone 4 was the coveted phone, no one cares about the 4S now (in the sense that it no longer has the 'mystique' it once had). ### Post by xyzzyman on 2012-01-23
> **dpny said:**
> FYI, Best Buy's house brand is made by Samsung.

I worked for Best Buy for 4 someodd years. Depends on the model who makes what parts, and they're only sometimes panels made by Samsung. And when they are they'd be in the Insignia branded, not the Dynex. Unfortunately the rest of the electronics are not made by Samsung or LG (Who was listed as MFG more often on Insignia's). So when an Insignia or Dynex would die under warranty or service plan, they were treated the same as third-tier (Polaroid, HP, Sanyo, Westinghouse, Coby) as even though they had in-home service of 26", parts are not available for them like they are with tier-one (Samsung, Sharp, LG, Toshiba) OT: My Zenith 42" Plasma is the identical model as an LG (It's parent company) except it has a Z on the end and they molded the plastic to cover one of the HDMI jacks.

Moral of the story: If an employee at BBY tells you that the TV is made my Samsung, make them prove it to you as RSS (Retail Stock System? I don't remember) will list for that SKU.

### Post by aysiu on 2012-01-23
I recommend LibreOffice to anyone for whom use who doesn't want to pay for Microsoft Office.

In the workplace, I prefer Microsoft Office. The pivot tables and charts are more robust, as is the email merge wizard. And there are also simple usability issues with LibreOffice. For example, in Excel, I can Control-Spacebar or Shift-Spacebar to highlight a row or column and then use Control-Minus to delete the row or column and the next row or column will be highlighted. In Calc, once I delete the row or column, only one cell is highlighted. Not very useful if I'm going through a spreadsheet looking for certain rows or columns to delete and trying to do it all via keyboard, especially if certain rows or columns I want to delete are adjacent to one another.

Please do not spend a lot of time saying "Oh, but there's this workaround and that workaround." That is just one example. And I don't want workarounds--I want efficiency and productivity. I am a huge proponent of LibreOffice and open source alternatives. But I am also a fan of using the best tool for the job. Microsoft products are not always the best (just take a look at Internet Explorer). That doesn't mean they can't make a good product, though. Microsoft Office and Microsoft OneNote are excellent products.

### Post by aysiu on 2012-01-24
There are lots of things Ubuntu does better than Mac OS X. Installing a ton of software with a single command. Saving files from an unbootable OS. Setting up a dual-boot. Setting up new desktop environments and window managers.

But the idea that Ubuntu can do *anything* better than OS X is ridiculous. I've used both Ubuntu and Mac OS X for years, and I know the pros and cons of each. But there definitely exist things Mac OS X does better. Netflix streaming. Adobe Creative Suite. iTunes. Immediate resume from sleep (including wireless).

In Ubuntu, take a look at /usr/lib/pm-utils/sleep.d/55NetworkManager

This is how the file begins: ```
#!/bin/sh
# If we are running NetworkManager, tell it we are going to sleep.
# TODO: Make NetworkManager smarter about how to handle sleep/resume
#       If we are asleep for less time than it takes for TCP to reset a
#       connection, and we are assigned the same IP on resume, we should
#       not break established connections. Apple can do this, and it is
#       rather nifty.
```

---

### Post by C.S.Cameron on 2012-01-24
Not wanting to get off track but:

> AUSTIN, TX, Jan 20, 2012 (MARKETWIRE via COMTEX) — The popularity of the Google Android smartphone operating system (OS) has increased significantly in the last few years. New iGR research shows that, at present, 47 percent of U.S. smartphone users have an Android device, followed by 24 percent who own/use an Apple iPhone. Of the major brands supporting Android, Samsung has the highest brand preference among consumers, followed by Motorola, HTC and LG. ZTE and Huawei ranked toward the bottom of the brands studied, although note that these brands currently sell comparatively lower volumes in the U.S. market.

iGR’s new research also shows that 45 percent of Android users researched the OS prior to purchase and specifically selected an Android device when they bought a new smartphone. It also appears that Google’s reputation is driving Android sales — 27 percent of Android users said that they selected an Android smartphone because they believed that Google was a “reputable company” and therefore inferred that Android must also be reputable.

### Post by cprofitt on 2012-01-24
> **KiwiNZ said:**
> Oh my goodness. Being Linux derived does NOT make a device a Linux device.:rolleyes::


I am going to agree with you... but give you a caveat.

I would call those derived devices part of the Linux family tree; just like many consider OS 9, OS 10 and iOS all part of the Apple OS tree. Just like many consider Windows 3.1, Windows 95, Windows 98, Windows NT 3.5, Windows NT 4.0, Windows 2000, Windows XP, Windows 2003, Windows 2003r2, Windows 2008, Windows Vista, Windows 2008r2 and Windows 7 all part of the Windows OS tree.

I think the issue is people do not differentiate between the 'Linux tree' and 'Linux' in every day speech... they let context do that... and that often leads to confusion.

A good resource that shows Linux growing, but more slowly than Mac is:
[http://www.w3schools.com/browsers/browsers_os.asp](http://www.w3schools.com/browsers/browsers_os.asp)

A more fine grained report is here:
[http://www.netmarketshare.com/operating-system-market-share.aspx?qprid=10](http://www.netmarketshare.com/operating-system-market-share.aspx?qprid=10)

I do not believe either of them have captured the truth, but I still think they show us something meaningful and do not doubt the data other than how it is gathered.

So why did Mac climb from below Linux to above it?

[LIST]
[*]advertising (mostly untrue advertising, but it got people's attention)
[*] simplifying the 'choice'
[LIST]
[*]when you go outside the Apple computer market you have a large almost overwhelming amount of choice - this causes doubt in people's mind what to buy. Apple really only gives you once choice per 'class'
[*]Apple does not send LOW end crap. Every other manufacturer does... and most people do not make informed choices they buy what is on sale. The resulting failure of the crap to be satisfactory is blamed on all non-Apple products and Windows vs. that one product
[/LIST]
 
[*]Apple built a perception that Apple was the 'good stuff' or the 'desired stuff' or the 'cool stuff' and anything else was boring (false advertising in many cases)
[*]Apple built some amazingly sexy equipment
[/LIST]
Linux on the other hand suffers from worse hardware choice issues -- not only are there so many choices, but trying to figure out if it will run Linux is NOT something a mortal computer user will do. Until mainstream OEMs embrace Linux it can not grow like Apple grew OS X. The good news is Linux is still growing market share... and as Apple takes a bit away from Microsoft people see that there are alternatives. The fear is Apple has a much more walled garden than Microsoft so if the majority of users start using Apple products it could very well be worse than when Microsoft was the 800lb gorilla.

> **C.S.Cameron said:**
> OSX is not free, unless you accept the  theory going around this thread that all software on a new computer is  free and it is just the price of the hardware that fluctuates.

Given the cost difference between a Lenovo T Series and a slightly less well equipped Mac Book Pro I would place the cost of OS X at: $900

MacBook Pro = 2199
Lenovo T520 = 1299

You might argue about all the other stuff Apple throws in, but vs. LInux and FOSS that argument really doesn't hold.

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2012-01-24
> **cprofitt said:**
> I am going to agree with you... but give you a caveat.

I would call those derived devices part of the Linux family tree; just like many consider OS 9, OS 10 and iOS all part of the Apple OS tree. Just like many consider Windows 3.1, Windows 95, Windows 98, Windows NT 3.5, Windows NT 4.0, Windows 2000, Windows XP, Windows 2003, Windows 2003r2, Windows 2008, Windows Vista, Windows 2008r2 and Windows 7 all part of the Windows OS tree.

I think the issue is people do not differentiate between the 'Linux tree' and 'Linux' in every day speech... they let context do that... and that often leads to confusion.

A good resource that shows Linux growing, but more slowly than Mac is:
[http://www.w3schools.com/browsers/browsers_os.asp](http://www.w3schools.com/browsers/browsers_os.asp)

A more fine grained report is here:
[http://www.netmarketshare.com/operating-system-market-share.aspx?qprid=10](http://www.netmarketshare.com/operating-system-market-share.aspx?qprid=10)

I do not believe either of them have captured the truth, but I still think they show us something meaningful and do not doubt the data other than how it is gathered.

So why did Mac climb from below Linux to above it?

[LIST]
[*]advertising (mostly untrue advertising, but it got people's attention)
[*] simplifying the 'choice'
[LIST]
[*]when you go outside the Apple computer market you have a large almost overwhelming amount of choice - this causes doubt in people's mind what to buy. Apple really only gives you once choice per 'class'
[*]Apple does not send LOW end crap. Every other manufacturer does... and most people do not make informed choices they buy what is on sale. The resulting failure of the crap to be satisfactory is blamed on all non-Apple products and Windows vs. that one product
[/LIST]
 
[*]Apple built a perception that Apple was the 'good stuff' or the 'desired stuff' or the 'cool stuff' and anything else was boring (false advertising in many cases)
[*]Apple built some amazingly sexy equipment
[/LIST]
Linux on the other hand suffers from worse hardware choice issues -- not only are there so many choices, but trying to figure out if it will run Linux is NOT something a mortal computer user will do. Until mainstream OEMs embrace Linux it can not grow like Apple grew OS X. The good news is Linux is still growing market share... and as Apple takes a bit away from Microsoft people see that there are alternatives. The fear is Apple has a much more walled garden than Microsoft so if the majority of users start using Apple products it could very well be worse than when Microsoft was the 800lb gorilla.

Their watershed products were the iPod and iPhone, these put their products in the hands of the masses and as a result interest grew for the rest of their products and records tumbled and continue to tumble, reference their latest quarterly result.

Now this could well happen with Samsung and their Android products and flow on to their other product lines.

### Post by 1clue on 2012-01-25
OS X can be installed on VMware or Parallels as long as it is running on Apple hardware.  That's in the EULA.  So there is a way you can buy an OS X license without it being an upgrade.  It might not be advertised on their site, but I'm sure you can call them and ask for it.

Microsoft and Apple do all sorts of things like that, and I'm sure other commercial vendors do too.  If you don't see something you want, you call them up and they'll lead you to a product that is almost certainly available but has too few buyers to make it worth advertising, or just isn't something they're pushing right then.  Every time I've bothered to look there was a full Mac OS license available.  Right now the only one that seems to be marketed is the unlimited copy for $499.

Second, last I heard Apple used a non-free BSD kernel, which means it is NOT Linux in any way.  It's a commercial UNIX.  The software that runs on it which is Open Source is simply Open Source software, which runs on a variety of things which are not Linux.

Third, Android uses a Linux kernel last I heard, which means it IS Linux, whether it's marketed as such or not.

### Post by xyzzyman on 2012-01-25
> **1clue said:**
> Third, Android uses a Linux kernel last I heard, which means it IS Linux, whether it's marketed as such or not.

You don't have to just hear it, you can read it, bottom of the page, right from android.com:

[http://developer.android.com/guide/basics/what-is-android.html](http://developer.android.com/guide/basics/what-is-android.html)

[http://andoid.git.kernel.org](http://andoid.git.kernel.org) now forwards to google's git servers as they took over hosting their own git. I don't know how anyone who's downloaded android source and compiled their own kernel & ROM and customized it can say that it's NOT linux. Some people say it should be a fork now as they haven't been active in submitting patches back upstream, but most of the work done is outside of the 2.6 kernel with the Dalvik VM. Linux is just the kernel, and android runs the 2.6 kernel w/their own hardware patches mostly involving much better sleep than PC hardware supports.

So large corporations stay with MS Office, with what works and has worked. So it all trickles down.

Corporations, governments and institutions are buying iPads at a much higher rate than Android/Linux based tablets. That gets the Apple brand into consumers heads immensely when they use an ipad at work or their kid does at school.

Ubuntu's Software Center is a start but it still needs alot of polish. Microsoft is moving there with the Windows 8 App Store.

At the end of the day the point is mute as has been stated here previously, the desktop is becoming less and less important as tablets and phones take over. Microsoft won on the desktop, Apple is winning on the tablet, and Android winning in entry-level to mid-range smartphones phones and Apple winning the high-end cell phone market for now.

The upside that Android-tablets had against Apple was flash support, but now flash is dead on mobile according to Adobe themselves so now it's going to be entry-mid range tablets for Android, Apple for high-end just like the cell phones. I've recommended Galaxy Tab's over iPad's but now the main point I had is being stripped soon.

People forget how large the casual game market is, and in retail I was surprised when it finally hit me how many people would want to make sure their new system would run games like those, across all ages & demographics.

### Post by rg4w on 2012-01-25
> **xyzzyman said:**
> I did mis-some words previously by thinking faster than I type. But no, advanced users don't use the mouse when it DOESN'T save time, especially when working in Excel. 
Microsoft's research finds that the Paste command is by far the most commonly used of all commands in Office.  While there are three ways to invoke it (main menu, context menu, and toolbar button), surprisingly the most frequently used is clicking the toolbar.

The following relates to Word, and while I haven't been able to find similar info on Excel specifically I'd be surprised if the data for that program is significantly different:

> What we didn't know until we analyzed the data was that even though so many people _do_ use CTRL+V and _do_  use "Paste" on the context menu, the toolbar button for Paste still  gets clicked more than any other button. The command is so incredibly  popular that even though there are more efficient ways of using it, many  people do prefer to click the toolbar button.

The data kept us from making a crucial, stupid mistake. One which we  might not have caught during the beta due to the high expertise level of  our beta users. Once we recognized the importance of the Paste toolbar  button, it was promoted to the first big button on the left side of  Word's first tab.[http://blogs.msdn.com/b/jensenh/archive/2006/04/07/570798.aspx](http://blogs.msdn.com/b/jensenh/archive/2006/04/07/570798.aspx)

Bringing this back on topic, while I've already stated my belief that  the main reason OS X is more popular than Ubuntu is that OS X usually comes with a computer while Ubuntu rarely does, with the research above we find the other half of the story:

Good and thorough usability research is the process that distinguishes the subset of innovations which resonate with users.  Like Steve Jobs said, one of the most powerful elements of design is knowing when to say "no".

While Microsoft currently has the most well-funded usability research in the industry, Apple has historically been a close second, and in its first decade may have been #1 on that scale.

It's not enough to dream up new features.  The variety of options an OS might adopt needs to be thoroughly tested against alternative approaches to arrive at the most optimal implementation possible.

Disciplined research will sometimes yield surprising results, like Microsoft's findings with the Paste button.  If you've followed Apple's research methods over the years (hard to do since Steve came back and they stopped publishing them) you'll usually see solid research methods and sometimes a willingness to change direction when the data suggests a better way to do things.

For all the technical achievements of the Linux community, usability research is not commonly among them.

I believe there are methods for distributed usability research which may yield data of sufficient quantity and quality to change this, and at lower cost than the proprietary OSes can acquire such data.  But taking advantage of such an opportunity will require an awareness of and commitment to it, and it will only be worth doing to the degree that there is a willingness to learn from it, even when it means changing direction.

---

That they found while advanced users were accessing everything the needed, that basic users weren't using the menu's. I'll be honest I've never taken the necessary time to learn the ribbons well enough apparently as it slows me down if I try to use them instead of using keyboard shortcuts, but it took me a good 6 months after Office 2007 came out to realize that the non-technical friends I had were all saying they liked them, so I haven't bad mouthed them since. Word vs Excel I can't say for sure, but anecdotal from my experience in offices I've seen accountants and even even accounts payable clerks do amazing things with Excel but not even be able to take full advantage of Microsoft Works let alone Word. Manipulating data and numbers probably uses the opposite side of the brain. As far as Apple, imagine if they spent just 5% on R&D instead of their miniscule 2%. That would probably cause them to become too unfocused and try to expand into markets that don't support their core ipad/iphone/mac hardware sales though.

I am glad that others recognize the amount of effort that MS puts into their studies and research. One gentleman on the forums here argued Microsoft was wrong when they are designing Windows 8 with 1366x768 as the standard resolution, finally changing it from 1024x768 after all these years... And that's more of a sales analysis then usability study.

Is anyone worrying about growing desktop marketshare at this point playing a game that's already moved on with tablet/smartphone and cloud based computing and the way that moves things back to the thin-client approach?

### Post by rg4w on 2012-01-25
> **xyzzyman said:**
> I am glad that others recognize the amount of effort that MS puts into their studies and research. One gentleman on the forums here argued Microsoft was wrong when they are designing Windows 8 with 1366x768 as the standard resolution, finally changing it from 1024x768 after all these years... And that's more of a sales analysis then usability study.
Arguably a little of both.  There are usability implications with such a decision, but optimizing for higher than 1024x768 seems well supported by available data:

[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Display_resolution](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Display_resolution)
[http://www.w3schools.com/browsers/browsers_display.asp](http://www.w3schools.com/browsers/browsers_display.asp)

> So from your point of view, what are your thoughts on the relevance of linux market share on the desktop relative to Mac OS X or even Windows? Is anyone worrying about growing desktop marketshare at this point playing a game that's already moved on with tablet/smartphone and cloud based computing and the way that moves things back to the thin-client approach?I touched on that [a few posts back]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=11620912&postcount=526").

In short, while the mobile space is obviously important, I believe phone are far more significant than tablets in the long run.  There's a role for tablets, but once the novelty of the form factor wears off it's likely not any more significant that what we currently think of as PCs (both laptops and even desktops).   I believe we'll see some modest growth in PCs in 2012 and more so in 2013 as folks rediscover the role of a powerful and flexible system and use their phones for mobility.

I don't think the desktop alone is the key to growth for any platform, but with ARM, uTouch, Unity, etc. Ubuntu has unusually good opportunities across the board in a business environment that no longer favors any single form factor.

Fingers crossed.

> What I need to do is spend a week or two only using OS X, now that I've fallen in love with global menus and I don't touch unity and just use Docky at the bottom of my screen OS X style.Careful.  It's not as flexible as Linux, but very well designed.  You may find yourself spending a lot of time with it once you get used to it.

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-25
> **KiwiNZ said:**
>  I also do not believe they are a Desktop/Laptop replacement, they are a new appliance in their own right and compliment a Laptop or Desktop.

Tablets are already laptop replacements for many people whose needs do not go beyond email/web/music/videos, which is a large percentage of the population. When the time comes for my mom to replace her laptop, I will steer her towards an iPad, because it's all she needs.

Give the technology five years and they will be viable desktop/laptop replacements for almost everybody. Give them ten years and most users will never touch anything but a tablet.

Remember how quickly things change in the computer world. In 1985 Cray was the undisputed master of high performance computing. By 1995 they were all but out of business, their specialized machines replaced by clusters of commodity processors and fast interlinks. In 1995 Sun was the premiere workstation company. By 2005 they were all but out of business, their market share destroyed by commodity desktops running commodity processors. In 2005 RIM was the undisputed master of what we now call mobile computing. They're barely hanging on.

---

You obviously didn't read the article.  Reasons Apple's stock likely has limited upside - huge market cap, everyone already has shares (so it's likely there will be more sellers than buyers), but most importantly, no dividend, share buybacks or any plans to use their cash to do anything but sue people.   Apple is a prime example of what's wrong with investing these days, they contribute to the problem you describe.  There's many Asian companies with faster growth, smaller market caps, who already pay generous dividends...  

BTW, that's how investing should work - if you own shares, you should get a dividend.  That would make investing much less of a gambler's game.

### Post by Mikeb85 on 2012-01-26

---

### Post by dpny on 2012-01-26
> **Mikeb85 said:**
> Except Apple is hardly doing that...  They've got a cash hoard of nearly $100B.

Are you aware of what Apple does with their cash?

They use it to ensure their production is never constrained by lack of components. They give loans to their component makers--low interest loans of several billion dollars--so their suppliers can do things like build new factories and enlarge assembly lines.

They also buy the odd company.
